The Coexistence of Faith and Sin
Sanusi presents the fifth proof: verses where believers commit sins but retain the label 'believer'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof: Quranic verses that show faith and sin can coexist, proving actions are not part of faith.

Core Idea
Believers can sin without losing faith; so sin does not negate iman.

The fifth and final proof addresses a potential objection: if actions were part of faith, then committing sins would nullify faith. But the Quran describes believers who commit sins—like fighting each other or being unwilling in battle—while still calling them 'believers'. This proves that faith and sin can coexist, so actions cannot be essential to the definition of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the four Quranic examples cited.
The verses include: 'those who believe and do not confuse their faith with wrongdoing', 'those who believe and have not emigrated', 'if two parties among the believers fight', and 'a party of the believers were unwilling'.
2
Analyze each verse
For each verse, note how it describes believers who are committing or experiencing something contrary to perfect obedience: wrongdoing, failure to emigrate, fighting, unwillingness.
These verses affirm that such people are still called believers, so sin does not remove faith.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
If actions were part of the reality of iman, then sin would necessarily remove iman. Since the Quran confirms that sinners remain believers, actions cannot be part of the definition of iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعمال غير داخلة في حقيقة الإيمان لوجوه : الأول - ما مر أنه اسم للتصديق، ولا دليل على النقل. الثاني - النص والإجماع على أنه لا ينفع عند معاينة العذاب، ويسمى اليأس، ولا خفاء في أن ذلك إنما هو التصديق والإقرار، إذ لا مجال للأعمال. الثالث - النصوص الدالة على الأوامر والنواهي بعد إثبات الإيمان كقوله تعالى: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا كتب عليكم الصيام) (۱) الرابع - النصوص الدالة على أن الإيمان والأعمال أمران متفارقان، كقوله تعالى : إن الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات (۲) ومن يؤمن بالله ويعمل صالحاً (۳) ومن يأته مؤمناً قد عمل الصالحات(٤) ومن يعمل من الصالحات وهو مؤمن (٥) وسئل النبي [؟] عن أفضل الأعمال فقال : إيمان لا شك فيه، وجهاد لا غلول فيه، وحج مبرور (1) . الخامس - الآيات الدالة على أن الإيمان والمعاصي قد يجتمعان، كقوله تعالى: (الذين آمنوا ولم يلبسوا إيمانهم بظلم (۷) والذين آمنوا ولم يهاجروا (۸) وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا . . (۹) الآية كما أخرجك ربك من بيتك بالحق وإن فريقاً من المؤمنين لكارهون (۱۰)
Actions are not included in the reality of faith (ḥaqīqat al-īmān) for [several] aspects: First: What has passed that it [faith] is a name for assent (taṣdīq), and there is no evidence for transference (naql) [of the meaning]. Second: The text (naṣṣ) and consensus (ijmāʿ) that it [faith] does not benefit at the sight of punishment, and is called despair; and it is not hidden that that is only assent and acknowledgment (iqrār), for there is no room for actions. Third: The texts indicating commands and prohibitions after the affirmation of faith, like His saying: 'O you who have believed, decreed upon you is fasting.' Fourth: The texts indicating that faith and actions are two separate matters, like His saying: 'Indeed those who believe and do righteous deeds' and 'Whoever believes in Allah and does righteousness' and 'Whoever comes to Him as a believer having done righteous deeds' and 'Whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer.' And the Prophet [?] was asked about the best deeds, so he said: 'Faith without doubt, jihad without cheating, and an accepted pilgrimage.' Fifth: The verses indicating that faith and sins may coincide, like His saying: 'Those who believe and do not confuse their faith with wrongdoing' and 'Those who believe and have not emigrated' and 'If two parties among the believers fight' the verse, and 'As your Lord brought you out of your house with truth while indeed a party of the believers were unwilling.'
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage lists five proofs (wujūh) that actions (aʿmāl) are not part of the reality of faith (ḥaqīqat al-īmān). Each proof is drawn from textual and rational sources. First proof: Faith is linguistically assent (taṣdīq); there is no evidence (naql) that the term's meaning has been transferred to include actions. Second proof: At the moment of death (muʿāyanat al-ʿadhāb), only assent and verbal acknowledgment (iqrār) are beneficial, not actions, as the sources state that faith at that point is called despair (yaʾs) and is not accepted. Third proof: The Qurʾān addresses believers with commands (e.g., fasting) after affirming their faith, showing that faith is prior and distinct from actions. Fourth proof: Many Qurʾānic verses juxtapose 'īmān' and 'ʿamal ṣāliḥ' as two separate categories (e.g., 'those who believe and do righteous deeds'), and a hadith lists faith as the best deed whereas actions like jihad and pilgrimage are separate. Fifth proof: Qurʾānic verses affirm that believers can commit sins (e.g., fighting among themselves, reluctance in battle) without losing their identity as believers, proving that faith and sin coexist.

Key Terms
المعاصي
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a sin automatically removes one from faith (takfir).
Correction: The author shows that the Quran describes believers who commit sins, so sin does not negate iman.

Checkpoint
Which of the following verses is NOT cited in the fifth proof?
A) 'If two parties among the believers fight'
B) 'Those who believe and do not confuse their faith with wrongdoing'
C) 'Those who believe and do righteous deeds'
D) 'A party of the believers were unwilling'

Key Takeaway
The Quranic examples of believers committing sins prove that faith and sin can coexist, so actions are not part of iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What sin in your life have you used as evidence that your faith is weak or gone? After this lesson, will you still call yourself a believer? What will you do to repent and renew your iman today?
?
Think of a fellow Muslim who committed a sin you found shocking. Did you treat them as a disbeliever or as a believer in need of help? How will you change your attitude after learning that the Quran still calls them 'believers'?
?
Is there a sin you are so ashamed of that you avoid Allah? This lesson says you are still a believer. Will you let that truth bring you back to prayer and tawbah, or will you continue to stay away?

Five Arguments that Iman is Only Assent (Tasdiq)
The author enumerates five distinct arguments from scripture and reason for iman as tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five key scriptural and rational arguments used to support the position that faith (iman) is solely inner assent (tasdiq) and does not include actions.

Core Idea
Faith is inner assent; five proofs from Quran, hadith, and reason separate it from deeds.

In classical Islamic theology, the exact definition of iman was debated. One prominent view—held by the Maturidis and some others—is that iman is only tasdiq (assent of the heart), not including actions. The author here presents five arguments to support this position, drawn from textual and rational sources. These arguments help clarify the distinction between inner faith and external deeds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument: linguistic and lack of transfer
The first argument recalls that iman linguistically means tasdiq, and there is no evidence that the term was transferred to a broader meaning in revelation.
This is a semantic argument.
2
Analyze the second argument: iman at death
The second argument points out that iman is not beneficial when punishment is seen at death; only assent remains, proving iman is inner.
Actions are impossible at that moment.
3
Examine the third argument: commands after faith
The third argument uses verses where Allah addresses believers with commands, showing faith precedes and is separate from the commanded actions.
4
Review the fourth argument: separate mentions of iman and deeds
The fourth argument lists verses that mention iman and righteous deeds as distinct, and the hadith about the best of deeds lists iman first.
This scriptural pattern indicates they are not identical.
5
Consider the fifth argument: coexistence of iman and sin
The fifth argument cites verses where believers commit sins, proving that iman can remain despite disobedience, so it cannot be equivalent to all good actions.
This refutes the idea that iman includes obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - ما مر أنه اسم للتصديق، ولا دليل على النقل.
الثاني - النص والإجماع على أنه لا ينفع عند معاينة العذاب، ويسمى اليأس، ولا خفاء في أن ذلك إنما هو التصديق والإقرار، إذ لا مجال للأعمال.
الثالث - النصوص الدالة على الأوامر والنواهي بعد إثبات الإيمان كقوله تعالى: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا كتب عليكم الصيام)
الرابع - النصوص الدالة على أن الإيمان والأعمال أمران متفارقان، كقوله تعالى : إن الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات (٢) ومن يؤمن بالله ويعمل صالحاً (٣) ومن يأته مؤمناً قد عمل الصالحات(٤) ومن يعمل من الصالحات وهو مؤمن (٥)
وسئل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن أفضل الأعمال فقال : إيمان لا شك فيه، وجهاد لا غلول فيه، وحج مبرور (1) .
والخامس - الآيات الدالة على أن الإيمان والمعاصي قد يجتمعان، كقوله تعالى: (الذين آمنوا ولم يلبسوا إيمانهم بظلم (۷) والذين آمنوا ولم يهاجروا (۸) وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا . . (۹) الآية كما أخرجك ربك من بيتك بالحق وإن فريقاً من المؤمنين لكارهون (١٠)
First: It has already been mentioned that it (iman) is a name for tasdiq (assent), and there is no evidence for transfer (of meaning). Second: The text and consensus (show) that it is not beneficial at the sight of punishment, and it is called despair, and there is no hiddenness in that that is only tasdiq and iqrar (affirmation), since there is no room for actions. Third: The texts indicating commands and prohibitions after the establishment of iman, like His saying: (O you who have believed, upon you is prescribed fasting). Fourth: The texts indicating that iman and actions are two separate matters, like His saying: (Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds) (2) and (Whoever believes in Allah and does righteousness) (3) and (Whoever comes to Him as a believer having done righteous deeds) (4) and (Whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer) (5). And the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about the best of deeds, so he said: 'Iman without doubt, jihad without treachery, and an accepted hajj' (1). Fifth: The verses indicating that iman and sins may combine, like His saying: (Those who have believed and do not mix their iman with wrong) (7) and (Those who have believed and have not emigrated) (8) and (If two parties among the believers fight) (9) and the verse (As your Lord brought you out of your house with the truth while indeed a party of the believers were unwilling) (10).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage lists five arguments in support of the view that iman (faith) is only tasdiq (assent of the heart) and does not include actions. The author does not number them as such but each begins with a number. The first argument recalls that iman is linguistically tasdiq and there is no proof it was transferred to a different meaning. The second argument uses the fact that iman at the moment of death (when one sees the punishment) is of no benefit, and this is called despair; it is clearly only tasdiq and iqrar, because there is no time for actions. The third argument cites Quranic verses that address believers with commands after calling them believers, showing that iman is separate from actions. The fourth argument lists several verses that mention iman and righteous deeds separately, indicating they are distinct. A hadith is cited where the Prophet was asked about the best of deeds and he answered with 'iman without doubt' first, then jihad and hajj, showing iman is the foundation. The fifth argument presents verses where believers commit sins (like fighting or reluctance), proving that iman and sin can coexist, so iman cannot be identical to all good actions.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق
أعمال
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman includes all acts of obedience, so without actions there is no faith.
Correction: These arguments demonstrate that iman is inner assent separate from external deeds; deeds are commanded after faith and can be absent while faith remains.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why does the fact that iman is not beneficial at the sight of punishment prove it is only tasdiq?
A) Because at that moment there is no time for actions.
B) Because the person is already a disbeliever.
C) Because actions are not required in the afterlife.
D) Because the punishment removes all iman.

Key Takeaway
The five arguments collectively show that iman is confined to inner assent, distinct from and prior to actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that iman is inner assent, not your deeds. Does your heart truly affirm Allah and His Messenger, or do you rely more on your actions to feel secure? Will you examine your inner state today to see if your faith is genuine assent or mere ritual?
?
The passage shows that even sins do not remove iman. Is there a sin you are using as an excuse to doubt your own faith? Will you repent without letting that sin shake your core belief, knowing that iman remains as long as you affirm it in your heart?
?
If iman were defined by your actions, you would be in constant anxiety. Are you living as if your salvation depends on your deeds, or have you placed your trust in the inner assent that Allah has given you? How will you rest in that assurance today?

The Seventh Argument: Iman Cannot Be All Acts or Each Act Individually
Sanusi presents the seventh argument: a dilemma against iman as acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that exposes the absurd consequences of defining iman as acts of obedience, whether collectively or individually.

Core Idea
If iman were acts, missing one negates it; each act being a separate iman makes moving between acts a change of religion.

The seventh argument sharpens the critique by presenting a dilemma: if iman includes acts, it must either be a name for all acts collectively or for each individually. Both lead to impossible conclusions that contradict clear consensus. This lesson unpacks those consequences and shows why the claim fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the seventh argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'السابع' to 'منتقل من دين إلى دين'. Then read its literal translation. Notice the two conditional scenarios introduced by 'فإما للجميع... وإما لكل عمل على حدة'.
The passage uses a classic reductio structure: assume X, then derive absurdity.
2
Understand the first horn: collective
If iman is for all acts collectively, then negating even one act negates iman entirely. This would mean a person who merely affirms and confesses is not a believer until performing all worship – contradicting consensus.
Consensus holds that a believer who dies before performing all acts is still a believer.
3
Understand the second horn: individual
If iman is for each act individually, then each act is a separate iman. Moving from one act to another would be leaving one religion for another – an absurdity.
This shows the view leads to religious fragmentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أنه لو كان اسماً للطاعات، فإما للجميع فيلزم انتفاؤه بانتفاء بعض الأعمال، فلم يكن من صدق وأقر مؤمناً قبل الإتيان بالعبادات . والإجماع على خلافه . وعلى أن من صدق وأقر فأدركه الموت مات مؤمناً . قال في التبصرة : قد أجمع المسلمون على تحقق إسم الإيمان وإثبات حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد وإما لكل عمل على حدة فتكون كل طاعة إيماناً على حدة . والمنتقل من طاعة إلى طاعة منتقلاً من دين إلى دين . الثامن - أن جبرائيل (عليه السلام) لما سأل النبي (عليه السلام) عن الإيمان، لم يجب إلا بالتصديق دون الأعمال، وقالت المعتزلة : نحن لا ننكر استعمال الإيمان في الشرع في معناه اللغوي، أعني التصديق لكنا ندعي نقله عن ذلك إلى معنى شرعي هو فعل الطاعات وترك المعاصي لأن المفهوم من إطلاق المؤمن في الشرع ليس هو المصدق فقط . ولأن الأحكام المجراة على المؤمنين دون الكفرة ليست منوطة بمجرد المعنى اللغوي ورد بأنا لا ندعي كونه اسماً لكل تصديق بل للتصديق بأمور مخصوصة كما في الحديث المشهور. فإن أريد بالنقل عن المعنى اللغوي مجرد هذا فلا نزاع ، ولا دلالة على ما يزعمون من كونه اسماً للطاعات .
Seventh: If it (iman) were a name for acts of obedience, then either (it would be a name) for all of them, so its negation would follow from the negation of some actions; then whoever affirmed and confessed would not be a believer before performing the acts of worship, and the consensus is contrary to that, and (consensus) that whoever affirms and confesses and then death overtakes him, he dies a believer. He said in al-Tabsira: The Muslims have agreed on the reality of the name of iman and the establishment of its ruling by mere belief. Or (it would be a name) for each action individually, so every act of obedience would be a separate iman, and one who moves from one act of obedience to another would be moving from one religion to another. Eighth: That Jibril (peace be upon him) when he asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about iman, he did not answer except with tasdiq (assent) without actions. And the Mu'tazila said: We do not deny the use of iman in the Law in its linguistic meaning, i.e., tasdiq, but we claim it has been transferred from that to a legal meaning which is the doing of acts of obedience and the abandoning of sins, because the understanding from the application of 'believer' in the Law is not merely the one who affirms. And because the rulings applied to believers as opposed to disbelievers are not dependent on the mere linguistic meaning. The response: We do not claim that it is a name for every tasdiq, but rather for tasdiq of specific matters, as in the well-known hadith. So if what is meant by transfer from the linguistic meaning is just this, then there is no dispute, and there is no evidence for what they claim that it is a name for acts of obedience.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents two more arguments (the seventh and eighth) against the view that iman includes actions. The seventh argument is a reductio: if iman were a name for acts of obedience, it must either be for all of them collectively or for each individually. If collectively, then missing one act would negate iman, so a person who merely affirms and confesses would not be a believer before performing worship—contrary to consensus. If individually, then each act would be a separate iman, so moving from one act to another would be changing religions—absurd. The eighth argument cites the famous hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet defined iman as tasdiq only, without mentioning actions. The author then addresses the Mu'tazila's claim that iman has been transferred from its linguistic meaning to a legal meaning including actions. The response is that the linguistic meaning is not simply any tasdiq but tasdiq of specific things (the articles of faith), and this is what is meant; if that is the claimed transfer, there is no dispute, but it does not support their position that iman is a name for acts of obedience.

Key Terms
طاعات
تصديق
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that iman requires all acts of obedience to be present simultaneously.
Correction: The argument shows this would mean no one is a believer until all acts are done, contradicting consensus that a believer who dies before completing all acts is still a believer.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence if iman were a name for all acts of obedience collectively?
A) A person would be a believer only after performing all acts.
B) A person who does some acts would be a believer.
C) Iman would be impossible to achieve.
D) Acts of obedience would become optional.

Key Takeaway
Iman cannot be a name for acts of obedience, because both collective and individual interpretations lead to contradictions with consensus and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman is not defined by the acts you do, then what distinguishes your faith from that of a hypocrite who also performs acts? Examine your heart: is your tasdiq (inner belief) alive and active, or is it merely a label you carry? How will you ensure that your belief is genuine before you perform any act?
?
The argument shows that moving from one act to another cannot be changing religion. Yet do you sometimes treat different acts as if they belong to different 'faiths'? For instance, do you pray but neglect charity, as if they are separate? This lesson challenges you to see all acts as expressions of one iman. Which act have you been neglecting, and what does that reveal about the state of your iman?
?
Consensus affirms that a person who dies after mere belief is a believer. Are you comforted by this, or does it make you complacent? Does your belief move you to act, or is your iman stagnant? Answer honestly: If you were to die right now, would your iman be sufficient? If so, why are you not more eager to act? If not, what are you waiting for?

The Eighth Argument and the Rebuttal of Transfer
Sanusi uses the hadith of Jibril and rebuts the Mu'tazila claim of transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of Jibril proves iman is tasdiq, and why the Mu'tazila's claim of transfer does not support their position.

Core Idea
The Prophet defined iman as tasdiq; the Mu'tazila's 'transfer' is just a specification of objects, not a change of meaning.

The eighth argument draws on the famous hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet explicitly defines iman as assent (tasdiq) without mentioning actions. The author then addresses the Mu'tazila's counter-claim that the term has been transferred from its linguistic meaning to a new legal meaning encompassing acts. The response clarifies that the shift is only in the objects of tasdiq, not its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the eighth argument
Read the Arabic from 'الثامن - أن جبرائيل' to the end. Then read the translation. Note the two parts: the hadith citation and the Mu'tazila objection with the response.
The hadith is a cornerstone for the view that iman is inner belief.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet (PBUH) answered Jibril's question about iman by mentioning only tasdiq – the six articles of faith. Actions were mentioned under Islam, not iman.
This shows the Prophet distinguished iman from actions.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila concede the linguistic meaning but claim a legal transfer to include acts. They argue that the legal usage of 'believer' implies more than just tasdiq.
They try to reconcile their view with the hadith by invoking transfer.
4
Grasp the author's response
The author responds that the 'transfer' is only in the objects – tasdiq is now for specific creedal matters, not any claim. This is not a genuine transfer; it just specifies the content. Thus no evidence for including acts.
The response preserves the linguistic continuity while acknowledging legal specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أنه لو كان اسماً للطاعات، فإما للجميع فيلزم انتفاؤه بانتفاء بعض الأعمال، فلم يكن من صدق وأقر مؤمناً قبل الإتيان بالعبادات . والإجماع على خلافه . وعلى أن من صدق وأقر فأدركه الموت مات مؤمناً . قال في التبصرة : قد أجمع المسلمون على تحقق إسم الإيمان وإثبات حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد وإما لكل عمل على حدة فتكون كل طاعة إيماناً على حدة . والمنتقل من طاعة إلى طاعة منتقلاً من دين إلى دين . الثامن - أن جبرائيل (عليه السلام) لما سأل النبي (عليه السلام) عن الإيمان، لم يجب إلا بالتصديق دون الأعمال، وقالت المعتزلة : نحن لا ننكر استعمال الإيمان في الشرع في معناه اللغوي، أعني التصديق لكنا ندعي نقله عن ذلك إلى معنى شرعي هو فعل الطاعات وترك المعاصي لأن المفهوم من إطلاق المؤمن في الشرع ليس هو المصدق فقط . ولأن الأحكام المجراة على المؤمنين دون الكفرة ليست منوطة بمجرد المعنى اللغوي ورد بأنا لا ندعي كونه اسماً لكل تصديق بل للتصديق بأمور مخصوصة كما في الحديث المشهور. فإن أريد بالنقل عن المعنى اللغوي مجرد هذا فلا نزاع ، ولا دلالة على ما يزعمون من كونه اسماً للطاعات .
Seventh: If it (iman) were a name for acts of obedience, then either (it would be a name) for all of them, so its negation would follow from the negation of some actions; then whoever affirmed and confessed would not be a believer before performing the acts of worship, and the consensus is contrary to that, and (consensus) that whoever affirms and confesses and then death overtakes him, he dies a believer. He said in al-Tabsira: The Muslims have agreed on the reality of the name of iman and the establishment of its ruling by mere belief. Or (it would be a name) for each action individually, so every act of obedience would be a separate iman, and one who moves from one act of obedience to another would be moving from one religion to another. Eighth: That Jibril (peace be upon him) when he asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about iman, he did not answer except with tasdiq (assent) without actions. And the Mu'tazila said: We do not deny the use of iman in the Law in its linguistic meaning, i.e., tasdiq, but we claim it has been transferred from that to a legal meaning which is the doing of acts of obedience and the abandoning of sins, because the understanding from the application of 'believer' in the Law is not merely the one who affirms. And because the rulings applied to believers as opposed to disbelievers are not dependent on the mere linguistic meaning. The response: We do not claim that it is a name for every tasdiq, but rather for tasdiq of specific matters, as in the well-known hadith. So if what is meant by transfer from the linguistic meaning is just this, then there is no dispute, and there is no evidence for what they claim that it is a name for acts of obedience.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents two more arguments (the seventh and eighth) against the view that iman includes actions. The seventh argument is a reductio: if iman were a name for acts of obedience, it must either be for all of them collectively or for each individually. If collectively, then missing one act would negate iman, so a person who merely affirms and confesses would not be a believer before performing worship—contrary to consensus. If individually, then each act would be a separate iman, so moving from one act to another would be changing religions—absurd. The eighth argument cites the famous hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet defined iman as tasdiq only, without mentioning actions. The author then addresses the Mu'tazila's claim that iman has been transferred from its linguistic meaning to a legal meaning including actions. The response is that the linguistic meaning is not simply any tasdiq but tasdiq of specific things (the articles of faith), and this is what is meant; if that is the claimed transfer, there is no dispute, but it does not support their position that iman is a name for acts of obedience.

Key Terms
تصديق
نقل
جبرائيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila completely reject the linguistic meaning of iman as tasdiq.
Correction: The passage shows they accept the linguistic meaning but claim a legal transfer to include actions. The response shows the transfer is only a specification of objects, not a change of core meaning.

Checkpoint
What was the Prophet's answer when Jibril asked about iman?
A) He defined iman as acts of obedience.
B) He defined iman as tasdiq without mentioning actions.
C) He said iman includes both belief and actions.
D) He did not answer.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Jibril provides direct evidence that iman is tasdiq, and the Mu'tazila's argument from transfer fails because it merely specifies the objects of assent, not a new meaning including acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet defined iman solely as tasdiq, why do you often judge your own faith by your actions rather than your inner certainty? Where does that habit come from, and how can you begin to shift your focus?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that including actions makes iman more practical, but the Prophet did not do so. Are you willing to trust the Prophet's definition over your own sense of what 'should' be included? What would it cost you to let go of relying on your deeds?
?
When you hear that iman is simply tasdiq, does it bring you comfort or unease? If it brings unease, is it because deep down you doubt your own inner assent? How can you actively strengthen that tasdiq today, not through actions but through reflection and certainty?

A Pronoun that Shakes a Doctrine: Refuting the Mu'tazila's First Argument
Mu'tazila proof and author's grammatical refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that the Quranic verse 'and that is the correct religion' proves that actions are part of iman, by demonstrating grammatical ambiguity.

Core Idea
A single pronoun can dismantle an entire theological argument.

The Mu'tazila built a strong case for actions being iman by quoting a key verse. The author's response is a masterclass in precise textual analysis: he shows that the verse does not necessarily refer to the specific acts mentioned. Let's see how grammar becomes a tool of theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila proof
Read the passage to identify their argument chain: obligatory acts are the accepted religion, which is Islam, which is iman, therefore acts are iman.
Note the key verses they cite: 98:5 and 3:19.
2
Identify the author's counter
Focus on the grammatical point about the word 'dhaalika' (that). The author argues that it is a masculine singular pronoun and could refer to sincerity or submission, not necessarily the whole list of acts.
This grammatical ambiguity weakens their inference.
3
Recognize the broader strategy
Notice that the author offers alternative referents (ikhlas, tadayyun, inqiyad) that shift the meaning away from specific obligatory acts toward an inner state.
This reflects a general principle: ambiguous proof texts cannot support a definitive doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتجوا بوجوه :
الأول - أن فعل الواجبات هو الدين المعتبر لقوله تعالى : ( وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين حنفاء ويقيموا الصلاة ويؤتوا الزكاة وذلك دين القيمة ) أي ذلك المذكور من إقامة الصلاة وغيرها هو الدين المعتبر . والدين المعتبر هو الإسلام لقوله تعالى : إن الدين عند الله الإسلام (۲) والإسلام هو الإيمان لما سيجيء. وأجيب أولاً بأن ذلك مفرد مذكر، وجعله إشارة إلى جملة ما سبق تأويل، ليس أولى وأقرب من جعله إشارة إلى الإخلاص أو التدين والانقياد ، ولما سبق من الأوامر،
So they (the Mu'tazila) argued by several proofs: First: That the doing of obligatory acts is the accepted religion, due to His saying: (And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, inclining to truth, and to establish prayer and to give zakah. And that is the correct religion) i.e., that which is mentioned of establishing prayer and others is the accepted religion. And the accepted religion is Islam, due to His saying: (Indeed, the religion in the sight of Allah is Islam) (2). And Islam is iman, as will come. And it was answered first: That (the word 'that') is a masculine singular, and making it refer to the totality of what preceded is an interpretation that is not more likely or closer than making it refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or to religiosity and submission (al-tadayyun wa al-inqiyad), and to the preceding commands.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazila's first argument and the author's response. The Mu'tazila argue that iman must include actions because the Quran says 'the correct religion' is worship, prayer, and zakah, and they identify this religion with Islam, and Islam with iman. Therefore, iman includes those acts. The author responds by pointing out that the grammatical referent of 'that' (dhaalika) in the verse is ambiguous; it could refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or to the general attitude of religious submission (al-tadayyun wa al-inqiyad) rather than the listed acts. Also, the word 'religion' (al-din) here may be interpreted as the general submission, not as a list of specific acts. Thus, the Mu'tazila's inference is not necessarily valid.

Key Terms
معتزلة
دين
إسلام
إخلاص
انقياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'dhaalika' in the verse must refer to all the specific acts mentioned before it.
Correction: Grammatically, 'dhaalika' can refer to the nearest singular concept, such as sincerity or submission, because the preceding list is not a single singular noun.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Mu'tazila's claim that the verse 'And that is the correct religion' proves acts are part of iman?
A) By arguing the verse is abrogated.
B) By showing that the word 'that' could refer to sincerity or submission, not the specific acts.
C) By denying that Islam is iman.
D) By saying the verse refers only to the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that the pronoun 'dhaalika' is ambiguous and does not necessarily refer to the listed acts, undermining their proof that actions are part of iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about a Quranic verse that later turned out to be based on a forced reading? How does this lesson challenge you to approach the Quran with greater grammatical care?
?
The Mu'tazila built an entire doctrine on one pronoun. Consider your own beliefs: are there any that depend on a single piece of evidence you might be interpreting too rigidly? Would you be willing to re-examine that evidence if a sound linguistic argument challenged it?
?
This passage shows that a small linguistic detail can overturn a major argument. When you read the Quran, do you actively consider the grammar and context, or do you rely on preconceived ideas? What practical step can you take today to deepen your engagement with the Arabic of the Quran?

Seventh Argument: Iman Cannot Be All Acts of Obedience
Seventh argument refuting iman as all acts of obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh theological argument proving that iman cannot be defined as all acts of obedience, and how it relies on consensus and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
If iman were all acts, missing one would lose faith — but consensus says belief alone suffices.

This lesson presents a powerful logical refutation of the view that iman encompasses every act of obedience. The argument proceeds by considering two exhaustive possibilities: either iman names the entire set of acts collectively, or it names each act individually. Both lead to conclusions that contradict established consensus and common sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice the two branches introduced by 'إما' and 'وإما'.
2
Understand the first branch (collective)
The argument assumes iman names all acts collectively. Then missing any act would negate iman. This contradicts the consensus that a person who believes and affirms is a believer even before performing acts.
The author quotes al-Tabsira to reinforce the consensus.
3
Understand the second branch (individual)
If iman names each act separately, then every act of obedience is its own iman. Moving from one act to another would mean changing religions, which is absurd.
This shows the definition leads to unacceptable consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أنه لو كان اسماً للطاعات، فإما للجميع فيلزم انتفاؤه بانتفاء بعض الأعمال، فلم يكن من صدق وأقر مؤمناً قبل الإتيان بالعبادات . والإجماع على خلافه . وعلى أن من صدق وأقر فأدركه الموت مات مؤمناً . قال في التبصرة : قد أجمع المسلمون على تحقق إسم الإيمان وإثبات حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد وإما لكل عمل على حدة فتكون كل طاعة إيماناً على حدة . والمنتقل من طاعة إلى طاعة منتقلاً من دين إلى دين .
Seventh – If it (iman) were a name for acts of obedience, then either for all of them, so its negation would follow from the negation of some acts; so one who believed and affirmed would not be a believer before performing acts of worship. And consensus is contrary to that. And that one who believed and affirmed and then death overtook him died as a believer. He said in al-Tabsira: Muslims have agreed upon the realization of the name of iman and the establishment of its ruling by mere belief; and if (it is) for each act separately, then every act of obedience would be iman separately, and one who moves from one act of obedience to another would be moving from one religion to another.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that iman is a name for acts of obedience. The author argues that if iman were a name for all acts of obedience, then either it is for all collectively or individually. If collectively, then if someone misses any act, the name of iman would be negated, meaning a person who believes and affirms but has not yet performed worship would not be a believer, which contradicts the consensus that such a person dies a believer. The author quotes from al-Tabsira: Muslims have agreed that the name and ruling of iman are established by mere belief (i'tiqad) alone. If it were for each act individually, then every act of obedience would be its own iman, and moving from one obedient act to another would be like moving from one religion to another, which is absurd.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الطاعات
التصديق
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that iman can be negated by missing some acts of obedience.
Correction: Consensus shows that iman is established by mere belief (i'tiqad), not by performing all acts.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, if iman were a name for all acts of obedience collectively, what would happen to someone who believes but has not yet performed acts of worship?
A) They would still be a believer
B) They would not be considered a believer until they perform all acts
C) They would be considered a hypocrite
D) They would be forgiven due to their belief

Key Takeaway
Iman is established by mere belief (i'tiqad), not by performing all acts of obedience, as proven by the absurd consequences of the opposing view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in an act of worship, do you feel your faith is diminished? How does this teaching challenge that feeling, and what would it look like to trust that your iman remains intact because of your belief alone?
?
Consider a time you neglected a religious obligation. Did you doubt your faith as a result? How can you hold onto the certainty that you are a believer despite your shortcomings, without using it as an excuse to neglect further?
?
Consensus affirms that a person who believes and affirms but dies before performing all acts dies as a believer. Does this truth comfort you or unsettle you? Are you using it as a foundation for gratitude and action, or as a safety net for complacency? Examine your heart and be honest.

The Hadith of Jibril and the Meaning of Iman
Argument from hadith of Jibril and reply to Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of Jibril is used to argue that iman is only affirmation (tasdiq), and how the Mu'tazilite claim of semantic transfer is answered by specifying the object of affirmation.

Core Idea
The Prophet's answer to Jibril defines iman as affirmation, not actions.

The eighth argument draws on the famous hadith where Jibril asks the Prophet about iman. The Prophet responds by listing the articles of faith, not actions. The Mu'tazilites argued that the word iman had been transferred in Islamic law to mean actions too. This lesson examines the dispute and the author's rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the structure: argument, objection, reply.
2
Identify the core argument from the hadith
The author cites the hadith where Jibril asks about iman and the Prophet answers only with tasdiq (belief in specific matters), not actions.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazila do not deny the linguistic meaning, but claim it was transferred to a legal meaning including actions. They support this by noting that 'believer' in law implies more than mere affirmation.
They argue that rulings are not based on linguistic meaning alone.
4
Follow the author's reply
The author replies that iman is not a name for any affirmation, but only for affirmation of specific matters (as in the hadith). If transfer means specifying the object, there is no dispute; it does not prove iman includes actions.
The key is that the meaning (tasdiq) remains, but the object is specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن - أن جبرائيل (عليه السلام لما سأل النبي (عليه السلام) عن الإيمان، لم يجب إلا بالتصديق دون الأعمال، وقالت المعتزلة : نحن لا ننكر استعمال الإيمان في الشرع في معناه اللغوي، أعني التصديق لكنا ندعي نقله عن ذلك إلى معنى شرعي هو فعل الطاعات وترك المعاصي لأن المفهوم من إطلاق المؤمن في الشرع ليس هو المصدق فقط . ولأن الأحكام المجراة على المؤمنين دون الكفرة ليست منوطة بمجرد المعنى اللغوي ورد بأنا لا ندعي كونه اسماً لكل تصديق بل للتصديق بأمور مخصوصة كما في الحديث المشهور. فإن أريد بالنقل عن المعنى اللغوي مجرد هذا فلا نزاع ، ولا دلالة على ما يزعمون من كونه اسماً للطاعات .
Eighth – That when Jibril (peace be upon him) asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about iman, he did not answer except with affirmation (tasdiq) without actions. And the Mu'tazila said: We do not deny the use of iman in the Law in its linguistic meaning, i.e., affirmation, but we claim it has been transferred from that to a legal meaning, which is performing acts of obedience and abandoning sins, because the understanding of applying 'believer' in the Law is not merely the one who affirms. And because the rulings applied to believers to the exclusion of disbelievers are not dependent on mere linguistic meaning. It was replied: We do not claim that it is a name for every affirmation, but for affirmation of specific matters as in the famous hadith. If by transfer from the linguistic meaning they mean merely this, then there is no dispute, and there is no indication of what they claim that it is a name for acts of obedience.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument. The author cites the hadith where Jibril asked the Prophet about iman and the Prophet answered by mentioning belief in Allah, angels, books, etc., i.e., tasdiq, without mentioning actions. The Mu'tazilites respond that they do not deny the linguistic meaning of iman as tasdiq, but they claim it has been transferred in Islamic law to a new meaning that includes performing acts of obedience and avoiding sins. They support this by noting that the term 'believer' in the law does not refer only to one who affirms, and that legal rulings are not based solely on linguistic meaning. The author refutes this by saying that we do not claim iman is a name for every kind of tasdiq, but only for tasdiq of specific matters (the core beliefs), as in the famous hadith. If by 'transfer' the Mu'tazila mean simply that the object of tasdiq is specified, then there is no dispute, and this does not prove that iman includes actions.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
المعتزلة
النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite claim that iman's legal meaning is entirely different from its linguistic meaning (tasdiq) and includes actions.
Correction: The author clarifies that iman remains tasdiq, but it is tasdiq of specific matters (the articles of faith). The so-called transfer is merely a specification of the object of affirmation, not a change in the core meaning.

Checkpoint

A) They accepted the transfer
B) They said iman is only for specific affirmations, not all affirmations
C) They said iman includes actions
D) They said the hadith is not authentic

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Jibril shows that iman is tasdiq of specific beliefs, not actions, and the Mu'tazilite claim of semantic transfer does not prove iman includes actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assess your own iman, do you instinctively look at your actions (prayer, charity) rather than the state of your heart? What would it take for you to prioritize inner conviction today?

?

First Mu'tazilite Proof: Verse on Worship and Religion
The first Mu'tazilite proof from the Quran with three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite proof that obligatory acts constitute the religion and the three linguistic responses that refute it.

Core Idea
The demonstrative 'that' can refer to sincerity, not actions, collapsing the argument.

The Mu'tazilites attempt to prove that obligatory acts are the very substance of religion by citing Surah al-Bayyinah. However, a careful linguistic analysis shows that the verse's wording does not force the conclusion they want. This lesson presents their proof and three devastating responses, the most important being the ambiguity of the demonstrative pronoun.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite proof
Read the opening lines carefully: They argue that performing obligatory acts is the religion because of the verse 'that is the correct religion' after mentioning acts.
Notice how they connect the verse to Islam and then to iman.
2
Identify the three responses
The passage presents three distinct responses. List them mentally: first linguistic, second on the meaning of Islam, third deferred.
The third response is only mentioned as forthcoming.
3
Analyze the first response
Focus on why the demonstrative 'dhālika' does not necessarily refer to the list of acts. Consider its gender and number, and the alternative referents like ikhlas (sincerity) or inqiyad (submission).
Also note the example from Surah al-Tawbah to strengthen the argument.
4
Understand the second response
The second response argues that 'religion' in 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' refers to the path (millah), not the attribute of the individual.
This refutes the equation of Islam with iman via this verse.
5
Preview the third response
Recognize that the third response is deferred to a later discussion on the unity of iman and Islam. This shows the author's systematic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - أن فعل الواجبات هو الدين المعتبر لقوله تعالى : ( وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين حنفاء ويقيموا الصلاة ويؤتوا الزكاة وذلك دين القيمة ) (١) أي ذلك المذكور من إقامة الصلاة وغيرها هو الدين المعتبر . والدين المعتبر هو الإسلام لقوله تعالى : إن الدين عند الله الإسلام (۲) والإسلام هو الإيمان لما سيجيء. وأجيب أولاً بأن ذلك مفرد مذكر، وجعله إشارة إلى جملة ما سبق تأويل، ليس أولى وأقرب من جعله إشارة إلى الإخلاص أو التدين والانقياد ، ولما سبق من الأوامر، بل ربما يكون هذا أولى لبقاء اللفظ على معناه اللغوي أو قريباً منه . ألا ترى أن قوله تعالى: إن عدة الشهور عند الله اثنا عشر شهراً .. إلى قوله ذلك الدين القيم (1) هو معناه أن التدين بكون الشهور اثنا عشر أربعة منها حرم، والانقياد لذلك الدين المستقيم ، على أن ههنا شيئاً آخر، وهو أن الدين في تلك الآية مضاف إلى القيمة ، لا موصوف كما في هذه الآية . والمعنى دين الملة القيمة، فلا يكون معناه الملة والطريقة ، بل الطاعة كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ مخلصين له الدين (۲) وحينئذ سقط الاستدلال بالكلية . وثانياً ـ بأن معنى الآية الثانية أن الدين المعتبر هو دين الإسلام للقطع بأن الدين الملة والطريقة التي تعتبر غالباً إضافتها إلى الرسول لا تكون نفس الإسلام الذي هو صفة المكلف. وثالثاً ـ بما سيجيء من الكلام على دليل اتحاد الإيمان والإسلام.
First – That performing obligatory acts is the considered religion (al-din al-mu'tabar) due to His saying: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, upright, and to establish prayer and give zakah, and that is the correct religion' – i.e., that mentioned, establishing prayer and others, is the considered religion. And the considered religion is Islam due to His saying: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam.' And Islam is iman as will come. It was answered: First, that (demonstrative) is a masculine singular, and making it refer to the entirety of what preceded is an interpretation that is not better or closer than making it refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or to religiousness (al-tadayyun) and submission (al-inqiyad), and to the preceding commands; indeed, perhaps this is more appropriate to keep the wording on its literal meaning or close to it. Do you not see that His saying: 'Indeed, the number of months with Allah is twelve months ... up to His saying: that is the correct religion' – its meaning is that the religious conviction that the months are twelve, four of them sacred, and submission to that upright religion. Moreover, there is another point: that 'al-din' in that verse (the verse from Surah al-Bayyinah) is added to 'al-qayyima', not described as in this verse. The meaning is the religion of the upright community (millat), so its meaning is not 'the community and path' but rather obedience, as in His saying: 'sincere to Him in religion' (mukhliṣīna lahu al-dīn). And then the entire argument collapses. Secondly – That the meaning of the second verse (Al 'Imran 19) is that the considered religion is the religion of Islam, definitively, because the religion which is the community and path, when added to the Messenger, is not the same as Islam which is an attribute of the responsible person. Thirdly – By what will come in the discussion on the proof of the unity of iman and Islam.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite proof and its three responses. The Mu'tazilites argue that performing obligatory acts is the considered religion (al-din al-mu'tabar) based on Surah al-Bayyinah verse 5, which mentions worship, sincerity, prayer, and zakah, then says 'that is the correct religion'. They claim that this 'that' refers to the mentioned acts, making them the religion. Then they link religion to Islam via Surah Al 'Imran 19, and later they will argue that Islam is iman. The first response is a linguistic argument: the demonstrative 'that' (dhālika) is singular masculine, so it could refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or submission (inqiyad) rather than the entire list of acts. Moreover, the author points to Surah al-Tawbah 36 where 'that is the correct religion' refers to the number of months and submission to that, not to acts. Also, in the Surah al-Bayyinah verse, 'al-din' is in genitive construction (mudaf) to 'al-qayyima', meaning 'the religion of the upright community', which indicates obedience, not the community itself. The second response: the meaning of Surah Al 'Imran 19 is that the considered religion is the religion of Islam, and since 'religion' when added to the Messenger refers to the community and path, it cannot be the same as 'Islam' which is an attribute of the responsible person (i.e., the individual's submission). The third response is deferred to later discussion on the unity of iman and Islam. Thus the proof is refuted.

Key Terms
الدين
الإسلام
الإيمان
الإخلاص
الانقياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'that is the correct religion' necessarily refers to the mentioned acts (prayer, zakah, etc.) and thus proves that performing those acts is the religion itself.
Correction: The demonstrative 'that' (dhālika) is masculine singular and could refer to sincerity (ikhlas) or submission (inqiyad), which are more literal and closer to the linguistic meaning. Also, the phrase 'din al-qayyima' means 'the religion of the upright community', indicating obedience, not the acts themselves.

Checkpoint
What was the first response to the Mu'tazilite proof from Surah al-Bayyinah?
A) The verse is weak
B) The demonstrative 'that' could refer to sincerity, not actions
C) The verse was abrogated
D) The verse refers to the five pillars of Islam

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof from Surah al-Bayyinah is refuted by the linguistic ambiguity of the demonstrative pronoun, showing that the verse does not necessarily equate acts with religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your own life, how often do you interpret Allah's commands based on your own assumptions rather than seeking the intended meaning? The Second Mu'tazilite Proof: Descriptions of True Believers
Second Mu'tazilite proof from Quranic descriptions of believers, answered by showing they indicate perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites used Quranic verses describing believers to argue that actions are part of iman, and how the response shows these verses refer to the perfection of iman.

Core Idea
Actions describe the perfect believer, not the definition of iman.

The Mu'tazilites often cited Quranic verses that describe believers as those who perform good deeds and have trembling hearts. They argued that if actions are mentioned as part of what makes someone a believer, then actions must be part of the definition of iman. In this lesson, we examine their second proof and the concise response that these verses indicate the perfection (kamal) of iman, not its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and the literal translation. Notice the two verses cited and the brief response.
The passage is short; take note of each part.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning
The Mu'tazilites interpret the phrase 'those are the believers truly' as indicating that only those who possess these qualities are true believers. This implies that actions are essential to iman.
The key is that they take 'truly' as a restrictive qualifier.
3
Grasp the response
The response says 'what is meant is the perfection of iman'. This means that these verses describe the ideal believer who has complete iman, not that iman itself is defined by these actions.
The response harmonizes the various proofs by showing they refer to completeness.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider why this distinction matters: if actions were part of iman, then iman would increase and decrease with actions, which the Sunni position denies.
Iman is affirmation of the heart; actions are its fruits.

And it was answered that what is meant is the perfection of iman, combining the proofs.

This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof. They cite verses from Surah al-Anfal and Surah al-Hujurat that describe believers as having trembling hearts and performing good deeds, and then declare them 'the believers truly'. The Mu'tazilites take this to mean that actions are essential to the definition of iman. The response is concise: these verses indicate the perfection (kamal) of iman, not its definition. The author notes that the various proofs are combined to show that the complete and perfect believer possesses these qualities, but iman itself is the affirmation that forms the basis.

Third Mu'tazilite Proof: Prayer as 'Faith'
Third Mu'tazilite proof with interpretation of 'iman' as prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazilite proof from the verse about prayer and why its contextual interpretation does not prove that iman means action.

Core Idea
Prayer is called 'faith' contextually, not by definition.

The Mu'tazilites cite a verse where 'iman' is interpreted as prayer to argue that faith includes actions. Sanusi presents this proof and explains that the verse refers specifically to prayer towards Jerusalem, a contextual usage that does not alter the lexical meaning of iman. This lesson examines the proof and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source and its literal English translation. Note how the verse is cited and immediately glossed as 'your prayer towards Jerusalem'.
2
Identify the proof and interpretation
Recognize that the Mu'tazilites use this verse as evidence that 'iman' can mean salat (prayer). The interpreter assigns a specific referent: prayer towards the former qibla.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The explanation 'i.e., your prayer towards Jerusalem' shows the verse uses 'iman' contextually for prayer, not as a general definition. This does not prove that all acts are part of iman in the lexical sense.
The key point is contextual vs. lexical meaning.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this proof fails to change the definition of iman as tasdiq (assent). The verse is about a specific historical occasion, not a doctrinal redefinition.

Third – His saying: 'And Allah would never waste your faith' – i.e., your prayer towards Jerusalem.

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazilite proof. They cite the verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:143) which says 'And Allah would never waste your faith' in the context of the change of the qibla from Jerusalem to Mecca. Here, 'iman' (faith) is interpreted by many commentators as referring to prayer (salat) toward Jerusalem. The Mu'tazilites use this as evidence that iman can mean actions (prayer) in the Quran. The response is implicit in the explanation: the verse is interpreted figuratively or contextually, as 'prayer' is a manifestation of faith. However, this does not prove that the general definition of iman includes all actions. The passage ends the proof with that simple explanation, indicating that the Mu'tazilite citation is answered by noting the specific interpretation, which does not change the core definition of iman as tasdiq.

Taftazani's Threefold Refutation on 'Din'
Taftazani refutes an argument about the meaning of 'din' in Quranic verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's threefold refutation of an argument that misinterpreted the meaning of 'din' in Quranic verses.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'din' shifts between 'obedience' and 'community' based on context.

In this passage, Taftazani dismantles an opponent's argument that tried to use Quranic verses to establish a particular meaning of 'din'. He offers three precise criticisms, each targeting a different weakness in the reasoning. Pay close attention to how he shifts between linguistic analysis, contextual interpretation, and theological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once through
Read the Arabic and English translation together to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the sequential markers 'firstly', 'secondly', 'thirdly'.
2
Identify the three refutation points
Notice the sequential markers 'firstly', 'secondly', 'thirdly' and see how each point challenges the opponent.
3
Analyze the first point's linguistic argument
Taftazani uses the annexation structure to argue that 'din' in the verse means obedience, not religion community.
Focus on the phrase 'al-din al-qayyim' and the difference between annexation and description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل ربما يكون هذا أولى لبقاء اللفظ على معناه اللغوي أو قريباً منه . ألا ترى أن قوله تعالى: إن عدة الشهور عند الله اثنا عشر شهراً .. إلى قوله ذلك الدين القيم (1) هو معناه أن التدين بكون الشهور اثنا عشر أربعة منها حرم، والانقياد لذلك الدين المستقيم ، على أن ههنا شيئاً آخر، وهو أن الدين في تلك الآية مضاف إلى القيمة ، لا موصوف كما في هذه الآية . والمعنى دين الملة القيمة، فلا يكون معناه الملة والطريقة ، بل الطاعة كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ مخلصين له الدين (۲) وحينئذ سقط الاستدلال بالكلية . وثانياً ـ بأن معنى الآية الثانية أن الدين المعتبر هو دين الإسلام للقطع بأن الدين الملة والطريقة التي تعتبر غالباً إضافتها إلى الرسول لا تكون نفس الإسلام الذي هو صفة المكلف. وهو وثالثاً ـ بما سيجيء من الكلام على دليل اتحاد الإيمان والإسلام.
But perhaps this is more appropriate because the word retains its literal meaning or close to it. Do you not see that His saying, Exalted is He, 'Indeed the number of months with Allah is twelve months' up to His saying 'That is the straight religion (al-din al-qayyim)'? Its meaning is that the religious commitment to the months being twelve, four of them are sacred, and submission to that straight religion. Moreover, there is another matter here, which is that 'al-din' in that verse is annexed to 'al-qayyim', not described as in this verse. And the meaning is 'the religion of the straight community', so it does not mean the community and way, but rather obedience, as in His saying, 'sincere to Him in religion'. And thus the deduction falls entirely. Secondly, because the meaning of the second verse is that the religion considered is the religion of Islam, decisively, because the religion, the community and way, which is usually attributed to the Messenger, is not itself Islam which is the attribute of the obligated person. And thirdly, by what will come in the discussion on the proof of the unity of faith and Islam.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that used the verse about months to claim something about the meaning of 'din'. The author gives three points. First point: The verse shows that 'din' retains its linguistic meaning (obedience/submission) because it is described as 'qayyim' (straight), and the meaning of the verse is that the months are a religious matter. Also, 'din' in that verse is annexed (not described), so it means obedience. Second point: The meaning of the second verse is that the accepted religion is Islam, because 'din' when added to the Messenger often means the way/community, not the attribute of the individual. Third point: The proof of the unity of faith and Islam will be discussed later, so the argument collapses. This is a dense multilayered refutation.

الإسلام
الملة
الطريقة
الطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'din' always means 'religion' as a community or way.
Correction: This passage clarifies that 'din' can mean 'obedience' or 'submission' based on linguistic context, as in the phrase 'al-din al-qayyim'.

The author cites verses describing believers and answers that they refer to perfect faith

Quranic descriptions of believers refer to perfect faith, not its minimum.

In theological debates about faith, some cite verses that appear to restrict 'believers' to those with trembling hearts and unwavering certainty. The Meaning of 'Iman' in the Jerusalem Prayer Verse
The author explains the third argument and its refutation regarding the meaning of 'iman' in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars interpret the word 'iman' in the verse about changing the qibla, and why it does not equate faith with prayer.

Core Idea
'Iman' in the verse does not literally mean prayer; it is either belief in its obligation or a metaphor.

In this lesson, we examine the third argument that claims the Quranic term 'iman' refers to prayer, thus equating faith and prayer. The response clarifies that 'iman' here has multiple possible meanings, none of which collapse faith into the act of prayer. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct theological reasoning about the relationship between faith and works.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three answers given

3
Understand the metaphor explanation
Pay close attention to the third interpretation: prayer is a branch of faith, so it is called 'iman' metaphorically. This is the most nuanced answer.
4
Connect the supporting hadith
The hadith 'Between a servant and disbelief is the abandonment of prayer' is cited to show the strong link between prayer and faith. Consider how this supports the metaphorical interpretation.
5
Reflect on the theological significance

Fourth Argument: The Highway Robber and Divine Disgrace
Refutation of the fourth argument about the highway robber

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument that a highway robber cannot be a believer is refuted by limiting the scope of Quranic verses to the Companions and requiring proof of universal application.

Core Idea
Disgrace does not automatically exclude a believer; context and proof matter.

In this passage, the author tackles a specific theological argument that claims a highway robber cannot be a true believer because he will be disgraced. This lesson walks through the argument, its refutation, and the subsequent objection and reply. You will see how careful interpretation of Quranic verses is central to resolving such debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the structure: argument, answer, objection, reply.
2
Identify the argument's logic
Trace the syllogism: highway robbers are disgraced (verse), all who are disgraced enter Fire, believers are not disgraced (verse), so robbers are not believers.
The key is the major premise about believers not being disgraced.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer denies the major premise by specifying the verse about no disgrace refers only to the Companions, not all believers.
Also note that the robber is not a disbeliever.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
The objector points out that Companions include sinners, so the argument could still work. The reply requires proof that such sinners are not forgiven and that the verses are general.
This shows the requirement for definitive proof in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن كل قاطع الطريق يخزى يوم القيامة لأنه يدخل النار بدليل قوله تعالى ولهم في الآخرة عذاب النار (۲) وكل من يدخل النار يخزى بدليل قوله تعالى حكاية وتقريراً: ربنا إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيته (۳) ولا شيء من المؤمن يخزى يوم القيامة ، لقوله تعالى : (يوم لا يخزي الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه (٤) وأجيب بمنع الكبرى، فإن المراد بالذين آمنوا معه الصحابة، لا كل مؤمن، ولا يصح لهم التمسك بقوله تعالى : إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين (٥) لأن القاطع ليس بكافر. فإن قيل : هب أن ليس في الذين آمنوا معه قاطع طريق، لكن لا شك أن فيهم العاصي والباغي، وبهذا يتم الاستدلال . قلنا : إنما يتم لو ثبت بالدليل أنه لا يعفى عنه، ولا يثاب عليه، بل يدخل النار البتة، وأن الآيات الثلاث مجراة على العموم.
Fourth: That every highway robber will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection because he enters the Fire, by the evidence of His saying: 'And for them in the Hereafter is the punishment of the Fire.' And everyone who enters the Fire is disgraced by the evidence of His saying, told and affirmed: 'Our Lord, indeed whoever You admit to the Fire, You have disgraced him.' And no believer is disgraced on the Day of Resurrection by the evidence of His saying: 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him.' And the answer is the denial of the major premise, for what is meant by 'those who believed with him' is the Companions, not every believer. And they cannot cling to His saying: 'Indeed disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' because the robber is not a disbeliever. If it is said: Suppose there is no highway robber among those who believed with him, but there is no doubt that among them are the disobedient and the aggressor, and by this the deduction is complete. We say: It would be complete only if it is established by proof that he is not forgiven and is not rewarded but enters the Fire absolutely, and that the three verses are applied universally.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument and its detailed refutation. The argument claims that highway robbers are disgraced, and since believers are not disgraced, robbers cannot be believers. The answer denies the major premise: the verse about no disgrace for believers refers specifically to the Companions, not all believers. The objector then points out that the Companions include sinners, so the argument still stands. The author responds that it would only work if it is proven that such sinners are not forgiven and enter the Fire, and that the verses are general. This is a classic kalam debate on whether major sins cause eternal punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Highway robbers cannot be believers because they will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection.
Correction: The verse about no disgrace for believers is specifically about the Companions, not all believers. Also, robbers are not disbelievers, so other verses do not apply. The argument fails without proof of universal application.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects the argument that a highway robber cannot be a believer?
A) The robber is a disbeliever.
B) The verse about no disgrace refers only to the Companions.
C) All believers are disgraced.
D) The robber is forgiven.

Key Takeaway
Theological arguments must be based on precise Quranic interpretation and definitive proof; verses are not always universal in scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about punishment, do you instinctively apply them to yourself as if you are the primary addressee? How does this lesson's emphasis on precise interpretation (e.g., 'those who believed with him' being specific) challenge you to read the Quran with more care and humility?
?
The argument assumes that a highway robber's sin automatically excludes him from faith because he will be disgraced. Is there anyone in your life whom you have written off as beyond Allah's mercy due to their sins? ?

Fourth Objection: The Highway Robber Will Be Disgraced
Taftazani presents the fourth objection against defining iman as tasdiq, with a syllogism and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against defining iman as tasdiq, its syllogistic structure, and how Taftazani refutes it by specifying the referent of 'those who believed with him' as the Companions and challenging the absolute punishment of believers.

Core Idea
The objection's major premise fails because the Quranic promise applies only to the Companions.

This passage presents the fourth objection to the view that iman is mere tasdiq. The objector argues that every highway robber will be disgraced, but no believer will be disgraced, so a robber cannot be a believer. Taftazani refutes this by narrowing the scope of the relevant Quranic verse and questioning the absolute punishment of disobedient believers. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's syllogism
Identify the three parts of the syllogism: major premise (everyone who enters Fire is disgraced), minor premise (every robber enters Fire), and conclusion (robber is not a believer). Note the Quranic proofs for each premise.
The objector uses two Quranic verses for the premises and one for the conclusion.
2
Understand the refutation
Notice that the answer denies the major premise by specifying that the verse about no disgrace refers specifically to the Companions, not all believers. Also, the robber is not a disbeliever, so the disgrace verse for disbelievers does not apply.
The key move is to restrict the scope of 'those who believed with him'.
3
Consider the further objection and response
See how the objector tries to include disobedient believers among the Companions. The response requires proof of absolute punishment and universal application of the verses, which is not established.
The argument remains inconclusive because the conditions are not met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن كل قاطع الطريق يخزى يوم القيامة لأنه يدخل النار بدليل قوله تعالى ولهم في الآخرة عذاب النار وكل من يدخل النار يخزى بدليل قوله تعالى حكاية وتقريراً: ربنا إنك من تدخل النار فقد أخزيته ولا شيء من المؤمن يخزى يوم القيامة ، لقوله تعالى : (يوم لا يخزي الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه وأجيب بمنع الكبرى، فإن المراد بالذين آمنوا معه الصحابة، لا كل مؤمن، ولا يصح لهم التمسك بقوله تعالى : إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين لأن القاطع ليس بكافر. فإن قيل : هب أن ليس في الذين آمنوا معه قاطع طريق، لكن لا شك أن فيهم العاصي والباغي، وبهذا يتم الاستدلال . قلنا : إنما يتم لو ثبت بالدليل أنه لا يعفى عنه، ولا يثاب عليه، بل يدخل النار البتة، وأن الآيات الثلاث مجراة على العموم.
Fourth: That every highway robber will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection because he enters the Fire, by evidence of His statement, the Exalted, 'And for them in the Hereafter is the punishment of the Fire.' And everyone who enters the Fire is disgraced, by evidence of His statement, the Exalted, narrated and affirmed: 'Our Lord, indeed whoever You cause to enter the Fire, You have disgraced him.' And none of the believers will be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection, by evidence of His statement, the Exalted: 'On the Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him.' And it was answered by denying the major premise: indeed what is meant by 'those who believed with him' is the Companions, not every believer. And it is not valid for them to cling to His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed the disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' because the robber is not a disbeliever. If it is said: suppose that among those who believed with him there is no highway robber, but there is no doubt that among them are the disobedient and the oppressor, and by this the deduction is complete. We say: it would only be complete if it were established by evidence that he is not forgiven and not rewarded, but rather enters the Fire absolutely, and that the three verses are applied universally.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against the definition of iman as merely tasdiq (assent). The objector constructs a syllogism: (1) every highway robber will be disgraced because he enters the Fire; (2) everyone who enters the Fire is disgraced; (3) no believer will be disgraced; thus, a highway robber cannot be a believer. The answer (al-Jawab) refutes the major premise by specifying that the verse 'On the Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' refers specifically to the Companions, not to all believers. Furthermore, the robber is not a disbeliever, so the other verse about disgrace upon disbelievers does not apply. A further objection is raised: even if the robber is not among those addressed, there are still disobedient and oppressive people among the Companions, so the argument might still hold. The response rejects this because it must first be proven that such people are absolutely punished without forgiveness or reward, and that the three verses are general in application—both of which are contested.

Key Terms
قاطع الطريق
خزي
المؤمن
الصحابة
الكبرى
الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disgrace mentioned in the Quran applies to all believers equally, so any believer who sins will be disgraced.
Correction: The verse about no disgrace specifically addresses the Prophet and the Companions; it does not guarantee that all believers will escape disgrace. Also, the robber is not a disbeliever, so the verse about disgrace on disbelievers does not apply.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the fourth objection?
A) The highway robber is a disbeliever.
B) The verse about disgrace applies only to the Companions, not all believers.
C) The robber will be forgiven.
D) The syllogism is invalid because the minor premise is false.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because the Quranic promise of no disgrace applies specifically to the Companions, not to all believers, and because punishment of disobedient believers is not absolute.
