The Fifth Objection: Hyperbolic Hadiths on Faith and Major Sins
Taftazani presents the fifth objection using hadith and answers with hyperbole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars interpreted hadiths that seem to negate faith from sinners as hyperbolic emphasis, not literal nullification of iman.

Core Idea
Hyperbole in hadith warns against sin without stripping believers of their faith.

This lesson presents the fifth objection raised against the Ash'ari position that major sins do not negate iman. The objectors cite apparent hadiths that deny faith to fornicators and thieves. Taftazani records the answer that these are hyperbole, similar to Quranic verses, and also notes a counter-citation of other hadiths retaining faith even for sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection hadiths
Read the two hadiths that objectors use: 'The fornicator does not fornicate while a believer' and 'No faith for one without trust or covenant.' Notice the apparent negation of iman.
These are the main proof texts for the opposition.
2
Understand the answer as hyperbole
The answer interprets these hadiths as hyperbolic emphasis (tighliz wa mubalaghah) to warn against sins, not as literal removal of faith. See the Quranic example of the Hajj verse.
Hyperbole is a recognized rhetorical device in both Quran and hadith.
3
See the counter-citation
The same Prophet (peace be upon him) also said: 'Even if he fornicates or steals' and 'Even if the nose of Abu Dharr is rubbed in dust,' affirming that faith remains despite sins.
This shows the need to reconcile texts, not take one set literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - قوله ( عليه السلام) لا يزني الزاني وهو مؤمن، ولا يسرق السارق وهو مؤمن » ( لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له ولا إيمان لمن لا عهد له» . وأجيب بأنه على قصد التغليظ والمبالغة في الوعيد كقوله تعالى في تارك الحج ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين ) والمعارضة بمثل قوله (عليه السلام) وإن زنى، أو سرق . . » حتى قال : وإن رغم أنف أبي ذر».
Fifth: His statement, peace be upon him: 'The fornicator does not fornicate while he is a believer, and the thief does not steal while he is a believer' and 'There is no faith for one who has no trustworthiness, and there is no faith for one who has no covenant.' And it was answered that it is intended for exaggeration and emphasis in warning, like His statement, the Exalted, regarding the one who abandons Hajj: 'And whoever disbelieves, then indeed Allah is free from need of the worlds.' And it is countered by similar statements of his, peace be upon him: 'Even if he fornicates or steals...' until he said: 'Even if the nose of Abu Dharr is rubbed in dust.'
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection: hadiths that apparently negate iman from those who commit major sins (zina, theft, breach of trust). The answer interprets these as hyperbole (mubalaghah) to emphasize the gravity of the sin, not as literal removal of iman. The example given is the Quranic verse about abandoning Hajj, which uses the word 'kufr' but is understood as exaggeration in condemnation. The answer further counters (al-mu'aradah) with other hadiths that affirm iman even in such sinners, like: 'Even if he fornicates or steals...' and the saying about Abu Dharr.

Key Terms
زنى
سرق
أمانة
عهد
تغليظ
مبالغة
وعيد
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these hadiths literally mean that anyone who commits zina or theft ceases to be a believer entirely.
Correction: The answer shows they are hyperbolic warnings, supported by other hadiths that affirm faith despite sins.

Checkpoint
How are the hadiths 'no iman for the fornicator' interpreted in the answer?
A) They are abrogated.
B) They are meant literally but only for persistent sinners.
C) They are hyperbole to emphasize the severity of the sin.
D) They apply only to the Prophet's companions.

Key Takeaway
Hyperbolic hadiths about faith and sin are interpreted as emphatic warnings, not literal statements of disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a stern warning in the Quran or hadith, do you rush to judge others as lost, or do you first examine your own soul for the same failing? How can you train yourself to hear such warnings as a call to self-accountability rather than a weapon against others?
?
If you knew that a fellow Muslim had committed zina or theft, would you consider them less of a believer? Based on this lesson, how will you reconcile the gravity of sin with the hope that faith remains, and what does that demand from you in how you treat them?
?
The prophet ﷺ said, 'Even if the nose of Abu Dharr is rubbed in dust.' Are you willing to accept humiliation and correction when you sin, or do you resist it? What is one concrete step you can take this week to respond to a warning from Allah with humility instead of defensiveness?

Sixth Objection: Outward Acts as Indicators of Kufr Despite Inner Tasdiq
Taftazani presents the sixth objection and its answer regarding outward acts as indicators of kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against defining iman as mere tasdiq and its answer that the Lawgiver designates certain outward acts as decisive indicators of the absence of inner assent.

Core Idea
Outward acts can be decisive signs that inner assent is actually absent.

The sixth objection challenges the definition of iman as simply inner assent (tasdiq). It argues that if iman were only tasdiq, then outward acts of hostility like hating the Prophet or prostrating to an idol would not be kufr as long as the heart still believes. But these acts are unanimously considered kufr. The answer reveals a key principle: the Lawgiver has designated certain acts as evidence (amarah) that inner assent is not truly present. This passage clarifies the relationship between outward actions and inner faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the conditional statement: if iman were tasdiq, then anyone who assents to something would be a believer. Note the implied problem with restricting to specific matters.
The objection uses a conditional to expose a flaw in the definition.
2
Grasp the needed conclusion
The objection assumes that even with restrictions, acts like hating the Prophet should not be kufr if inner assent remains. But they are clearly kufr, so the premise is false.
The consequent is rejected by consensus.
3
Learn the answer
The answer explains that the Lawgiver designated certain acts as signs (amarat) that inner assent is absent. These acts are not just sins but indicators of kufr.
Contrast with acts like zina which are sins but not necessarily signs of absence of tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - لو كان الإيمان هو التصديق لكان كل مصدق بشيء مؤمناً . وعلى تقدير التقييد بالأمور المخصوصة لزم أن لا يكون بغض النبي ( عليه السلام) وإلقاء المصحف في القاذورات، وسجدة ،الصنم، ونحو ذلك كفراً ما دام تصديق القلب، بجميع ما جاء به النبي عليه السلام) باقياً. واللازم منتف قطعاً. وأجيب بأن من المعاصي ما جعله الشارع أمارة عدم التصديق تنصيصاً عليه ، أو على دليله. والأمور المذكورة من هذا القبيل، بخلاف مثل الزنا وشرب الخمر من غير استحلال
Sixth: If iman were assent, then every assenter to something would be a believer. And on the assumption of restriction to specific matters, it would be necessary that hatred of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and throwing the Mushaf into filth, and prostration to an idol, and similar acts not be considered kufr as long as the heart's assent to all that the Prophet, peace be upon him, brought remains. But the consequent is definitely false. And it was answered: among sins, the Lawgiver has made some as signs of the absence of assent, either by explicit text or by indication. And the mentioned matters are of this type, unlike zina and drinking wine without considering them lawful.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection: if iman were simply tasdiq (assent of the heart), then any person who assents to something would be a believer, but the definition is restricted to specific objects of faith. However, even with that restriction, if one's heart truly assents to all that the Prophet brought, outward acts like hating the Prophet, throwing the Quran into filth, or prostrating to an idol should not be kufr as long as inner assent persists—but they are clearly kufr. The answer explains that the Lawgiver (al-Shari') has designated certain acts as evidence (amarah) that assent is not actually present, either by explicit textual indication or by implication. Therefore, these acts indicate the absence of true tasdiq, unlike zina or drinking wine (unless deemed lawful), which do not necessarily imply negation of tasdiq.

Key Terms
تصديق
بغض النبي
إلقاء المصحف
سجدة الصنم
أمارة
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Outward acts do not affect iman as long as the heart truly believes.
Correction: The Lawgiver designated certain acts as decisive signs that inner assent is absent, so those acts constitute kufr regardless of claimed inner belief.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why are acts like prostrating to an idol considered kufr despite claimed inner assent?
A) They are major sins that automatically negate iman.
B) The Lawgiver designated them as signs that inner assent is absent.
C) They are worse than zina and thus always kufr.
D) The heart's assent is irrelevant if the act is done.

Key Takeaway
Certain outward acts are designated by the Lawgiver as definitive signs that inner assent (tasdiq) is absent, making them kufr despite any claimed inner belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking, 'My heart is pure even if my actions don't always match'? After reading this passage, are you willing to examine which of your outward actions might actually be signs that your inner tasdiq is weaker than you assume?
?
If the Lawgiver has designated certain acts as clear signs of the absence of faith, what acts in your life could be considered such signs? What would it take for you to uproot them completely, not just manage them?
?
The objection assumes one could hate the Prophet (peace be upon him) while claiming to believe in him. Are there any subtle forms of 'hatred' in your heart—resentment toward his commands, annoyance at his sunnah, or irritation with his teachings—that you have been rationalizing? Will you repent from them right now?

Seventh Objection: Tasdiq Coexists with Shirk
Taftazani presents the seventh objection that tasdiq allows shirk, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that tasdiq can coexist with shirk is answered by distinguishing between insufficient generic assent and mere verbal hypocrisy.

Core Idea
Not every assent is faith; the Quran itself distinguishes merely acknowledging Allah from true iman.

Taftazani now addresses a critical objection: if faith is simply assent (tasdiq), then why does the Quran say that most believers still associate partners? And why are there people who claim to believe but are not believers? The answer reveals a crucial nuance—the difference between a minimal acknowledgment and genuine, saving faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two Qur'anic verses
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the objection cites two distinct verses: one about mushrikun who still 'believe', and one about hypocrites who claim belief.
The objection tries to use these verses to prove that tasdiq cannot be the definition of iman.
2
Examine the answer's first distinction
The answer explains that the first verse refers to tasdiq bi Allah wahdah (assent to Allah alone) which is insufficient by consensus. This is not true iman.
This tasdiq is a mere acknowledgment of Allah as Creator, but falls short of the required heartfelt submission.
3
Examine the answer's second distinction
The second verse refers to tasdiq bi al-lisan (assent by the tongue only), which is pure hypocrisy (nifaq). This is not genuine tasdiq of the heart.
Thus, neither example contradicts the definition of iman as heart-based assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أن الإيمان بمعنى التصديق يجامع الشرك ، ونفي الإيمان الشرعي بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون ﴾ وقوله تعالى : ومن الناس من يقول آمنا بالله و باليوم الآخر ، وماهم بمؤمنين ﴾ وأجيب بأن الأول تصديق بالله وحده وهو غير كاف بالاتفاق. والثاني تصديق باللسان فقط، وهو محض النفاق .
Seventh: That iman in the sense of assent coexists with shirk, and the negation of legal iman by His statement, the Exalted: 'And most of them believe not in Allah except while they associate others with Him' and His statement, the Exalted: 'And of the people are those who say, We believe in Allah and the Last Day, but they are not believers.' And it was answered: the first is assent to Allah alone, and it is insufficient by agreement. And the second is assent by the tongue only, which is pure hypocrisy.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection: if iman is tasdiq, then it can coexist with shirk, as the Quran says 'most of them believe in Allah but associate others'. Also, some claim to believe but are not believers. The answer distinguishes: the first verse refers to a tawhid that acknowledges Allah as Creator but still commits shirk—this tasdiq is insufficient by consensus. The second refers to mere verbal assent, which is hypocrisy (nifaq), not true iman. Thus, these verses do not undermine the definition of iman as genuine heart assent.

Key Terms
إيمان
شرك
تصديق
لسان
نفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any form of tasdiq equals 'iman' in a religious sense.
Correction: The Quranic examples show that not all assent is true iman. One is insufficient generic acknowledgment, the other is mere hypocrisy. True iman requires heartfelt assent.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain the verse 'most of them believe in Allah but associate others'?
A) It refers to a valid iman that is deficient.
B) It means they believe in Allah alone, which is not enough.
C) It refers to hypocrites.
D) It shows that tasdiq can include shirk.

Key Takeaway
The two Qur'anic verses cited do not refute the definition of iman as heart-based assent, because one is insufficient generic acknowledgment and the other is mere verbal hypocrisy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Shahada or say 'I believe in Allah,' is it a deep, heart-felt certainty that shapes your entire life, or is it merely an intellectual acknowledgment like that of the polytheists?
?
Are there areas of your life—your habits, priorities, or secret thoughts—where your tongue professes faith but your heart is not truly submitted? What would it take to align them?
?
Could it be that your 'iman' is simply cultural or inherited, lacking the transformative tasdiq that distinguishes true believers from mere hypocrites? What step will you take today to move from verbal assent to heartfelt certainty?

Eighth Objection: Does 'Believer' Imply Impeccability?
Taftazani begins the eighth objection about the term 'mu'min' implying exalted praise, but the answer is cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth objection against calling major sinners 'believers' based on the high praise implied by the term 'mu'min'.

Core Idea
The objection uses 'believer' as a term of highest praise, questioning its application to sinners.

Taftazani presents the eighth objection against the standard Ash'ari position on faith. The objector argues that the term 'mu'min' (believer) inherently denotes utmost praise and glorification, as seen in the Quranic description of prophets as 'believing servants'. If so, it seems inappropriate to call a major sinner a believer. This lesson examines the objection as far as it is presented here; the answer will be covered in the next section.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that it begins with 'Eighth' and presents the core argument before breaking off.
The text ends mid-sentence at 'And the committer of a major sin...'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the Arabic terms: 'mu'min' (believer), 'madḥ' (praise), 'taʿẓīm' (glorification), and 'murtakib al-kabīra' (committer of a major sin).
3
Understand the objection's logic
The objector claims that the name 'mu'min' entails utmost praise. The Quranic verse about prophets ('Indeed he is among Our believing servants') is used as evidence. Therefore, major sinners cannot be called 'mu'min' because they do not deserve such praise.
This is the beginning of the objection; the full answer is not provided here.
4
Note the incomplete state
Recognize that this passage is a fragment. The objection is cut off, and the response from the Ash'ari perspective is missing. This lesson only covers the objection.
The continuation likely contains the rebuttal.

The objection is that major sinners cannot be so praised, so they cannot be called 'mu'min'. However, the text breaks off in the middle of the objection, and the answer is not provided in this section. The argument is incomplete, likely to be continued on the next page. (Note: The lesson is truncated.)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'mu'min' is so praiseworthy that it cannot be used for anyone who sins.
Correction: While 'mu'min' can denote high praise in its perfect sense, Islamic theology maintains that a person can be called a believer despite committing major sins, as long as they have faith. The objection is addressed by distinguishing between absolute perfection and minimal qualification.

D) The prophets are the only true believers.

Key Takeaway
The eighth objection argues that the term 'mu'min' implies the highest praise, based on Quranic usage, making it unsuitable for major sinners; the answer is not included in this passage.

?

?
The Quran calls prophets 'Our believing servants'. Does that description inspire you to strive for a higher level of faith, or does it make you feel that true 'faith' is out of reach for someone like you? Be honest.

Objection and Answer on the Hadith 'No Faith for the Untrustworthy'
Objection and answer on the hadith about trustworthiness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars interpret severe warnings in hadiths as hyperbole rather than literal negation of faith.

Core Idea
Threats in hadith are often hyperbole, not literal negation of faith.

This passage presents a theological objection: does the hadith 'No faith for the untrustworthy' mean that an untrustworthy person has no faith? The answer, grounded in Quranic usage, shows that such expressions are severe warnings. But the objector then counters with another hadith that affirms faith despite sins, creating a tension we must resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector quotes the hadith 'No faith for one who has no trustworthiness' to argue that lack of trustworthiness negates iman. Notice the direct claim.
The objector is using a literal reading of the hadith.
2
Study the answer
The defender answers that such expressions are for intensification (mubalaghah) and exaggeration in warning, citing the Quranic verse about abandoning Hajj where 'disbelief' is used hyperbolically.
This is a common method: interpreting apparent threats as hyperbole.
3
Examine the counter-objection
The objector responds with another hadith that affirms faith even for those who commit adultery and theft, indicating that major sins do not negate iman. This strengthens the objection.
The hadith about 'even if he commits adultery or steals' is used to argue that sin does not remove faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السارق وهو مؤمن (۱) ( لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له ولا إيمان لمن لا عهد له) (۲). وأجيب بأنه على قصد التغليظ والمبالغة في الوعيد كقوله تعالى في تارك الحج ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين (۳) والمعارضة بمثل قوله (عليه السلام) وإن زنى أو سرق حتى قال : وإن رغم أنف أبي ذر.
The thief while he is a believer (1) 'There is no faith for one who has no trustworthiness and no faith for one who has no covenant' (2). And it was answered that it is for the purpose of intensification and exaggeration in the threat, like His statement the Most High concerning the one who abandons Hajj: 'And whoever disbelieves, then indeed Allah is Free from need of the worlds' (3). And the objection with the like of his statement (peace be upon him) 'Even if he commits adultery or steals' until he said: 'Even if the nose of Abu Dharr is rubbed in the dust'.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the definition of iman. The objector points out that the hadith 'No faith for the untrustworthy' seems to deny faith to whoever lacks trustworthiness, even if he has assent (tasdiq). The answer explains that such expressions are for the purpose of hyperbole and severe warning, not literal negation of iman, citing the Quranic verse about abandoning Hajj where disbelief is mentioned as exaggeration. The objector then counters with another hadith that affirms faith even for those who commit adultery and theft, indicating that grave sins do not negate iman. The phrase 'until he said: Even if the nose of Abu Dharr is rubbed in the dust' emphasizes the persistence of faith despite sins.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hadiths like 'No faith for the untrustworthy' literally mean an untrustworthy person is a disbeliever.
Correction: Such expressions are hyperbolic warnings (taghliz wa mubalaghah), not literal negation of faith, as shown by the Quranic verse and other hadith.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the expression 'لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له' according to the answer?
A) To literally negate iman
B) To intensify the warning and exaggerate the threat
C) To indicate that trustworthiness is a condition of iman
D) To make iman dependent on actions

Key Takeaway
Expressions of severe warning in Islamic texts are often not literal but serve to emphasize the gravity of the sin.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Sixth objection: Iman as tasdiq leads to absurd consequences; the answer distinguishes sins that indicate disbelief
Objection and answer about sins as indicators of disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that defining iman as simple tasdiq leads to absurd consequences, and the answer that the Lawgiver designates certain sins as signs of disbelief.

Core Idea
Some sins are designated as signs of disbelief by the Lawgiver, not all sins.

The sixth objection against defining iman as mere mental assent tries to show that it would make even the gravest acts of disobedience—like hating the Prophet or prostrating to idols—merely sinful rather than disbelief, as long as the heart's assent remains intact. The answer introduces a crucial distinction: certain sins are explicitly made by the Lawgiver into indicators (amarat) of the absence of genuine tasdiq. This passage clarifies that not every sin is equal in its relationship to faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection begins by stating that if iman were simply tasdiq, then anyone who assents to anything would be a believer. Then it considers a restricted version.
Notice the two prongs of the objection.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The objection claims that even with restriction, acts like hating the Prophet would not be kufr if heart's assent remains. This is obviously false.
This is meant to be a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the answer
The answer explains that the Lawgiver has designated some sins as indicators (amarat) of lack of tasdiq. The mentioned acts fall under that category.
This distinction is crucial for the definition of iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - لو كان الإيمان هو التصديق لكان كل مصدق بشيء مؤمناً . وعلى تقدير التقييد بالأمور المخصوصة لزم أن لا يكون بغض النبي ( عليه السلام) وإلقاء المصحف في القاذورات، وسجدة الصنم، ونحو ذلك كفراً ما دام تصديق القلب بجميع ما جاء به النبي عليه السلام باقياً. واللازم منتف قطعاً. وأجيب بأن من المعاصي ما جعله الشارع أمارة عدم التصديق تنصيصاً عليه ، أو على دليله. والأمور المذكورة من هذا القبيل، بخلاف مثل الزنا وشرب الخمر من غير استحلال
Sixth: If iman were tasdiq, then everyone who assents to something would be a believer. And on the assumption of restricting to specific matters, it would follow that hatred of the Prophet (peace be upon him), throwing the Mushaf into filth, prostration to an idol, and the like would not be kufr as long as the assent of the heart to all that the Prophet brought remains. The consequent is definitely false. And it was answered: Among sins, the Lawgiver has made some as indicators of lack of tasdiq, either by explicit text or by its evidence. The mentioned matters are of this type, unlike such as zina and drinking wine without considering them lawful.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection against defining iman as tasdiq. It argues that if iman is simple assent, then anyone who assents to anything would be a believer, and even if we restrict to specific beliefs, then actions like hating the Prophet, desecrating the Quran, or prostrating to idols would not constitute disbelief as long as heart's assent remains. This is obviously false. The answer distinguishes between sins that are mere disobedience and sins that the Lawgiver has designated as evidence (amara) of the absence of genuine tasdiq, whether by explicit text or by clear evidence. The mentioned acts are of the latter type, whereas zina and drinking wine without considering them lawful are not necessarily indicators of lack of tasdiq.

Key Terms
تصديق
أمارة
تنصيص
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all sins are equally indicative of lack of faith.
Correction: The Lawgiver designates certain sins (like hatred of the Prophet, idol prostration) as explicit signs of disbelief, while other sins (like zina, drinking wine) are not necessarily indicators of lack of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why do hatred of the Prophet and idol prostration constitute kufr, whereas zina does not?
A) Because the former are more heinous
B) Because the Lawgiver has made them indicators (amarat) of lack of tasdiq
C) Because the former contradict the Shahada
D) Because they are physical actions

Key Takeaway
The Lawgiver designates certain acts as signs of disbelief, so not every sin indicates lack of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions hatred of the Prophet, desecrating the Quran, and prostration to idols as explicit signs of disbelief. Are there any subtle attitudes in your heart—like resentment toward Islamic teachings, neglect of the Quran, or prioritizing worldly desires over Allah—that could be early indicators of weakened tasdiq? What will you do to identify and eliminate them?
?
You likely believe in your heart that Islam is true. But your actions sometimes contradict that belief. How do you distinguish between sins that are mere weakness and sins that might indicate a genuine lack of tasdiq? Are you using the Lawgiver's criteria, or your own justifications?
?
When you see another Muslim commit a sin like drinking alcohol, do you immediately assume their iman is compromised? How does the passage's distinction challenge you to judge less harshly and instead focus on the specific signs the Lawgiver has designated?

5 min
The Objection on Calling Grave Sinners 'Believers' and Its Answer
Objection that 'believer' implies highest praise, inconsistent with grave sins, and the answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth objection against calling grave sinners 'believers' and the answer based on the distinction between tasdiq and actions.

Core Idea
A person is called 'believer' based on tasdiq, not perfection in actions.

This lesson addresses a key objection: since the term 'believer' implies the highest praise and honor, it cannot apply to those who commit grave sins and deserve blame. The answer draws a critical distinction between the essence of faith (tasdiq) and one's deeds, clarifying that absolute praise in the Quran refers to complete faith, not to all believers. Understanding this resolution helps maintain the balance between faith and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that the name 'believer' indicates utmost praise, supported by Quranic verses about prophets. Then note the conclusion that grave sinners cannot be called believers absolutely.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between tasdiq (assent), which is the head of obedience, and actions. The person deserves praise for tasdiq and blame for failing in actions, with no contradiction.
3
Reflect on the Salaf's position
When the Quran uses 'believer' in absolute praise, it refers to complete faith (kamal al-iman), according to the Salaf. This means a grave sinner can still be called a believer in a qualified sense.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن - ان اسم المؤمن ينبيء عن استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم وكفاك قوله تعالى في آخر قصة بعض الأنبياء: إنه من عبادنا المؤمنين (٦) ومرتكب الكبيرة إنما يستحق الذم والعذاب الأليم فلا يستحق اسم المؤمن على الإطلاق. وأجيب بأنه يستحق المدح من جهة التصديق الذي هو رأس الطاعات، والذم من حيث الإخلال بالأعمال، ولا منافاة. وما يقع في معرض المدح على الإطلاق يحمل كل كمال الإيمان، على ما هو مذهب السلف
Eighth: That the name 'believer' indicates deserving utmost praise and honor. Sufficient for you is His statement the Most High at the end of the story of some prophets: 'Indeed, he was of Our believing servants' (37:81, 111, 132). And the committer of a major sin only deserves blame and painful punishment, so he does not deserve the name 'believer' absolutely. And it was answered: He deserves praise from the aspect of tasdiq, which is the head of acts of obedience, and blame from the aspect of failing in works, and there is no contradiction. And what occurs in the context of absolute praise is interpreted as complete faith, according to the way of the Salaf.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection: the title 'mu'min' implies the highest praise and honor, as seen in the Quran where prophets are described as 'believing servants'. Since a grave sinner deserves blame and punishment, he cannot be called a believer without qualification. The answer distinguishes two aspects: the person deserves praise for his tasdiq (assent), which is the greatest act of obedience, and blame for his deficiency in actions; there is no contradiction. When the Quran uses the term 'believer' in absolute praise, it refers to complete faith (kamal al-iman), which is the understanding of the Salaf. Thus, a grave sinner can still be called a believer in a qualified sense, having iman but not its perfection.

Key Terms
مؤمن
مدح
تعظيم
كبيرة
تصديق
كمال الإيمان
سلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute praise for 'believers' in the Quran means that every believer, including grave sinners, is free from blame and deserving of highest honor.
Correction: Absolute praise refers to complete faith (perfect believers), not to all who have faith; grave sinners are believers in a qualified sense due to their tasdiq, but they also deserve blame for their sins.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does a grave sinner deserve the name 'believer'?
A) Because his good deeds outweigh his sins
B) Because he has tasdiq, the head of obedience
C) Because he repents eventually
D) Because the Salaf said so

Key Takeaway
A grave sinner is still a believer because of his tasdiq, though he lacks complete faith; the term 'believer' in absolute praise refers to perfect faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a believer, do you assume that this title automatically means you are among the 'عبادنا المؤمنين' in the absolute sense, without addressing your own shortcomings in actions? How does this lesson change your understanding of what it truly means to be a believer in God's sight?
?
The objection says grave sinners deserve blame, so they can't be called believers. But the answer says they still deserve praise for their assent. Does your own life reflect a proper balance? That is, do you ever feel that because you commit sins, you are no longer a believer at all, or do you presume upon God's mercy without fearing His punishment? How can you hold both aspects in your heart?
?
The answer uses the Salaf's understanding that absolute praise in the Quran refers to complete faith. Are you content with a level of faith that is not complete? What steps will you take today to move from being merely a believer in name to one who seeks perfect faith, knowing that the path involves both tasdiq and righteous actions?

Sectarian Views on the Grave Sinner
Summary of sectarian positions on the grave sinner and rebuttal of Mu'tazilite arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major theological positions on the grave sinner and the Sunni defense of his status as a believer.

Core Idea
The grave sinner remains a believer; other sects err by misjudging his status.

In this concluding passage, the author surveys the major sectarian views on the status of a Muslim who commits a major sin. While Sunni theology holds that such a person is still a believer (with deficient faith), other groups like the Mu'tazila and Khawarij place him in a different category. We will explore these positions and see how the author refutes the Mu'tazilite reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
Read the first few lines of the source and note the four views: Sunni, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hasan al-Basri.
The Sunni view is 'believer'; Mu'tazila say 'neither believer nor disbeliever'; Khawarij say 'disbeliever'; al-Hasan al-Basri says 'hypocrite'.
2
Examine the Mu'tazilite arguments
The passage presents two Mu'tazilite arguments: (1) taking the agreed-upon (fisq) and leaving the disputed (iman/kufr), and (2) mixing rulings of believer and disbeliever implying a middle station.
3
Understand the Sunni refutation
The author dismisses the first argument as false, and for the second argues that the mixed rulings are not exclusive to kufr, and the claim of 'not a believer for praise' is a retreat from the Mu'tazilite position.
The refutation is concise; notice the phrase 'قلنا : ذاك ليس أحكام الكفر خاصة'.
4
Note the incomplete Kharijite proofs
The author begins citing Kharijite proof texts, but the passage cuts off mid-verse. This indicates the discussion continues later.
The incomplete quote should not be used for independent analysis; it shows the author's plan to refute them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة - (صاحب الكبيرة عندنا مؤمن، وعند المعتزلة لا مؤمن، ولا كافر، وعند الخوارج كافر ، وعند الحسن البصري منافق، ومن شبه المعتزلة أن هذا أخذ بالمتفق عليه وهو الفسق، وترك للمختلف فيه، وهو الايمان والكفر. وفساده ظاهر . ومنها أن له بعض أحكام المؤمن كعصمة الدم والمال، وبعض أحكام الكافر كالذم وسلب أهلية الإمامة والقضاء والشهادة ، فله منزلة بين المنزلتين ، واسم بين الاسمين. قلنا : ذاك ليس أحكام الكفر خاصة، وما قيل : إنه ليس بمؤمن بمعنى استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم رجوع عن المذهب. وللخوارج النصوص الناطقة بكفر العصاة، وبانحصار العذاب على الكفار، مع أن الفاسق معذب، وبأن الفاسق مكذب بالقيامة، وبآيات الله، وبأن مقابل المنفي كافر، مثل قوله تعالى : ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون (۱) ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين (۲) ومثل : إن العذاب على من كذب وتولى (۳) لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى (٤) ومثل: وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار . . إلى قوله: ﴿كنتم بها
Conclusion - The committer of a major sin is in our view a believer; according to the Mu'tazila he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever; according to the Khawarij he is a disbeliever; and according to al-Hasan al-Basri he is a hypocrite. Among the arguments of the Mu'tazila is that this takes the agreed-upon, which is fisq, and leaves the disputed, which is iman and kufr. Its falsity is apparent. And among (their arguments) is that he has some rulings of the believer, like inviolability of blood and property, and some rulings of the disbeliever, like blame and removal of eligibility for leadership, judgeship, and testimony, so he has a station between the two stations and a name between the two names. We say: Those are not specific to the rulings of kufr. And what is said: that he is not a believer in the sense of deserving utmost praise and honor is a retraction from the (Sunni) madhhab. And the Khawarij have texts that speak of the kufr of sinners, and the restriction of punishment to the disbelievers, while the fasiq is punished, and that the fasiq denies the resurrection and the verses of Allah, and that the opposite of that which is negated is kufr, like His statement the Most High: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, then it is they who are the disbelievers' (5:44) and 'And whoever disbelieves, then indeed Allah is Free from need of the worlds' (3:97) and like: 'Indeed the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' (20:48) and 'None shall enter it except the most wretched who denies and turns away' (92:15-16) and like: 'And as for those who defiantly disobeyed, their refuge is the Fire' until His saying: 'You were with it' (?)
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This concluding section presents the views of different sects on the grave sinner (sahib al-kabirah). The Sunni position is that he is a believer (with deficient faith). The Mu'tazila hold the 'station between the two stations' (al-manzilah bayn al-manzilatayn) — neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq. The Khawarij consider him a disbeliever. Al-Hasan al-Basri considered him a hypocrite. The author then refutes the Mu'tazilite arguments: 1) Their argument that they take the agreed-upon (fisq) and leave the disputed (iman/kufr) is false. 2) Their argument from mixed rulings (some believer rulings, some kafir rulings) is answered by saying that those rulings are not exclusive to kufr. Also, the Mu'tazilite claim that the grave sinner is not a believer in the sense of deserving utmost praise is actually a retreat from the Mu'tazilite position. Then the author presents the Kharijite proofs from the Quran, which include verses that appear to call sinners disbelievers. The author lists these verses, presumably to then refute them, but the passage ends mid-verse, likely to be continued.

Key Terms
كبيرة
مؤمن
معتزلة
خوارج
فاسق
منزلة بين المنزلتين
منافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The grave sinner is not a believer at all because of his sin.
Correction: Sunni theology holds that he is a believer with deficient faith; sin does not remove iman entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the Sunni view on the committer of a major sin?
A) He is a believer
B) He is a fasiq
C) He is a kafir
D) He is a munafiq

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position affirms that the grave sinner remains a believer, and the Mu'tazilite arguments for an intermediate status are refuted as inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you feel your faith is completely gone, or do you assume you are still a believer and neglect repentance? This lesson teaches that the grave sinner remains a believer but with deficient faith. Which extreme do you lean toward, and how will you correct it starting today?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite argument that the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever. Have you ever justified your sins by thinking you are 'not that bad' or in a middle state? How does the lesson's refutation of the 'middle station' challenge you to take full responsibility for your sins without despairing of Allah's mercy?
?
The Khawarij view holds that a single major sin makes you a disbeliever. Is there any person or group you have written off as a disbeliever because of their sins? How does this lesson's affirmation that the grave sinner remains a believer call you to show mercy and hope for their repentance, just as you hope for your own?

The Ash'ari Position on the Grave Sinner
The author defends calling the grave sinner a believer by distinguishing between faith (tasdiq) and acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris consider a grave sinner a believer despite deserving blame.

Core Idea
A grave sinner is a believer because his faith (tasdiq) remains, even if his actions are corrupt.

This passage addresses a key theological question: does committing a major sin strip a person of the title 'believer'? The Ash'ari response, rooted in the Salaf's approach, distinguishes between the essence of faith and its perfection. You will see how the author defends the view that a grave sinner is still a believer, deserving both praise for faith and blame for sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Notice the objection that a grave sinner deserves only blame, so cannot be called a believer. Then read the answer: he deserves both praise and blame from different aspects.
The answer introduces the concept of tasdiq as the root of faith.
2
Understand the distinction
The passage explains that praise is for tasdiq, which is the head of obedience, while blame is for neglecting works. These are not contradictory.
This is a key Ash'ari principle: faith is affirmation, sin is a separate matter.
3
Connect to the Salaf's doctrine
The author notes that when praise is given absolutely, it refers to perfect faith, as the Salaf held. This clarifies that calling a grave sinner a believer is not the same as claiming they have perfect iman.
This avoids the misconception that Ash'aris equate sinlessness with faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يستحق الذم والعذاب الأليم فلا يستحق اسم المؤمن على الإطلاق.
وأجيب بأنه يستحق المدح من جهة التصديق الذي هو رأس الطاعات، والذم من حيث الإخلال بالأعمال، ولا منافاة. وما يقع في معرض المدح على الإطلاق يحمل على كمال الإيمان، على ما هو مذهب السلف.
قال: خاتمة -
(صاحب الكبيرة عندنا مؤمن، وعند المعتزلة لا مؤمن ولا كافر، وعند الخوارج كافر، وعند الحسن البصري منافق. ومن شبه المعتزلة أن هذا أخذ بالمتفق عليه وهو الفسق، وترك للمختلف فيه وهو الإيمان والكفر. وفساده ظاهر.
ومنها أن له بعض أحكام المؤمن كعصمة الدم والمال، وبعض أحكام الكافر كالذم وسلب أهلية الإمامة والقضاء والشهادة، فله منزلة بين المنزلتين واسم بين الاسمين.
قلنا: ذاك ليس أحكام الكفر خاصة، وما قيل: إنه ليس بمؤمن بمعنى استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم رجوع عن المذهب)
For he deserves blame and painful punishment, so he does not deserve the name of believer absolutely. And it was answered that he deserves praise from the aspect of affirmation (tasdiq) which is the head of acts of obedience, and blame from the aspect of neglecting works, and there is no contradiction. And what is said in the context of praise absolutely is carried upon the perfection of faith, according to the doctrine of the Salaf. He said: Conclusion - (The grave sinner is, in our view, a believer; in the view of the Mu'tazila, neither believer nor disbeliever; in the view of the Khawarij, a disbeliever; and in the view of Hasan al-Basri, a hypocrite. Among the arguments of the Mu'tazila is that this takes the agreed-upon, which is fisq, and leaves the disagreed-upon, which is faith and disbelief. And its corruption is apparent. And among them is that he has some rulings of the believer, like inviolability of blood and property, and some rulings of the disbeliever, like blame and disqualification from imamate, judiciary, and testimony, so he has a station between the two stations, and a name between the two names. We said: That is not specific rulings of disbelief, and what was said: that he is not a believer in the sense of deserving the utmost praise and exaltation is a retreat from the madhhab.)
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting an objection: the grave sinner deserves only blame, not the name believer. The author replies that he deserves praise for his faith (tasdiq) and blame for his sin, and these are not contradictory. The praise refers to perfect faith, as the Salaf held. Then the author summarizes the positions of different sects in a 'Conclusion' section: Ash'aris say he is a believer, Mu'tazila say neither believer nor disbeliever (the middle station), Khawarij say disbeliever, Hasan al-Basri says hypocrite. The first Mu'tazila argument is: the community agrees he is a 'fasiq' (corrupt), but differs on whether he is 'mu'min' or 'kafir', so we must take the agreed term 'fasiq' and leave the disputed terms. The author says this is corrupt because it abandons the agreed-upon fact that he is either a believer or a disbeliever with no third option. The second Mu'tazila argument is that he has some rulings of a believer (e.g., blood inviolability) and some of a disbeliever (e.g., blame, disqualification from leadership), so he occupies a middle status. The author rebuts: those rulings are not specific to disbelief, and if they mean he is not a believer in the sense of deserving ultimate praise, that is a retreat from their own school (since they deny him any positive faith).

Key Terms
تصديق
صاحب الكبيرة
مؤمن
كمال الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris consider grave sinners to have perfect faith, like a sinless believer.
Correction: Ash'aris call them believers in the fundamental sense (tasdiq), but acknowledge their faith is imperfect due to sin.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position as presented, why does a grave sinner deserve praise?
A) Because of his good deeds
B) Because of his affirmation (tasdiq)
C) Because he repents
D) Because he avoids major sins

Key Takeaway
A grave sinner remains a believer because faith is fundamentally tasdiq (affirmation), distinct from sinful acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you still see yourself as a believer with genuine tasdiq, or do you feel your faith has completely evaporated? How does the Ash'ari distinction between faith and actions change the way you approach repentance and hope in Allah's mercy?
?
Look at a fellow Muslim who is known for a big sin—do you view them as a believer or as an outsider? Based on this lesson, what responsibility do you have towards them in your heart and dealings?
?
The lesson says absolute praise applies only to perfect faith. Where do you honestly stand: are you content with bare minimum faith, or are you striving for the perfection that the Salaf exemplified? What is one concrete step you can take today to move closer to that perfection?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Arguments for the Middle Station
The author refutes the Mu'tazila arguments for a middle station between faith and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Mu'tazila arguments for the 'middle station' and how the Ash'ari refutes them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila middle station collapses because they confuse rulings and abandon the agreed fact that a person is either a believer or disbeliever.

In this concluding section, the author presents the positions of different sects on the grave sinner: Ash'aris say believer, Mu'tazila say neither, Khawarij say disbeliever, Hasan al-Basri says hypocrite. Then he engages the two major Mu'tazila arguments: the first based on taking the agreed term 'fasiq', the second based on mixed rulings. You will see how each argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the sectarian positions
Quickly scan the four views: Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, Hasan al-Basri. This contextualizes the refutation.
The Mu'tazila view is the main target.
2
Analyze the first argument
The Mu'tazila say: the community agrees he is fasiq, but disagrees on iman/kufr, so we take 'fasiq' as the shared term. The author says this is corrupt because the community also agrees he must be either mu'min or kafir—there's no third option.
This argument tries to avoid the dichotomy.
3
Analyze the second argument
The Mu'tazila point to mixed rulings: some like a believer (blood inviolability) and some like a disbeliever (blame, disqualification). So a middle status. The author rebuts: those rulings are not exclusive to disbelief; and if they mean he is not a believer in the absolute praise sense, that is a retreat from their own position.
The Ash'ari agrees he doesn't deserve ultimate praise, but still calls him believer in the basic sense.
4
Summarize the refutation
The Mu'tazila arguments fail because they either ignore the fundamental dichotomy or confuse different meanings of 'believer'.
This preserves the Ash'ari position.

Source Text

We said: That is not specific rulings of disbelief, and what was said: that he is not a believer in the sense of deserving the utmost praise and exaltation is a retreat from the madhhab.)

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting an objection: the grave sinner deserves only blame, not the name believer. The author replies that he deserves praise for his faith (tasdiq) and blame for his sin, and these are not contradictory. The praise refers to perfect faith, as the Salaf held. Then the author summarizes the positions of different sects in a 'Conclusion' section: Ash'aris say he is a believer, Mu'tazila say neither believer nor disbeliever (the middle station), Khawarij say disbeliever, Hasan al-Basri says hypocrite. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila middle station is well-founded because of the mixed rulings the grave sinner receives.
Correction: The rulings are not exclusive to disbelief, and the negation of the name 'believer' in the absolute sense does not imply a third category.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the first Mu'tazila argument based on taking the agreed term 'fasiq'?
A) By showing that fisq itself implies disbelief
B) By pointing out that the disagreement does not eliminate the fundamental dichotomy between faith and disbelief
C) By citing a verse from the Qur'an
D) By saying the community does not agree on fisq

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila attempt to establish a middle station fails because their arguments either ignore the fundamental dichotomy or misuse the concept of rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you interact with a Muslim who openly commits major sins, do you subconsciously treat them as less of a believer? Does your attitude reflect the Ash'ari view (they are still a believer in essence) or the Mu'tazila view (a middle category)? Examine your heart: is there any hidden judgment that contradicts your creed?
?
The Mu'tazila argument used mixed rulings (like disqualification from leadership) to claim an intermediate status. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse external actions or shortcomings with a person's core faith? How can you separate a person's deeds from their essential iman, especially when the person is yourself?
?
If you were to face a grave sin in your own life, would you despair of being a believer? Or would you affirm that your faith remains intact despite the sin, as the Ash'aris teach? What practical difference does your doctrinal position make when you struggle with sin?

Khawarij Scriptural Proofs for Takfir of Sinners
Author presents Khawarij Qur'anic proofs for takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic arguments the Khawarij use to justify declaring grave sinners disbelievers.

Core Idea
The Khawarij interpret literal verses to mean grave sinners are disbelievers.

The Khawarij built their extreme doctrine on a set of Qur'anic verses, interpreting them in a rigid, literal manner without considering context or standard hermeneutical principles. This lesson presents their main proofs as outlined by the author, showing the verses they relied upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij claims
Read the Arabic passage that summarizes the Khawarij position and the verses they cite. Notice how they deduce takfir from these texts.
The Khawarij argue that punishment is only for disbelievers, so anyone punished (like the fasiq) must be a disbeliever.
2
Identify the four cited verses
Locate each verse: 1) 'Whoever does not judge...', 2) 'Indeed the punishment...', 3) 'As for those who are corrupt...', 4) 'Those who disbelieved will be driven...'.
Verses are partially quoted; understand their full context from the commentary.
3
Understand the Khawarij logic
For each verse, note the Khawarij reasoning: e.g., verse 1 labels non-judgment as kufr, so grave sinners who commit injustice are kuffar.
They also argue that the opposite of 'believer' is 'disbeliever', so if someone is not a believer in the full sense, they are a disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللخوارج النصوص الناطقة بكفر العصاة، وبانحصار العذاب على الكفار، مع أن الفاسق معذب، وبأن الفاسق مكذب بالقيامة، وبآيات الله، وبأن مقابل المنفي كافر، مثل قوله تعالى: (ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون) ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين، ومثل: (إن العذاب على من كذب وتولى) (لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى)، ومثل: (وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار) ... إلى قوله: (كنتم بها تكذبون بآياتنا هم أصحاب المشأمة)، ومثل: (وسيق الذين كفروا إلى جهنم زمراً) ... إلى قوله: (وسيق الذين اتقوا ...).
والجواب الدفع بالتخصيص، وبالحمل على التغليظ، وبصرف المطلق إلى الكمال، ونحو ذلك.
And for the Khawarij are the texts speaking of the disbelief of the disobedient, and that punishment is restricted to the disbelievers, while the corrupt (fasiq) is punished, and that the corrupt disbelieves in the Resurrection and in the signs of God, and that the opposite of the negated is disbelief, like His statement: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, then those are the disbelievers' and 'whoever disbelieves, then Allah is free from need of the worlds', and like: 'Indeed the punishment is on those who lie and turn away' 'It will not be admitted except the most wretched who lies and turns away', and like: 'And as for those who are corrupt, their abode is the Fire' ... up to His statement: 'You used to deny Our signs, they are the companions of the left hand', and like: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven to Hell in groups' ... up to His statement: 'And those who feared their Lord will be driven to Paradise in groups'. And the answer is repel by specification, and by carrying upon exaggeration, and by directing the absolute to perfection, and the like.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij arguments based on Qur'anic verses. They claim that the texts label grave sinners as disbelievers (kuffar), restrict punishment to disbelievers (while the fasiq is punished), and imply the fasiq denies the Resurrection and God's signs. They also argue that the opposite of a negated term (like 'believer') is 'disbeliever'. To support this, they cite verses such as 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, those are the disbelievers' (Q.5:44), and verses about punishment for those who lie and turn away, and verses equating 'fasiqun' with the people of the Fire. The author's response is concise: these verses are repelled by three methods: (1) specification (takhsis) — the verses apply to specific grave sinners, not all; (2) carrying upon exaggeration (taghliz) — the term 'kufr' is used hyperbolically to emphasize the gravity of sin, not literal disbelief; (3) directing the absolute (mutlaq) to perfection — the term is directed to the perfect form, as in calling a minor sin 'kufr' metaphorically.

Key Terms
خوارج
كفر
نصوص
عصاة
تكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Khawarij verses are simple and straightforward, proving that any grave sinner is a disbeliever.
Correction: The verses are subject to interpretation; scholars use specification, exaggeration, and other methods to show they do not apply to all sinners.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the Khawarij claims mentioned in the passage?
A) Texts speak of the disbelief of the disobedient
B) Punishment is restricted to disbelievers
C) The fasiq is a hypocrite (munafiq)
D) The opposite of the negated is disbelief

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij use selected Qur'anic verses in a literal and decontextualized manner to argue that grave sinners are disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken a single verse of the Qur'an and used it to judge someone harshly, ignoring its context and other teachings? What would it take for you to instead seek a balanced understanding before forming an opinion?
?
If you were to apply the Khawarij's logic of 'the opposite of the negated is disbelief' to yourself, are there any areas where you might fall short and condemn yourself? How does that make you reconsider the need for mercy and comprehensive study of the deen?
?
When you read verses about punishment for sinners, does it move you to humble self-reflection or to pride against others? Will you commit today to use such verses to fear Allah for yourself rather than to look down on anyone else?

Refuting Khawarij Proofs: Three Interpretive Methods
Author provides three methods to refute Khawarij scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three methods Sunni scholars use to refute the Khawarij's literalist interpretation of the Qur'an: specification, exaggeration, and directing the absolute to perfection.

Core Idea
The verses are not absolute; they are specified, exaggerated, or directed to the perfect form.

Having presented the Khawarij proofs, the author offers a concise refutation using three hermeneutical tools. These methods—takhsis, taghliz, and sarf al-mutlaq ila al-kamal—allow scholars to harmonize the apparent meaning of the verses with the established creed that grave sinners are not disbelievers unless they deny the essentials of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic sentence summarizing the refutation. It lists three methods: al-takhsis, al-haml 'ala al-taghliz, and sarf al-mutlaq ila al-kamal.
The response is brief; each method requires explanation.
2
Understand specification (takhsis)
This method means that the verses are not general; they apply to specific persons or contexts, such as those who deny the revealed law entirely, not every sinner.
For example, 'whoever does not judge...' refers to those who reject the law, not those who sin out of weakness.
3
Understand exaggeration (taghliz)
This means the term 'kufr' is used hyperbolically to emphasize the gravity of the sin, not to indicate actual exit from faith.
Calling a sin 'kufr' is like calling someone who neglects prayer a 'kafir' in a rhetorical sense, but legally they remain Muslim.
4
Understand directing the absolute to perfection
This means the absolute term is interpreted to refer to the perfect or complete form of that concept. For instance, 'kufr' in its absolute sense is directed to its perfect manifestation (denial of faith), not minor acts.
This is similar to applying 'kufr' only to the extreme, perfect form of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللخوارج النصوص الناطقة بكفر العصاة، وبانحصار العذاب على الكفار، مع أن الفاسق معذب، وبأن الفاسق مكذب بالقيامة، وبآيات الله، وبأن مقابل المنفي كافر، مثل قوله تعالى: (ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون) ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين، ومثل: (إن العذاب على من كذب وتولى) (لا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى)، ومثل: (وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار) ... إلى قوله: (كنتم بها تكذبون بآياتنا هم أصحاب المشأمة)، ومثل: (وسيق الذين كفروا إلى جهنم زمراً) ... إلى قوله: (وسيق الذين اتقوا ...).
والجواب الدفع بالتخصيص، وبالحمل على التغليظ، وبصرف المطلق إلى الكمال، ونحو ذلك.
And for the Khawarij are the texts speaking of the disbelief of the disobedient, and that punishment is restricted to the disbelievers, while the corrupt (fasiq) is punished, and that the corrupt disbelieves in the Resurrection and in the signs of God, and that the opposite of the negated is disbelief, like His statement: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, then those are the disbelievers' and 'whoever disbelieves, then Allah is free from need of the worlds', and like: 'Indeed the punishment is on those who lie and turn away' 'It will not be admitted except the most wretched who lies and turns away', and like: 'And as for those who are corrupt, their abode is the Fire' ... up to His statement: 'You used to deny Our signs, they are the companions of the left hand', and like: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven to Hell in groups' ... up to His statement: 'And those who feared their Lord will be driven to Paradise in groups'. And the answer is repel by specification, and by carrying upon exaggeration, and by directing the absolute to perfection, and the like.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij arguments based on Qur'anic verses. They claim that the texts label grave sinners as disbelievers (kuffar), restrict punishment to disbelievers (while the fasiq is punished), and imply the fasiq denies the Resurrection and God's signs. They also argue that the opposite of a negated term (like 'believer') is 'disbeliever'. To support this, they cite verses such as 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, those are the disbelievers' (Q.5:44), and verses about punishment for those who lie and turn away, and verses equating 'fasiqun' with the people of the Fire. The author's response is concise: these verses are repelled by three methods: (1) specification (takhsis) — the verses apply to specific grave sinners, not all; (2) carrying upon exaggeration (taghliz) — the term 'kufr' is used hyperbolically to emphasize the gravity of sin, not literal disbelief; (3) directing the absolute (mutlaq) to perfection — the term is directed to the perfect form, as in calling a minor sin 'kufr' metaphorically.

Key Terms
تخصيص
تغليظ
إطلاق
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the only way to interpret the verses is literally, and any other interpretation is a distortion.
Correction: Islamic hermeneutics provides valid tools like specification and figurative interpretation to reconcile texts with other principles.

Checkpoint
Which method is NOT mentioned in the author's response to the Khawarij scriptural arguments?
A) Specification (takhsis)
B) Exaggeration (taghliz)
C) Abrogation (naskh)
D) Directing the absolute to perfection (sarf al-mutlaq ila al-kamal)

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response to the Khawarij scriptural proofs relies on sound interpretive principles that show the verses do not literally mean every grave sinner is a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, do you instinctively label them as a disbeliever, or do you apply the method of specification (takhṣīṣ) to remember that the Quranic threats are not absolute? Which of these three methods do you most need to practice to correct your own harshness?
?
The Khawarij used scripture to justify excommunication. Is there a belief you hold that you might be taking out of context, refusing to apply the method of 'directing the absolute to perfection'? Will you pause today to examine one such belief and let the hermeneutic principles of this lesson challenge you?

Refutation of the Hypocrite Claim and the Sects' Positions
Refutation of the claim that a grave sinner is a hypocrite and summary of different sects' positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the argument that a grave sinner is a hypocrite and how different Islamic sects label the grave sinner.

Core Idea
The Prophet's hadith on signs of hypocrisy is a warning, not a categorical judgment.

The passage begins by addressing those who call the grave sinner a hypocrite, presenting their evidence from the Prophet's hadith. The author then responds with two clear points, before summarizing the various positions held by different Islamic sects regarding the status of the grave sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for hypocrisy
Read the first part of the passage (in Arabic and translation) which presents the reason some give for calling the grave sinner a hypocrite: that sin proves his faith is false, and that the Prophet listed certain sins as signs of hypocrisy.
Notice the two pieces of evidence: logical (sin contradicts claim) and textual (hadith).
2
Follow the author's two-fold response
The author denies the logical premise (sin does not prove falsehood) and reinterprets the hadith as a severe warning, not a definitive judgment. Understand why this refutation works.
3
Review the sects' positions on naming
Read the part where the author lists the different names given to the grave sinner by various groups: believer (Sunni), neither believer nor disbeliever (Mu'tazila), disbeliever (Khawarij), and hypocrite (Hasan al-Basri).
This shows that the debate is not just about ruling but about naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللقائلين بكونه منافقاً بأن عصيانه دليل على كذبه في دعوى التصديق، وبأن النبي (صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم) جعل الكذب والخيانة وإخلاف الوعد من علامات النفاق.
وأجيب بمنع الأول، وحمل الثاني على تهويل شأن تلك المعاصي.
كما اختلفت الأمة في حكم صاحب الكبيرة، فكذلك في اسمه، بعد الاتفاق على تسميته فاسقاً، فعندنا مؤمن، وعند المعتزلة لا مؤمن ولا كافر ويسمون ذلك المنزلة بين المنزلتين، وعند الخوارج كافر، وعند الحسن البصري منافق. وقد فرغنا من إقامة الأدلة ودفع شبه المعتزلة المبنية على كون الأعمال من الإيمان، فالآن نشير إلى دفع باقي شبههم، وشبه الخوارج، ومن يسميه بالمنافق. فمن شبه المعتزلة ما احتج به واصل بن عطاء على عمرو بن عبيد حتى رجع إلى مذهبه، وهو أنه اجتمعت الأمة على أن صاحب الكبيرة فاسق، واختلفوا في كونه مؤمناً أو كافراً، فوجب ترك المختلف والأخذ بالمتفق عليه. والجواب أن هذا ترك للمتفق عليه، وهو أنه إما مؤمن أو كافر، ولا وساطة.
And for those who say he is a hypocrite: that his disobedience is evidence of his falsehood in claiming to believe, and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) made lying, betrayal, and breaking promises among the signs of hypocrisy. And it was answered: by denying the first, and carrying the second upon terrifying the gravity of those sins. Just as the community differed on the ruling of the grave sinner, similarly on his name, after agreeing to call him a corrupt (fasiq). So in our view he is a believer, in the Mu'tazila view neither believer nor disbeliever and they call that the station between the two stations, in the Khawarij view a disbeliever, and in Hasan al-Basri's view a hypocrite. And we have finished presenting the evidence and repelling the Mu'tazila arguments based on deeds being part of faith, and now we point to repelling the rest of their arguments, and the arguments of the Khawarij, and those who call him a hypocrite. Among the Mu'tazila arguments is that which Wasil ibn Ata argued against Amr ibn Ubayd, until he returned to his school, that the community agreed that the grave sinner is a corrupt, and differed on whether he is a believer or a disbeliever, so it is necessary to leave the disputed and take the agreed-upon. And the answer is that this abandons the agreed-upon, which is that he is either a believer or a disbeliever, with no intermediate.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage first addresses those (notably Hasan al-Basri) who call the grave sinner a hypocrite (munafiq). Their argument: his sin proves he is lying about his faith (since true faith prevents sin), and the Prophet listed lying, betrayal, and breaking promises as signs of hypocrisy. The author replies by denying the premise (sin does not prove falsehood of faith) and interpreting the hadith as a severe warning about the gravity of those sins, not as a definitive judgment of hypocrisy. Then the author summarizes the different positions again and transitions to the final Mu'tazila argument presented by Wasil ibn Ata. Wasil argued: the community agreed that the grave sinner is a 'fasiq' but differed on whether he is a believer or disbeliever, so we must take the agreed-upon term 'fasiq' and leave the disputed. The author's rebuttal is the same as before: this abandons the agreed-upon fact that he must be either a believer or a disbeliever—there is no middle ground. The community did not agree that he is neither believer nor disbeliever; they only differed on which of the two he is.

Key Terms
منافق
علامات النفاق
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a grave sin automatically makes one a hypocrite because of the hadith about signs of hypocrisy.
Correction: The author explains that the hadith is a warning about the seriousness of those sins, not a categorical judgment of hypocrisy. A person can commit these sins and still be a believer with weak faith.

Checkpoint
How did the author respond to the argument that the Prophet's hadith about lying and betrayal makes the grave sinner a hypocrite?
A) He accepted the argument as valid.
B) He denied the premise that sin proves falsehood of faith, and said the hadith is a warning about the gravity of those sins.
C) He argued that the hadith is weak.
D) He claimed the hadith applies only to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
A grave sinner is not automatically a hypocrite; the hadith on signs of hypocrisy is a warning, and the Sunni position is that he remains a believer despite his sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself in a lie or broken promise, do you immediately fear that your faith is in danger? How does this lesson's distinction between a warning sign and a categorical judgment change how you respond to your own failures? Will you still take those sins seriously but without despairing of your belief?
?
Think of a fellow Muslim you know who commits a major sin. Do you ever silently label them as a hypocrite or less of a believer? What would it mean for your heart if you instead saw them as a believer who is struggling—as the Sunni position teaches? Can you commit to seeing them that way starting today?
?
If you were to die tonight with a major sin unrepented, what do you genuinely believe would happen to you? Does this lesson's affirmation that you remain a believer despite sin give you hope, or does it feel like a license to keep sinning? Be honest: which reaction is closer to your heart, and what must you change to align with the warning and hope of this teaching?

Wasil's Argument and the Refutation of the Intermediate Station
Wasil ibn Ata's argument and the author's rebuttal, denying any intermediate status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Wasil ibn Ata's argument for the 'station between the two stations' and why the author rejects it.

Core Idea
There is no middle ground between belief and disbelief in Islam.

After refuting the hypocrisy claim, the author turns to the Mu'tazila argument famously presented by Wasil ibn Ata. Wasil argued that since the community agreed on calling the grave sinner 'fasiq' but differed on whether he is a believer or disbeliever, we should take the agreed-upon term and avoid the disputed one. The author's response is sharp: this actually abandons the agreed-upon fact that he must be one of the two (believer or disbeliever), with no intermediate.

Guided Path
1
Read Wasil's argument
Read the part where Wasil argues that the community agreed on 'fasiq' but disagreed on 'believer' and 'disbeliever', so we should just use 'fasiq' and leave the disputed labels.
2
Understand the author's rebuttal
The author points out that this reasoning abandons the agreed-upon fact that the person is either a believer or a disbeliever. There is no third option, so you cannot avoid the dispute entirely.
Notice the key phrase 'لا وساطة' (no intermediate).
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this debate matters: the Mu'tazila 'station between the two stations' would mean a third category that the author denies. Reflect on how this connects to the earlier refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And we have finished presenting the evidence and repelling the Mu'tazila arguments based on deeds being part of faith, and now we point to repelling the rest of their arguments, and the arguments of the Khawarij, and those who call him a hypocrite. Among the Mu'tazila arguments is that which Wasil ibn Ata argued against Amr ibn Ubayd, until he returned to his school, that the community agreed that the grave sinner is a corrupt, and differed on whether he is a believer or a disbeliever, so it is necessary to leave the disputed and take the agreed-upon. And the answer is that this abandons the agreed-upon, which is that he is either a believer or a disbeliever, with no intermediate.

Commentary
This passage first addresses those (notably Hasan al-Basri) who call the grave sinner a hypocrite (munafiq). Their argument: his sin proves he is lying about his faith (since true faith prevents sin), and the Prophet listed lying, betrayal, and breaking promises as signs of hypocrisy. The author replies by denying the premise (sin does not prove falsehood of faith) and interpreting the hadith as a severe warning about the gravity of those sins, not as a definitive judgment of hypocrisy. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the term 'fasiq' alone is enough to describe a grave sinner's religious status, avoiding the need to label him as believer or disbeliever.

What was the author's main rebuttal to Wasil ibn Ata's argument?
A) He agreed with Wasil and adopted the term 'fasiq'.

D) He claimed that the grave sinner is actually a believer, so Wasil's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A grave sinner must be either a believer or a disbeliever; there is no third category of 'between belief and disbelief'.

?

?
Wasil's argument appeals to taking what is agreed upon, but it misses the essential truth. In your own life, when you face a difficult choice about your identity as a believer, do you ever take the easy middle ground to avoid the full implications of being either a believer or a disbeliever? What are you avoiding by not committing fully?
?
The author says there is no middle ground. Where in your life have you tried to create a middle ground between obedience and disobedience, thinking you can be 'good enough' without fully submitting? What would it mean for you to abandon that middle ground today?
