Naming the Grave Sinner and Refuting the Mu'tazili Consensus Argument
Taftazani enumerates views on the grave sinner's name and refutes the Mu'tazili consensus argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views on naming the grave sinner and how the first Mu'tazili argument from consensus is refuted by identifying the true point of agreement.

Core Idea
The grave sinner is either a believer or a disbeliever — there is no middle station.

After establishing that the umma agrees on calling the grave sinner a fasiq, scholars differed on his actual status. This passage summarizes the key positions and then presents the first major Mu'tazili argument — based on consensus — along with its decisive refutation. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the boundaries of faith and disbelief in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different views
Read the passage carefully and list the four positions on naming the grave sinner mentioned by Taftazani.
Notice that all groups agree he is a fasiq, but differ on his ultimate religious designation.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili argument
Focus on Wasil ibn 'Ata's argument: since the umma agrees on 'fasiq' and disagrees on 'mu'min'/'kafir', we should stick to the agreed term and leave the disputed.
This argument relies on a principle of taking the common ground.
3
Analyze the response
See how the response shows that the real consensus is that the sinner is either mu'min or kafir — the Mu'tazila are abandoning that consensus and adopting a position no one else holds.
The refutation turns the argument on its head.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the argument fails: it mistakes a partial agreement (on 'fasiq') for the full consensus (that there is no third category).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما اختلفت الأمة في حكم صاحب الكبيرة، فكذلك في اسمه، بعد الاتفاق على تسميته فاسقاً، فعندنا مؤمن، وعند المعتزلة لا مؤمن ولا كافر، ويسمون ذلك المنزلة بين المنزلتين، وعند الخوارج كافر. وعند الحسن البصري منافق. وقد فرغنا من إقامة الأدلة ودفع شبه المعتزلة المبنية على كون الأعمال من الإيمان، فالآن نشير إلى دفع باقي شبههم، وشبه الخوارج، ومن يسميه بالمنافق. فمن شبه المعتزلة ما احتج به واصل بن عطاء على عمرو بن عبيد حتى رجع إلى مذهبه، وهو أنه اجتمعت الأمة على أن صاحب الكبيرة فاسق، واختلفوا في كونه مؤمناً أو كافراً، فوجب ترك المختلف والأخذ بالمتفق عليه. والجواب أن هذا ترك للمتفق عليه، وهو أنه إما مؤمن أو كافر ولا وساطة بينهما، وأخذ بما لم يقل به أحد فضلاً عن الاتفاق.
Just as the umma differed on the ruling of the grave sinner, likewise on his name, after agreeing to call him a fasiq (transgressor). According to us (Ahl al-Sunna) he is a mu'min (believer); according to the Mu'tazila he is neither a mu'min nor a kafir (disbeliever), and they call that 'al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' (the station between the two stations); according to the Khawarij he is a kafir; and according to al-Hasan al-Basri he is a munafiq (hypocrite). We have finished establishing proofs and refuting the Mu'tazila's arguments based on deeds being part of iman (faith). Now we point to refuting the rest of their arguments, and the arguments of the Khawarij, and those who call him munafiq. Among the arguments of the Mu'tazila is what Wasil ibn 'Ata advanced against 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd until he returned to his doctrine: that the umma has agreed that the grave sinner is fasiq, and they disagreed on whether he is mu'min or kafir, so it is necessary to leave the disagreed and take the agreed. The response: this is abandoning the agreed, which is that he is either mu'min or kafir, and there is no intermediate between them, and taking what no one has said, let alone consensus.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage begins with a summary of the differing views on how to name the grave sinner (sahib al-kabirah) after agreeing to call him fasiq. The author lists the positions: Ahl al-Sunna consider him a mu'min, Mu'tazila place him in a middle station (neither mu'min nor kafir), Khawarij deem him kafir, and Hasan al-Basri calls him munafiq. Then the author says they have already refuted arguments based on deeds being part of iman and now turn to remaining arguments. The first Mu'tazili argument from Wasil ibn 'Ata is presented: the umma agrees the grave sinner is fasiq but disagrees on iman/kufr, so we should stick to the agreed term (fasiq) and leave the disputed. The response is that this move actually abandons what is truly agreed upon: that he is either mu'min or kafir with no third option. Instead, they adopt a position (a middle status) that no one holds, let alone has consensus. The argument is structured as a claim from consensus, but the refutation shows it fails because it misidentifies the point of agreement.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
منافق
شبه
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili consensus argument that we should stick to the agreed term 'fasiq' and leave the disputed iman/kufr is logically sound.
Correction: The argument fails because the real consensus is that the grave sinner is either mu'min or kafir, not that we should abandon those categories. The middle station is an innovation, not a consensus.

Checkpoint
What is the first Mu'tazili argument regarding the grave sinner?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) Since the umma agreed he is fasiq but disagreed on iman/kufr, we should stick to the agreed term (fasiq) and leave the disputed.
C) He is a believer.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The consensus that the grave sinner is either a believer or a disbeliever is what truly unites the umma; any attempt to posit a middle status violates this fundamental agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever try to carve out a middle ground in your faith to avoid the full weight of claiming yourself a believer or a disbeliever? What would it cost you to accept that you are either on the path of iman or kufr, with no comfortable third option?
?
Is there a sin in your life where you subtly pretend there is a 'middle station'—that you are not fully obedient, but not fully disobedient either? How does the lesson's rejection of al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn challenge you to take a definitive stand?
?
The Mu'tazila's argument seemed reasonable—taking what is agreed upon—but the refutation shows it deserted the real consensus. When have you settled for a 'least common denominator' in your beliefs to avoid conflict, even though it contradicted the deeper truth you knew?

The Mu'tazili Mixed-Rulings Argument and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents and refutes the second Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazili argument for the middle station and Taftazani's refutation that it assumes the exclusivity of kafir rulings.

Core Idea
Mixed rulings do not prove a middle station unless those rulings are exclusive to kafirs.

The Mu'tazila argued that the fasiq (grave sinner) has a mix of believer and disbeliever rulings, so he must be in an intermediate category. Taftazani presents this argument and immediately points out its flaw: it assumes that the disbeliever's rulings are exclusive to them, which is precisely what Ahl al-Sunna disputes. Let's read the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili argument
Read the first part of the passage which lists the mixed rulings: the fasiq has some rulings of a believer and some rulings of a kafir.
Notice the specific rulings mentioned: life, property, inheritance, marriage, burial versus blame, curse, unfitness for leadership.
2
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that the fasiq has a station between the two stations and is neither mu'min nor kafir.
3
Study Taftazani's response
Taftazani refutes by saying the argument only works if the kafir rulings are exclusive to kafirs and never apply to any believer. That is the point of contention.
Ahl al-Sunna hold that some of those 'kafir rulings' can apply to believers in certain contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن للفاسق بعض أحكام المؤمن المطلق كعصمة الدم والمال والإرث من المسلم، والمناكحة، والغسل، والصلاة عليه، والدفن في مقابر المسلمين. وبعض أحكام الكافر كالذم واللعن، وعدم أهلية الإمامة، والقضاء والشهادة. فيكون له منزلة بين المنزلتين فلا يكون مؤمناً ولا كافراً. والجواب أن هذا إنما يتم لو كان ما جعلتموه أحكام الكافر خواصه التي لا تتجاوزه إلى المؤمن أصلاً، كما في أحكام المؤمن، وهذا نفس المتنازع. فإنها عندنا تهم الكافر وبعض المؤمنين. وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع، وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم، وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل، ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وننفيه عن الفساق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة هذا النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر بالاتفاق، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض، كما يقال في نفي الصفات: أنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الإعراض، وإلا فقد ماؤهم. يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة، وبأن القول بتعدد القديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره.
And among them (the arguments) is that the fasiq has some rulings of the absolute believer, such as inviolability of life and property, inheritance from the Muslim, marriage, washing, prayer over him, and burial in Muslim cemeteries. And some rulings of the disbeliever, such as blame and curse, and unfitness for imama (leadership), judiciary, and testimony. Thus he has a station between the two stations, so he is neither mu'min nor kafir. The response: this only holds if what you have made rulings of the kafir are his exclusive properties that do not extend to the believer at all, just as in the rulings of the believer. And this is the very point of contention. For according to us, they apply to the kafir and also to some believers. And in the speech of the later Mu'tazila there is something that removes the disagreement: they do not deny describing the fasiq with iman in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation) or in the sense of applying rulings, but in the sense of deserving ultimate praise and glorification, which is what we call complete iman (iman kamil), and we consider deeds in it and deny it for the fasiq, so they have a station between the station of this type of iman and the station of kufr by agreement. It is as if it is a return from the doctrine and turning away, as is said in the negation of attributes: we intend what is of the type of turning away, otherwise their water has gone. They explicitly state that the one who neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Sharia, but only according to language, and that the statement that there are multiple eternals (qadim) is kufr without distinction between accident and other.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This is the second Mu'tazili argument. It claims the fasiq has mixed rulings: some like a believer (e.g., inviolability of life, inheritance, marriage, funeral prayers) and some like a kafir (e.g., blame, curse, unfitness for leadership and testimony). Therefore he occupies a middle station and is neither mu'min nor kafir. The refutation points out that this only works if the rulings attributed to the kafir are exclusive to him and never apply to any believer. That is exactly the point of dispute, because according to Ahl al-Sunna, some of these rulings also apply to certain believers. The author then notes that later Mu'tazila have a formulation that might resolve the disagreement: they do not deny calling the fasiq a mu'min in the sense of tasdiq (inward affirmation) or in the sense of applying legal rulings, but only in the sense of deserving ultimate praise and glorification. That type of iman (complete iman) involves deeds and is denied for the fasiq. So by agreement there is a middle position between this type of iman and kufr. However, the author criticizes this as effectively a retreat from their original doctrine, comparing it to the Mu'tazila's position on divine attributes where they say they intend 'turning away' (i'rad) rather than actual negation. Finally, he notes that they explicitly state that the one who fails in obedience is not a believer according to Sharia but only linguistically, and that claiming multiple eternal beings is kufr regardless of whether the 'other' is an accident or something else.

Key Terms
فاسق
مؤمن مطلق
أحكام
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mixed-rulings argument proves the fasiq is neither believer nor disbeliever.
Correction: The argument assumes that the 'kafir rulings' are exclusive to kafirs, but Ahl al-Sunna hold that some rulings like blame and disqualification can apply to believers too. Thus mixed rulings do not necessitate a middle station.

Checkpoint
What is the core flaw Taftazani identifies in the Mu'tazili argument?
A) The fasiq does not actually have any rulings of a kafir.
B) The argument assumes that the rulings of a kafir are exclusive to kafirs.
C) The fasiq is actually a believer despite his sins.
D) The rulings of a believer are not as described.

Key Takeaway
Mixed rulings alone do not prove a middle station; the Mu'tazili argument fails because it assumes the exclusivity of kafir rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on their outward sins? This lesson shows that mixed rulings do not indicate a middle station. How does that challenge your tendency to label people as 'good' or 'bad' Muslims? What would it take for you to stop using such labels and trust Allah's judgment alone?
?
The Mu'tazila argued from the fact that a sinner is blamed and disqualified from leadership that he is not a believer. But this lesson shows that believers can be blamed too. In your own life, when you are blamed or criticized, do you feel your faith is diminished? How can you separate your worth as a believer from your failures?
?
This argument required the Mu'tazila to assume that kafir rulings are exclusive. How often do you make assumptions about people based on isolated behaviors? Can you think of a time you assumed someone was not a true Muslim because of something they did? How would this lesson correct that assumption?

The Later Mu'tazila's Resolution and Its Critique
Taftazani discusses the later Mu'tazila's resolution and criticizes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the later Mu'tazila attempted to resolve the disagreement by redefining the sense of iman, and why Taftazani considers this a retreat from their original position.

Core Idea
The later Mu'tazila admitted iman in the sense of tasdiq but denied 'complete iman' which involves deeds.

After the refutation, Taftazani notes that later Mu'tazila proposed a nuanced position that might remove the disagreement. They distinguish between iman as mere affirmation (tasdiq) and iman as full praise and glorification. However, Taftazani criticizes this as effectively abandoning their original doctrine, similar to their stance on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the later Mu'tazila's position
Read the passage where Taftazani explains that later Mu'tazila do not deny iman in the sense of tasdiq or rulings, but in the sense of deserving utmost praise.
Notice the three senses of iman discussed: tasdiq, rulings, and complete iman.
2
Understand the agreement
They say the fasiq has a station between the station of complete iman and kufr by agreement.
3
Examine Taftazani's critique
Taftazani dismisses this as a retreat and compares it to their position on attributes, where they claim to negate only 'turning away' (i'rad) but not the attribute itself.
The phrase 'otherwise their water has gone' implies their doctrine is diluted.
4
Note the final explicit statements
Later Mu'tazila explicitly say the one who neglects obedience is not a believer by Sharia but by language, and that multiplicity of qadim is kufr always.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن للفاسق بعض أحكام المؤمن المطلق كعصمة الدم والمال والإرث من المسلم، والمناكحة، والغسل، والصلاة عليه، والدفن في مقابر المسلمين. وبعض أحكام الكافر كالذم واللعن، وعدم أهلية الإمامة، والقضاء والشهادة. فيكون له منزلة بين المنزلتين فلا يكون مؤمناً ولا كافراً. والجواب أن هذا إنما يتم لو كان ما جعلتموه أحكام الكافر خواصه التي لا تتجاوزه إلى المؤمن أصلاً، كما في أحكام المؤمن، وهذا نفس المتنازع. فإنها عندنا تهم الكافر وبعض المؤمنين. وفي كلام المتأخرين من المعتزلة ما يرفع النزاع، وذلك أنهم لا ينكرون وصف الفاسق بالإيمان بمعنى التصديق أو بمعنى إجراء الأحكام، بل بمعنى استحقاق غاية المدح والتعظيم، وهو الذي نسميه الإيمان الكامل، ونعتبر فيه الأعمال وننفيه عن الفساق فيكون لهم منزلة بين منزلة هذا النوع من الإيمان وبين منزلة الكفر بالاتفاق، وكأنه رجوع عن المذهب وإعراض، كما يقال في نفي الصفات: أنا نريد ما هو من قبيل الإعراض، وإلا فقد ماؤهم. يصرحون بأن من أخل بالطاعة ليس بمؤمن بحسب الشرع، بل بمجرد اللغة، وبأن القول بتعدد القديم كفر من غير فرق بين العرض وغيره.
And among them (the arguments) is that the fasiq has some rulings of the absolute believer, such as inviolability of life and property, inheritance from the Muslim, marriage, washing, prayer over him, and burial in Muslim cemeteries. And some rulings of the disbeliever, such as blame and curse, and unfitness for imama (leadership), judiciary, and testimony. Thus he has a station between the two stations, so he is neither mu'min nor kafir. The response: this only holds if what you have made rulings of the kafir are his exclusive properties that do not extend to the believer at all, just as in the rulings of the believer. And this is the very point of contention. For according to us, they apply to the kafir and also to some believers. And in the speech of the later Mu'tazila there is something that removes the disagreement: they do not deny describing the fasiq with iman in the sense of tasdiq (affirmation) or in the sense of applying rulings, but in the sense of deserving ultimate praise and glorification, which is what we call complete iman (iman kamil), and we consider deeds in it and deny it for the fasiq, so they have a station between the station of this type of iman and the station of kufr by agreement. It is as if it is a return from the doctrine and turning away, as is said in the negation of attributes: we intend what is of the type of turning away, otherwise their water has gone. They explicitly state that the one who neglects obedience is not a believer according to the Sharia, but only according to language, and that the statement that there are multiple eternals (qadim) is kufr without distinction between accident and other.

Commentary
This is the second Mu'tazili argument. It claims the fasiq has mixed rulings: some like a believer (e.g., inviolability of life, inheritance, marriage, funeral prayers) and some like a kafir (e.g., blame, curse, unfitness for leadership and testimony). Therefore he occupies a middle station and is neither mu'min nor kafir. The refutation points out that this only works if the rulings attributed to the kafir are exclusive to him and never apply to any believer. That is exactly the point of dispute, because according to Ahl al-Sunna, some of these rulings also apply to certain believers. The author then notes that later Mu'tazila have a formulation that might resolve the disagreement: they do not deny calling the fasiq a mu'min in the sense of tasdiq (inward affirmation) or in the sense of applying legal rulings, but only in the sense of deserving ultimate praise and glorification. That type of iman (complete iman) involves deeds and is denied for the fasiq. So by agreement there is a middle position between this type of iman and kufr. However, the author criticizes this as effectively a retreat from their original doctrine, comparing it to the Mu'tazila's position on divine attributes where they say they intend 'turning away' (i'rad) rather than actual negation. Finally, he notes that they explicitly state that the one who fails in obedience is not a believer according to Sharia but only linguistically, and that claiming multiple eternal beings is kufr regardless of whether the 'other' is an accident or something else.

The Khawarij View: Every Sin is Disbelief
Taftazani presents the Khawarij argument that every sin is kufr and cites their Quranic proof texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij position that every sin constitutes kufr and the Quranic verses they cite as evidence.

Core Idea
The Khawarij believed every sin is kufr, citing Quranic verses literally.

The Khawarij, an early sect, held an extreme view on sin and faith. This lesson presents their core argument and the Quranic proof texts they relied upon. Understanding this position helps clarify the broader theological debate about the relationship between sin and disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: position, minor variation, first proof.
2
Identify the Khawarij position
Find the sentence that states the majority view. What does it say about every sin?
The text says: 'كل معصية كفر'.
3
Examine the proof texts
Look at the three Quranic verses cited. Notice how they are used as textual evidence for the claim.
These verses appear to condemn certain actions as kufr.
4
Reflect on the literal vs. interpretative approach
Consider why the Khawarij take these verses at face value, while others interpret them differently.
This distinction is key to understanding the controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخوارج فمذهب جمهورهم إلى أن كل معصية كفر. ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة والكبيرة، وتمسكوا بوجوه: الأول - النصوص الناطقة بكفر العصاة كقوله تعالى: {ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون} وقوله تعالى في تارك الحج: {ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين} وقوله تعالى: {ومن كفر بعد ذلك فأولئك هم الفاسقون}
As for the Khawarij, the doctrine of the majority of them is that every sin is kufr. And among them are those who differentiate between the minor and the major. They hold onto arguments: the first – the texts that speak of the kufr of sinners, such as His saying (Exalted is He): 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' (5:44), and His saying concerning the one who abandons Hajj: 'And whoever disbelieves – then indeed Allah is free from need of the worlds' (3:97), and His saying: 'And whoever disbelieves after that – then it is those who are the defiantly disobedient' (24:55).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the Khawarij. The majority of them hold that every sin (ma'siya) is kufr (disbelief). Some among them differentiate between minor and major sins. They support their position with arguments (wujuh). The first argument is the scriptural texts that explicitly call sinners disbelievers. Three Quranic verses are cited: (1) 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – they are the disbelievers' (5:44); (2) concerning the one who abandons Hajj: 'Whoever disbelieves – Allah is free from need of the worlds' (3:97); (3) 'Whoever disbelieves after that – they are the defiantly disobedient' (24:55). The text ends here, and the author's refutation presumably follows on the next pages. This lesson merely presents the Khawarij argument and their proof texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verses cited by the Khawarij prove that every sin is kufr.
Correction: These verses are taken literally by the Khawarij, but mainstream scholars interpret them as referring to specific contexts or types of disbelief (e.g., rejecting Allah's judgment entirely, not mere sin).

Checkpoint

B) Only major sins are kufr.
C) Only sins related to shirk are kufr.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij argued that any sin, regardless of size, is an act of disbelief, based on a literal reading of certain Quranic verses.

Refutation: Fasiq is not Kafir by Contrast Alone
Refuting the argument that fasiq is kafir based on contrast with mutaqi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic contrast between disbelievers and the God-fearing does not automatically place the fasiq in the category of kafir.

Core Idea
A scriptural contrast does not always exclude a middle category.

This lesson tackles a fourth argument used by some to claim that a grave sinner (fasiq) is a disbeliever. The argument relies on a Quranic juxtaposition of disbelievers and the God-fearing. The refutation shows a logical flaw: pairing two groups does not prove the absence of a third.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: argument followed by refutation.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument: 1) The Quran contrasts disbelievers with the God-fearing with no third. 2) The fasiq is not God-fearing. 3) Therefore, the fasiq is a disbeliever. Notice how the refutation challenges step 1.
The key is the claim 'no third category'.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that the pairing does not prove the exclusion of a third category. The verses do not explicitly say there is no intermediate status. Thus, the fasiq could be neither kafir nor muttaqi.
This is a logical critique: absence of mention is not proof of absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - ما يدل على كون الكافر في مقابلة المتقي من غير ثالث. ولا شك أن الفاسق ليس بمتقِ (۱) فيكون كافراً، وذلك قوله تعالى : (وسيق الذين كفروا إلى جهنم زمراً ... إلى قوله : ( وسيق الذين اتقوا ربهم إلى الجنة زمراً). قلنا لا دلالة على نفي قسم ثالث
Fourth: What indicates that the disbeliever is juxtaposed to the God-fearing with no third. There is no doubt that the sinner (fāsiq) is not God-fearing, so he is a disbeliever. That is His statement, the Exalted: 'And those who disbelieved will be driven to Hell in groups...' up to His statement: 'And those who feared their Lord will be driven to Paradise in groups.' We said: There is no indication of the negation of a third category.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a fourth argument for the Mu'tazilite position that the fasiq (grave sinner) is a kafir. The argument is based on a scriptural juxtaposition: the Qur'an contrasts the disbelievers (kuffār) with the God-fearing (muttaqūn), with no mention of a third category. Since the fasiq is not God-fearing, he must fall into the category of disbelievers. The refutation (qulnā) denies that the pairing necessarily excludes an intermediate category; the verses do not explicitly negate a third status, such as a fasiq who is neither kafir nor muttaqi. Thus the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq)
كافر (kāfir)
متقي (muttāqī)
مقابلة (juxtaposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the Quran contrasts two groups without mentioning a third, then no third group exists.
Correction: The Quran does not always intend an exhaustive classification; a contrast may simply highlight two ends of a spectrum without excluding intermediate states.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation deny the conclusion of the fourth argument?
A) Because the fasiq could still be a believer.
B) Because the verses do not explicitly negate a third category.
C) Because the fasiq is actually a muttaqi.
D) Because the argument relies on weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic contrast between disbelievers and the God-fearing does not necessarily imply a third category is absent; the fasiq may occupy an intermediate status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that the Quranic descriptions of 'believers' and 'disbelievers' exhaust all possible categories, and that anyone not fitting the 'God-fearing' mold must be outside the faith? Knowing now that a fasiq can be a believer, how does that change the way you view Muslims who commit major sins? Are you willing to withhold judgment and leave their ultimate status to Allah?
?
Consider your own life: Are there sins you commit that you think are 'minor' yet you still consider yourself a believer? This lesson teaches that a fasiq (grave sinner) does not automatically become a kafir. How does this affect your self-assessment? Do you sometimes fear that your own sins have removed you from faith, or do you use this teaching to slide into complacency? Commit to doing tawbah for a specific sin while still affirming your faith.
?
The refutation in this passage is a logical one: absence of mention does not prove non-existence. In your daily interactions, do you sometimes make similar leaps—assuming that because something is not said, it is not true? For example, when a fellow Muslim does not display visible piety, do you quickly categorize them as 'not serious' about faith? How can you apply the lesson's logic to avoid unjust assumptions about others' imaan?

Answering the Objection: Not All Self-Professed Non-Believers Are Disbelievers
Answering the objection that the fasiq who considers himself not a believer is a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response of denying the major premise refutes the argument that a fasiq who considers himself not a believer is a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Self-identification as non-believer does not automatically make one a kafir; the major premise is denied.

This lesson examines a concise objection-and-answer exchange. The objector argues that since the fasiq himself admits he is not a believer legally, he must be a disbeliever. The answer simply denies the major premise — not everyone who says 'I am not a believer' is a kafir. This highlights a key maneuver in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice its logical structure: the fasiq believes he is not a believer (minor premise) and everyone like that is a kafir (major premise).
2
Identify the objector's logic
The objector uses a syllogism: minor premise (the fasiq's belief) + major premise (all such people are kafir) → conclusion (the fasiq is kafir). Understand this form.
This is a standard deductive argument.
3
Examine the answer
The answer is simply 'man‘ al-kubrā' (denial of the major premise). The scholar rejects the universal claim that anyone who believes themselves not a believer is a kafir.
The denial is not explained further here, but it rests on the distinction between legal and actual faith.
4
Connect to broader teachings
This passage shows that in kalam, objections are often refuted by denying a premise rather than accepting the conclusion. It also ties to the concept that sin does not nullify faith entirely.
The fasiq may still be a believer in the sense of possessing faith in the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هو يعتقد أنه ليس بمؤمن شرعاً، وكل من كان كذلك فهو كافر. أجيب بمنع الكبرى.
For if it is said: He believes that he is not a believer (mu'min) legally, and everyone who is like that is a disbeliever (kafir). It is answered by denying the major premise.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This short passage is an objection and its answer. The objector argues: the fasiq himself acknowledges he is not a believer in the legal sense, so he must be a kafir. The answer simply denies the major premise: 'everyone who believes he is not a believer is a kafir' is not accepted. The fasiq might still be a believer in some sense (e.g., faith in the heart) despite his sin. The response is terse and relies on the broader theological position that sin does not remove faith.

Key Terms
مؤمن شرعاً (believer legally)
منع الكبرى (denial of the major premise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person says or believes they are not a believer, they must be a disbeliever (kafir).
Correction: The passage shows that the major premise 'everyone who believes he is not a believer is a kafir' is not accepted; there can be exceptions, such as a fasiq who still has faith in the heart.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection?
A) It is accepted.
B) The major premise is denied.
C) The minor premise is denied.
D) The fasiq is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a fasiq who considers himself not a believer is a kafir is refuted by denying the universal major premise, not by challenging the minor premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on their own admission that they are not a believer? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that judgment? Will you now pause before labeling a person as a disbeliever, even if they say so themselves?
?
When you have committed a major sin, did you feel you had lost your faith entirely? This passage shows that self-assessment can be wrong regarding legal definitions of belief. How does that change your view of your own faith after sin? Are you willing to hold onto hope that faith remains despite feelings of unworthiness?
?
The objector assumed a universal claim that anyone who thinks they are not a believer is a kafir. Do you hold any similar blanket assumptions about faith and disbelief? What would it cost you to examine and possibly discard such assumptions in light of this teaching?

Refuting the Argument that Fasiq Despairs of Mercy
Refuting the argument that fasiq is kafir because he despairs of God's mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation denies the minor premise of the fifth argument and why the fasiq is not necessarily a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Despair is not automatically assumed; hope can coexist with sin.

This lesson examines the fifth argument that the fasiq is a kafir because he despairs of God's mercy, citing Quran 12:87. The refutation denies that the fasiq necessarily despairs, offering possibilities of hope for forgiveness or divine enablement for repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the argument is presented, then the refutation begins with 'قلنا' (We said).
The Quranic verse is a key proof for the argument.
2
Identify the Minor Premise
The argument claims the fasiq despairs, which is the minor premise. The refutation denies this ('الصغرى ممنوعة').
This is the main move in the refutation.
3
Understand the Counter-Objection
The refutation also preempts an objection against the Mu'tazila by stating that even if they do not believe in forgiveness, they still hope for divine enablement for repentance.
This strengthens the refutation by showing it is not a special pleading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - أن الفاسق آيس من روح الله . وكل من هو كذلك فهو كافر لقوله تعالى: (لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون). قلنا: الصغرى ممنوعة فإنه ربما يرجو العفو من الله تعالى، أو التوبة من نفسه، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إن العاصي من المعتزلة يلزم ان يكون كافراً لكونه آيساً، فإنه وإن لم يعتقد العفو فليس بأيس من توفيق التوبة .
Fifth: That the sinner (fāsiq) despairs of the Spirit of God. And everyone who is like that is a disbeliever, because of His statement, the Exalted: 'None despairs of the Spirit of God except the disbelieving people.' We said: The minor premise is denied, for perhaps he hopes for forgiveness from God the Exalted, or repentance from himself. And with this is repelled what is said: That the disobedient person among the Mu'tazila necessarily becomes a disbeliever because he is despairing; for even if he does not believe in forgiveness, he is not despairing of being granted repentance.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a fifth argument: the fasiq is a kafir because he despairs of God's mercy (rawḥ Allāh), citing the verse 'None despairs of the Spirit of God except the disbelieving people' (Q 12:87). The refutation denies the minor premise of the syllogism: it is not established that the fasiq despairs of mercy. He may still hope for forgiveness or for the ability to repent. This also counters a potential objection against the Mu'tazila themselves: even if a Mu'tazilī does not believe in God's forgiveness without repentance, he does not despair of being granted tawfīq (divine enablement) for repentance. Therefore the argument fails.

Key Terms
آيس (despairing)
روح الله (Spirit of God)
يأس (despair)
توفيق التوبة (divine enablement for repentance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a fasiq necessarily despairs of God's mercy, making him a disbeliever.
Correction: The refutation shows that the fasiq may still hope for forgiveness or for divine enablement to repent, so despair is not necessarily present.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation deny the minor premise of the fifth argument?
A) By arguing that the fasiq never despairs.
B) By stating that the fasiq might hope for forgiveness or repentance.
C) By quoting a different Quranic verse.
D) By claiming that despair is not a sin.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq is not necessarily a disbeliever because despair is not established; he may hope for mercy or repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin gravely, do you truly believe that God's mercy is available to you immediately, or do you feel a sense of despair that pushes you further from Him? How can you actively remind yourself of hope for forgiveness today?
?
If you were to die tonight while in a state of disobedience, would you trust that God’s mercy could still reach you? What specific action can you take right now to turn towards repentance, not relying solely on future hope?
?
The passage shows that even a Mu'tazilī who does not believe in forgiveness without repentance can still hope for tawfīq. Do you ever feel that your sins are too great for God to forgive unless you first become perfect? How can you cultivate hope in God’s mercy without waiting for a guarantee of repentance?

Refutation of Arguments that the Fasiq is a Munafiq
Refuting both rational and transmitted arguments that the fasiq is a munafiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational and transmitted arguments that the fasiq is a munafiq are refuted, and why the fasiq's state is opposite to the hypocrite.

Core Idea
A sinner is not a hypocrite; hope and pleasure explain sinful actions.

This passage presents two proofs used by those who claim that a committer of major sins (fasiq) is a hypocrite (munafiq): a rational analogy and a Prophetic hadith. It then provides clear refutations for each, showing that the fasiq's situation is fundamentally different from the munafiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense with two proofs and two responses.
2
Identify the two proofs
Notice the two proofs: the rational proof (snake hole analogy) and the transmitted proof (hadith of the three signs).
The rational proof is an analogy; the transmitted proof is a text.
3
Understand the refutation of the rational proof
The refutation argues that the fasiq may hope for mercy, seek repentance, or be distracted by immediate pleasure, unlike the snake hole example where action clearly contradicts belief.
The key is that mitigating factors prevent automatic hypocrisy.
4
Understand the refutation of the transmitted proof
The hadith is not taken literally, as breaking a promise does not always indicate religious hypocrisy. The fasiq's state is actually opposite: he hides good deeds and shows bad deeds.
The munafiq shows good deeds and hides bad deeds.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the fasiq is not a munafiq; his condition is the reverse of hypocrisy. Reflect on how this clarifies the relationship between sin and faith.
This ties back to the broader discussion on faith and sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بكون الفاسق منافقاً فتمسكوا بوجهين : عقلي، وهو أن إقدامه على المعصية المفضية إلى العذاب يدل على أنه كاذب في دعوى تصديقه بما جاء به النبي (عليه السلام) كمن ادعى أنه يعتقد في هذا الجحر حية ، ثم يدخل فيها يده. ونقلي، وهو قوله (عليه السلام) «آية المنافق ثلاث : إذا وعد أخلف، وإذا حدث كذب وإذا ائتمن خان». والجواب عن الأول أنه وإن كان يخاف العذاب، لكن يرجو الرحمة، ويأمل توفيق التوبة، أو يلهيه عن أجل العقوبة عاجل اللذة بخلاف حديث الجحر والحية . وعن الثاني بأنه مع كونه من الأحاد ليس على ظاهره وفاقاً للقطع بأن من وعد غيره عدة ثم أخلفها لم يكن منافقاً في الدين، وإذا تأملت، فحال الفاسق على عكس حال المنافق، لأنه يضمر حسناته ويظهر سيئاته .
As for those who hold that the sinner (fāsiq) is a hypocrite (munāfiq), they cling to two proofs: rational, and it is that his committing a sin that leads to punishment indicates that he is lying in his claim of believing in what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought, like one who claims that he believes there is a snake in this hole, then inserts his hand into it. And transmitted, which is his statement (peace be upon him): 'The sign of the hypocrite is three: when he promises he breaks, when he speaks he lies, and when he is entrusted he betrays.' And the answer to the first is that although he fears punishment, he nevertheless hopes for mercy, and hopes for the divine enablement of repentance, or the immediate pleasure distracts him from the term of punishment, unlike the story of the hole and the snake. And as for the second, despite it being from the hadiths, it is not taken literally, in agreement with the certainty that one who promises another a promise and then breaks it is not a hypocrite in religion. And if you reflect, the state of the sinner (fāsiq) is the opposite of the hypocrite, because he conceals his good deeds and reveals his evil deeds.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two proofs for the claim that the fasiq is a munafiq (hypocrite). The first is a rational analogy: a person who claims to believe but commits sins leading to punishment is like someone who claims there is a snake in a hole and then sticks his hand in – his action contradicts his claim, proving hypocrisy. The second is a transmitted proof: the Prophetic hadith that the signs of a hypocrite are breaking promises, lying, and betraying trust; the fasiq often exhibits these. The refutation of the rational proof argues that the fasiq may still hope for mercy or repentance, or be overwhelmed by the immediate pleasure, unlike the clear self-contradiction of the snake hole example. The refutation of the transmitted proof argues that the hadith is not to be taken literally, since breaking a promise does not always indicate religious hypocrisy. Finally, the author notes that the fasiq's condition is actually opposite to the munafiq: the fasiq hides his good deeds and shows his sins, whereas a munafiq does the reverse. Thus the fasiq is not a munafiq.

Key Terms
منافق (munāfiq)
عقلي (rational)
نقلي (transmitted)
آية المنافق (sign of the hypocrite)
توفيق التوبة (divine enablement for repentance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing a sin automatically proves hypocrisy because the action contradicts belief.
Correction: The passage explains that hope for mercy, divine enablement for repentance, or being overcome by immediate pleasure can explain sin without indicating hypocrisy.

Checkpoint
What is one reason given to refute the rational proof that the fasiq is a munafiq?
A) The fasiq has no fear of punishment.
B) The fasiq may hope for mercy or be distracted by pleasure.
C) The fasiq openly admits his sin.
D) The fasiq always repents.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq is not a munafiq; his condition is the opposite of the hypocrite's, as he conceals good deeds and reveals evil deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a sin, do you secretly judge them as a hypocrite? How does this lesson's refutation of that assumption challenge you to change your inner attitude toward them? Will you actively replace judgment with hope for their repentance?
?
Think of a specific sin you committed while knowing it was wrong. Were you distracted by immediate pleasure, or did you hope for Allah's mercy? How does that experience show the truth of this passage? What will you do differently to strengthen your fear of Allah without losing hope?
?
The fasiq conceals his good deeds and reveals his evil deeds; the munafiq does the opposite. Honestly examine yourself: Do you hide your good deeds out of humility or show them off for praise? Do you hide your sins or openly commit them? What does your pattern reveal about your heart, and what step will you take today to become more like a fasiq in concealing good and revealing evil out of shame?

The Majority View: Islam and Iman Are Synonymous
The author states the majority view and supports it with consensus and three verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position that Islam and Iman are synonymous in meaning, along with the evidence from consensus and three Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are two names for the same reality: acceptance and submission.

This passage from al-Maqasid introduces a key debate in Islamic theology: the relationship between Islam and Iman. The majority of scholars hold that the two terms are synonymous, meaning both refer to the inner acceptance and outward submission to God. The author presents their evidence from consensus and three Quranic verses, which we will examine in this lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author states the majority view and then immediately provides evidence.
2
Identify the majority view and its definition
The majority says Islam and Iman are 'one' (wāḥid) meaning they both refer to acceptance (qubūl) and submission (idh‘ān). Understand that this implies every believer is a Muslim and vice versa.
This includes name, ruling, and abode in the afterlife.
3
Examine the three Quranic proofs
Go through each verse: Q 3:85, Q 51:35-36, Q 49:17. For each, ask how it supports the synonymity. Note the connection between Islam and faith in each.
The third verse is subtle: it distinguishes Islam from guidance to faith, but still calls them Muslims.
4
Understand the role of consensus
The author cites consensus (ijmā‘) as the first evidence. Why would consensus be a strong proof in this context?
Consensus reflects the collective understanding of scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني في الإسلام - الجمهور على أن الإسلام والإيمان واحد بمعنى رجوعهما إلى القبول والإذعان، وكسون كل مؤمن مسلماً، والعكس في حق الاسم، والحكم، والدار للإجماع على ذلك ولشهادة النصوص مثل : (ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه) مع أن الإيمان مقبول وفاقاً ومثل : قوله تعالى : (فأخرجنا من كان فيها من المؤمنين فما وجدنا فيها غير بيت من المسلمين) ومثل : (قل لا تمنوا عليّ إسلامكم بل الله يمن عليكم أن هداكم للإيمان). احتج المخالف بتفارقهما لقوله تعالى : (قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا) وتعاطفهما كقوله تعالى : (إن المسلمين والمسلمات والمؤمنين والمؤمنات) وقوله تعالى : (فما زادوهم إلا إيماناً وتسليماً) وتخالفهما في البيان بعد الاستفسار كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله .. إلى آخره، والإسلام أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله .. إلى آخره
He said: The second investigation concerning Islam - The majority (jumhūr) holds that Islam and Faith (īmān) are one, meaning that both refer to acceptance and submission. So every believer (mu'min) is a Muslim, and the reverse in terms of the name, the ruling, and the abode, due to consensus on that and the testimony of texts such as: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Q 3:85) while faith is accepted by agreement; and such as His statement, the Exalted: 'Then We brought out from them whoever was of the believers, but We found not there other than one house of Muslims' (Q 51:35-36); and such as: 'Say: Do not confer favor upon me by your Islam; rather, Allah confers favor upon you that He has guided you to the faith' (Q 49:17). The opponent argued based on their being different by His statement, the Exalted: 'Say: You have not believed, but say, We have submitted' (Q 49:14); and their being coupled, as in His statement: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, and the believing men and believing women' (Q 33:35); and His statement: 'And it only increased them in faith and submission' (Q 33:22); and their being distinguished in explanation after inquiry, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Faith is that you believe in Allah...' etc., and 'Islam is that you testify that there is no god but Allah...' etc.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage begins a new section (al-mabḥath al-thānī fī al-islām) discussing the relationship between Islam and īmān (faith). The majority view is that the two terms are synonymous, both meaning acceptance (qubūl) and submission (idh‘ān). This implies that every believer is a Muslim and vice versa in terms of name, religious ruling, and final abode in the afterlife. The author cites several pieces of evidence: firstly, consensus (ijmā‘); secondly, texts such as Q 3:85 (Islam is the only accepted religion) combined with the fact that īmān is also accepted universally; thirdly, Q 51:35-36 where 'believers' and 'Muslims' are used interchangeably; and fourthly, Q 49:17 where God says He guided them to īmān, implying that their Islam (outward submission) is not the ultimate goal, but they are still called Muslims. The opponents of this view argue that the terms are distinct because of Q 49:14 (the bedouins say they believe but are told to say they submit), Q 33:35 which couples Muslims and believers as distinct groups, Q 33:22 which mentions both īmān and taslīm (submission) as separate increases, and the famous hadith of Jibril which defines īmān and Islam separately. The passage sets up the debate, leaving the author's response for later.

Key Terms
إسلام (islām)
إيمان (īmān)
جمهور (jumhūr)
قبول (qubūl)
إذعان (idh‘ān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Islam and Iman are always distinct because other texts separate them.
Correction: The majority view argues they are synonymous, and the three verses here support that. Other texts are interpreted differently.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning to which both Islam and Iman refer according to the majority?
A) Outward actions only
B) Acceptance and submission
C) Inner belief without action
D) Specific rituals

Key Takeaway
The majority holds that Islam and Iman are synonymous, both meaning acceptance and submission, supported by consensus and three Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a Muslim, do you also feel the weight of being a mu'min? Have you ever considered that your external actions (Islam) might be disconnected from your internal acceptance (Iman)? What would it take to make them one reality in your life?
?
The majority view insists that every believer is a Muslim and vice versa. But examine your heart: Are there areas where you submit outwardly (you pray, fast, etc.) but inside you resist or question? How can you align your inner submission with your outer practice starting today?
?
Allah reminds the Bedouins not to boast about their Islam, but to recognize that He guided them to faith. When was the last time you caught yourself feeling proud of your religious acts? How can you shift your gratitude from your actions to Allah's guidance?

Opposing Arguments: Are Islam and Iman Distinct?
The author lists the opponent's arguments from Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments of those who distinguish between Islam and Iman, based on Quranic verses and the hadith of Jibril.

Core Idea
Opponents use separation, coupling, increase, and distinct definitions to argue that Islam and Iman are not synonymous.

Not all scholars agree on the synonymity of Islam and Iman. The opponents present strong evidence from the Quran and hadith that seem to differentiate the two. This lesson examines their arguments as presented in the passage, setting the stage for the majority's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection section
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'احتج المخالف' and its translation. Identify the four distinct arguments.
2
Analyze the argument from separation
Q 49:14 tells the Bedouins not to claim belief but only submission. This suggests that Islam (submission) can exist without Iman (belief).
3
Examine the argument from coupling
Q 33:35 lists Muslims and believers separately, implying they are distinct groups.
4
Consider the argument from increase
Q 33:22 mentions an increase in both faith and submission, indicating two different things that can be increased.
5
Reflect on the hadith of Jibril
The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave separate definitions for Iman and Islam in response to a question, showing they are distinct concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The majority view is that the two terms are synonymous, both meaning acceptance (qubūl) and submission (idh‘ān). This implies that every believer is a Muslim and vice versa in terms of name, religious ruling, and final abode in the afterlife. The author cites several pieces of evidence: firstly, consensus (ijmā‘); secondly, texts such as Q 3:85 (Islam is the only accepted religion) combined with the fact that īmān is also accepted universally; thirdly, Q 51:35-36 where 'believers' and 'Muslims' are used interchangeably; and fourthly, Q 49:17 where God says He guided them to īmān, implying that their Islam (outward submission) is not the ultimate goal, but they are still called Muslims. The Majority View: Islam and Iman Are One
Al-Taftazani presents the majority view and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position that Islam and Iman are identical, and how they respond to textual objections that seem to differentiate them.

Core Idea
The majority holds that Islam and Iman are one in meaning and ruling.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating the position of the majority (al-jumhur) that Islam and iman are identical, both referring to acceptance and submission. He provides Quranic evidence and then addresses objections from those who cite verses and hadith that seem to distinguish between the two. This lesson examines the initial claim and the scholarly response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the majority claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the majority explicitly says Islam and Iman are one, meaning both are acceptance and submission.
Pay attention to the terms 'qabūl' and 'idh‘ān'.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the three verses cited. How do they show the unity of Islam and Iman?
The verses use both terms interchangeably or imply that one entails the other.
3
Understand the objection and the response
The opponent cites verses and a hadith that seem to differentiate. The response explains that the difference is in concept, not in reality.
The response 'qulnā' is key: it says there is no dispute about applying 'Islam' to outward surrender, and that conceptual difference does not prevent coupling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في الإسلام - الجمهور على أن الإسلام والإيمان واحد بمعنى رجوعهما إلى القبول والإذعان، وكسون كل مؤمن مسلماً، والعكس في حق الاسم، والحكم، والدار للإجماع على ذلك ولشهادة النصوص مثل : ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه مع أن الإيمان مقبول وفاقاً ومثل : قوله تعالى : ( فأخرجنا من كان فيها من المؤمنين فما وجدنا فيها غير بيت من المسلمين ) ومثل : قل لا تمنوا عليّ إسلامكم بل الله يمن عليكم أن هداكم للإيمان ). احتج المخالف بتفارقهما لقوله تعالى : قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا وتعاطفهما كقوله تعالى : إن المسلمين والمسلمات والمؤمنين والمؤمنات وقوله تعالى : ( فما زادوهم إلا إيماناً وتسليماً ) وتخالفهما في البيان بعد الاستفسار كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله .. إلى آخره، والإسلام أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله .. إلى آخره قلنا : لا نزاع في إطلاقه على الاستسلام والانقياد الظاهر، وتغاير المفهموم كافٍ في صحة العطف، وفي الحديث بيان لمتعلق الإيمان، وشرائع الإسلام، وقد ورد مثله في الإيمان الجمهور على أن الإسلام والإيمان واحد إذ معنى آمنت بمـا جـاء بـه : النبي عليه السلام) صدقته. ومعنى أسلمت له سلمته . ولا يظهر بينهما كثير فرق لرجوعهما إلى معنى الاعتراف والانقياد والإذعان والقبول. وبالجملة لا يعقل بحسب الشرع مؤمن ليس بمسلم أو مسلم ليس بمؤمن، وهذا مراد القوم بترادف الاسمين واتحاد المعنى وعدم التغاير على ما قال في التبصرة: الاسمان من قبيل الأسماء المترادفة وكل مؤمن مسلم، وكل مسلم مؤمن، لأن الإيمان اسم لتصديق شهادة العقول والآثار على وحدانية الله تعالى، وأن له الخلق والأمر ، لا شريك له في ذلك . والإسلام إسلام المرء نفسه بكليتها الله تعالى بالعبودية له ، من غير شرك، فحصلا من طريق المراد منهما على معنى واحد . ولو كان الاسمان متغايرين لتصور وجود أحدهما بدون الآخر، ولتصور مؤمن ليس بمسلم ، أو مسلم ليس بمؤمن، فيكون لأحدهما في الدنيا أو الآخرة حكم ليس للآخر، وهذا باطل قطعا. وقال في الكفاية : الإيمان هو تصديق الله فيما أخبر من أوامره ونواهيه والإسلام هو الانقياد والخضوع لألوهيته. وذا لا يتحقق إلا بقبول الأمر والنهي . فالإيمان لا ينفك عن الإسلام حكماً، فلا يتغايران، وإذا كان المراد بالاتحاد هذا المعنى، صح التمسك فيه بالإجماع على أنه يمتنع أن يأتي ما أعتبر في الإيمان ولا يكون مسلماً أو بجميع ما اعتبر في الإسلام ولا بجميع يكون مؤمناً. وعلى أنه ليس للمؤمن حكم لا يكون للمسلم، وبالعكس. وعلى أن دار أحد
The second section on Islam. The majority (al-jumhūr) hold that Islam and faith (īmān) are one, meaning both revert to acceptance (al-qabūl) and submission (al-idh‘ān). And they call every believer (mu’min) a Muslim, and vice versa, regarding the name, the ruling, and the abode, due to consensus (ijmā‘) on that and due to the testimony of texts such as: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' [Āl ‘Imrān 3:85] – while faith is accepted by agreement – and such as His statement: 'So We brought out whoever was in it of the believers, but We found not there any house of Muslims' [al-Dhāriyāt 51:36], and such as: 'Do not consider your Islam a favor to me. Rather, Allah has conferred favor upon you that He has guided you to the faith' [al-Ḥujurāt 49:17]. The opponent argued by their differentiation due to His statement: 'Say, you have not [truly] believed, but say, 'We have submitted' [al-Ḥujurāt 49:14] and their being coupled like His statement: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, the believing men and believing women' [al-Aḥzāb 33:35] and His statement: 'And it only increased them in faith and submission' [al-Aḥzāb 33:22] and their difference in the explanation after clarification like the Prophet’s saying: 'Faith is to believe in Allah...' and 'Islam is to testify that there is no god but Allah...' We say: There is no dispute about it being applied to outward surrender (al-istislām) and compliance (al-inqiyād). And the difference in concept (al-mafhūm) is sufficient for the validity of coupling. And in the hadith is an explanation of the object of faith and the laws of Islam. And similar has been reported about faith. The majority hold that Islam and faith are one, for the meaning of 'I believed in what the Prophet brought' is 'I affirmed his truthfulness' (ṣaddaqtuhu) and the meaning of 'I submitted to him' is 'I surrendered to him' (sallamtuhu). And there does not appear between them much difference because they both revert to the meaning of acknowledgment (al-i‘tirāf), compliance (al-inqiyād), submission (al-idh‘ān), and acceptance (al-qabūl). In short, it is not rationally conceivable, according to the Law, a believer who is not a Muslim or a Muslim who is not a believer. And this is what the people mean by the synonymity (tarāduf) of the two names and the unity of meaning and the lack of differentiation, according to what is said in al-Tabṣira: 'The two names are of the category of synonymous names (al-asmā’ al-mutarādifa). Every believer is a Muslim, and every Muslim is a believer, because faith is a name for affirming the testimony of intellects (al-‘uqūl) and transmitted proofs (al-āthār) on the oneness of Allah Most High, and that to Him belongs creation and command, no partner has He in that. And Islam is a person's surrendering of his entire self to Allah Most High in servitude to Him, without shirk. So they both result, from the way of intended meaning, in one meaning. And if the two names were different, it would be conceivable for one to exist without the other, and it would be conceivable for a believer not to be a Muslim, or a Muslim not to be a believer. Then there would be for one of them in this world or the Hereafter a ruling not for the other, and this is absolutely false (bāṭil qaṭ‘an).' And it is said in al-Kifāya: 'Faith is affirming Allah (taṣdīq Allāh) in what He informed of His commands and prohibitions, and Islam is compliance (al-inqiyād) and humility (al-khushū‘) to His divinity. And this is not realized except by acceptance of the command and prohibition. So faith does not separate from Islam in terms of ruling, so they are not different.' And if what is meant by unity is this meaning, then it is valid to adhere in it to the consensus that it is impossible for one to come with what is considered in faith and not be a Muslim, or with all that is considered in Islam and not be a believer. And that there is no ruling for a believer that is not for a Muslim, and vice versa. And that the abode of one...
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the relationship between Islam and Iman (faith). It begins by stating the position of the majority (al-jumhūr): Islam and Iman are the same, both returning to the meanings of acceptance (qabūl) and submission (idh‘ān). They consider every believer a Muslim and vice versa in terms of name, ruling, and final abode. This is based on consensus and textual evidence, including Quranic verses that use both terms interchangeably and imply their unity. For example, verse 3:85 states that only Islam is accepted, yet Iman is also accepted, indicating they are the same; verse 51:36 uses 'believers' and 'Muslims' in parallel; verse 49:17 mentions guidance to Iman as a favor after they have already entered Islam. The passage then presents the objection of the opponent, who adduces verses and hadith that seem to differentiate between Iman and Islam, such as verse 49:14 ('Say, you have not believed, but say, we have submitted'), coupling of terms in 33:35, and the hadith of Gabriel that distinguishes between Iman and Islam. The response (qulnā) clarifies that there is no dispute about the outward application of Islam to surrender, and that the difference in conceptual definition is sufficient for the validity of coupling in speech. The hadith itself explains the objects of Iman and the laws of Islam, not a real distinction. The author then reasserts the majority view, explaining the meanings: 'I believed' means 'I affirmed truthfulness' and 'I submitted' means 'I surrendered.' Both revert to acknowledgment, compliance, and acceptance. Consequently, it is inconceivable in Islamic law to have a believer who is not a Muslim or vice versa. This is what scholars mean by synonymity. The passage quotes al-Tabṣira for a more detailed definition: Iman is affirmation of the testimony of intellects and transmitted proofs concerning God's oneness, while Islam is surrendering one's whole self to God in servitude without shirk. Both lead to the same meaning. If they were different, one could conceive of a believer without Islam or a Muslim without Iman, which would entail different rulings in this world or the afterlife—a clearly false conclusion. The passage also cites al-Kifāya, where Iman is defined as affirming God's commands and prohibitions, and Islam as compliance and humility to His divinity; the two are inseparable in ruling. Finally, it argues that if by 'unity' we mean this inseparability, then we can rely on consensus that someone who fulfills the conditions of Iman cannot be other than a Muslim, and vice versa, and there is no ruling specific to one that does not apply to the other.

Key Terms

إيمان
الجمهور
قبول
إذعان
ترادف
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the hadith of Gabriel proves Islam and Iman are separate and that a person can be a Muslim without faith.
Correction: The passage explains that the hadith clarifies different aspects (objects of faith vs. practices) and that the conceptual distinction does not imply separate realities.

Checkpoint
What is the majority view on the relationship between Islam and Iman according to this passage?
A) They are completely different and mutually exclusive.
B) They are one, both meaning acceptance and submission.
C) Islam is outward actions, Iman is inward belief, and a person can have one without the other.
D) Iman is superior to Islam, and only believers will enter Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The majority view is that Islam and Iman are one in meaning and ruling; apparent textual differences are reconciled by understanding conceptual distinctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform your daily prayers and outward acts of worship, do you sense your heart fully surrendered to Allah, or is there a disconnect? What specific step will you take today to align your inner Iman with your outer Islam, knowing that they are meant to be one?
?
The verse 'قل لا تمنوا عليّ إسلامكم' warns against seeing your Islam as a favor to Allah. In your relationship with Allah, do you ever feel that your obedience puts Him in your debt? How can this lesson's emphasis on submission and acceptance cultivate humility in your heart?
?
If someone were to observe your actions for a month, would they see the same level of commitment in your private devotion as in your public appearance? What one hidden practice will you recommit to today, seeking to make your faith a true inner reality?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
7 min
The Definitional Unity of Islam and Iman
Al-Taftazani defines Iman and Islam, proves their unity via absurdity, and cites consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitional arguments for the unity of Islam and Iman, including the proof by absurdity and the consensus that no ruling distinguishes them.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman both mean surrender and affirmation, making separation inconceivable.

After addressing objections, al-Taftazani deepens the argument by defining the terms: 'Iman' is tasdiq (affirmation) and 'Islam' is taslim (surrender). He shows they lead to the same meaning and argues that if they were different, it would be possible to have one without the other, which is absurd. He then cites al-Tabṣira and al-Kifāya to reinforce the point, and concludes with consensus that there is no ruling for a believer that is not for a Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of Iman and Islam
Notice that Iman is defined as tasdiq (affirming truthfulness) and Islam as taslim (surrender). Both lead to the same meaning of acknowledgment and submission.
The passage says: 'لا يظهر بينهما كثير فرق' (there is not much difference).
2
Understand the proof by absurdity
If the two were different, one could exist without the other, leading to different rulings in this world and the Hereafter. This is rejected as absolutely false.
The phrase 'وهذا باطل قطعا' emphasizes the certainty.
3
Examine the consensus argument
Al-Kifāya states that Iman and Islam are inseparable in ruling, and the consensus is that it is impossible to have one without the other in terms of legal rulings.
The final sentences highlight that there is no ruling specific to one that does not apply to the other.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the relationship between Islam and Iman (faith). It begins by stating the position of the majority (al-jumhūr): Islam and Iman are the same, both returning to the meanings of acceptance (qabūl) and submission (idh‘ān). They consider every believer a Muslim and vice versa in terms of name, ruling, and final abode. This is based on consensus and textual evidence, including Quranic verses that use both terms interchangeably and imply their unity. For example, verse 3:85 states that only Islam is accepted, yet Iman is also accepted, indicating they are the same; verse 51:36 uses 'believers' and 'Muslims' in parallel; verse 49:17 mentions guidance to Iman as a favor after they have already entered Islam. The response (qulnā) clarifies that there is no dispute about the outward application of Islam to surrender, and that the difference in conceptual definition is sufficient for the validity of coupling in speech. The hadith itself explains the objects of Iman and the laws of Islam, not a real distinction. The author then reasserts the majority view, explaining the meanings: 'I believed' means 'I affirmed truthfulness' and 'I submitted' means 'I surrendered.' Both revert to acknowledgment, compliance, and acceptance. Consequently, it is inconceivable in Islamic law to have a believer who is not a Muslim or vice versa. This is what scholars mean by synonymity. The passage quotes al-Tabṣira for a more detailed definition: Iman is affirmation of the testimony of intellects and transmitted proofs concerning God's oneness, while Islam is surrendering one's whole self to God in servitude without shirk. Both lead to the same meaning. If they were different, one could conceive of a believer without Islam or a Muslim without Iman, which would entail different rulings in this world or the afterlife—a clearly false conclusion. The passage also cites al-Kifāya, where Iman is defined as affirming God's commands and prohibitions, and Islam as compliance and humility to His divinity; the two are inseparable in ruling. Finally, it argues that if by 'unity' we mean this inseparability, then we can rely on consensus that someone who fulfills the conditions of Iman cannot be other than a Muslim, and vice versa, and there is no ruling specific to one that does not apply to the other.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Islam and Iman are distinct categories with different rulings, such that one could be a Muslim without faith or vice versa.
Correction: The passage shows that they are inseparable in meaning and ruling; consensus affirms that fulfilling one necessarily entails the other, and there is no ruling that applies to one but not the other.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if Islam and Iman were truly different according to the passage?
A) They would have the same rulings.
B) One could exist without the other, leading to different rulings in this world or the Hereafter.
C) There would be no need for both terms.
D) They would be synonyms with no difference.

Key Takeaway
Iman and Islam are definitionally one; they cannot be separated in reality or ruling, as consensus confirms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

First Proof for Identity of Iman and Islam (Quran 3:85) with Objection and Answer
Taftazani presents the first proof that iman is identical to Islam from Quran 3:85, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that iman is identical to Islam based on Quran 3:85, and how the objection and answer refine the understanding of 'din'.

Core Idea
The Quran itself declares that true religion is Islam, so iman cannot be separate.

Taftazani presents the first of several proofs for the identity of iman and Islam. This proof draws directly from the Quran and addresses a subtle objection about the meaning of 'din'. By examining this argument, you will see how careful interpretation of key terms is essential in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's sequence come through before interpreting.
Notice the compact style of theological argument.
2
Identify the thesis and the proof
The passage begins by stating the thesis (iman = Islam) and then gives the first proof: Quran 3:85. Locate this verse and understand how it functions as proof.
The proof is a conditional: if iman were other than Islam, seeking it would be unacceptable, but that is false.
3
Understand the objection
The objector suggests that iman might be different from Islam but still not be another 'religion' because 'din' here means acts of obedience. Follow this objection.
The objector uses a previous definition of 'din' as acts of obedience.
4
See how the author answers
Taftazani rejects the objection by clarifying that 'din' in the verse means the established way (millah/tariqa) of the Prophet, which is exactly what iman is. He also explains the difference in usage between 'Islam' and 'iman'.
The answer shows that 'iman' is not a separate religion but the same as Islam.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this proof establishes the identity of iman and Islam, and why the objection fails. Also note the important distinction in how the terms are commonly used.
This proof is one of two aspects mentioned; the next lesson may cover the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان دار الإسلام وبالعكس، وعلى أن الناس كانوا في عهد النبي عليه السلام ثلاث فرق، مؤمن وكافر، ومنافق، لا رابع لهم والمشهور من استدلال القوم وجهان: أحدهما - أن الإيمان لو كان غير الإسلام لم يقبل من مبتغيه لقوله تعالى: (ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه) واللازم باطل، بالاتفاق، واعترض بأنه يجوز أن يكون غيره. لكن لا يكون ديناً غيره لكون الدين عبارة عن الطاعات على ما سبق. وقد عرفت ما فيه. بل المراد بالدين الملة والطريقة الثابتة من النبي عليه السلام والإيمان كذلك، وإن استمر في إطلاق أهل الشرع دين الإسلام ولم يسمع دين الإيمان، وذلك لاشتهار لفظ الإسلام في طريقة النبي واعتبار الإضافة إليه حتى صار بمنزلة اسم لدين محمد (عليه السلام). ولفظ الإيمان في فعل المؤمن من حيث الإضافة إليه، ولم يصر بمنزلة الاسم للدين ولهذا كثيراً ما يفتقر في الإيمان إلى ذكر المتعلق مثل: آمنوا بالله ورسوله. وغير ذلك، بخلاف الإسلام.
Iman is the same as Islam and vice versa, and that the people in the era of the Prophet, peace be upon him, were three groups: believer, disbeliever, and hypocrite, no fourth for them. The well-known from the proofs of the people are two aspects: One of them – that iman, if it were other than Islam, it would not be accepted from the one who seeks it, due to His saying, exalted is He: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, never will it be accepted from him' (3:85). And the consequent is false by consensus. And it was objected that it is possible that it is other than it, but it is not other than it because 'religion' (din) is an expression for acts of obedience as previously mentioned. And you have known what is in it. Rather, by 'religion' is meant the creed and established way (milla and tariqa) from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and iman is likewise. And this continues in the usage of the people of Sacred Law regarding the religion of Islam, and the religion of iman is not heard, and that is due to the fame of the word 'Islam' in the way of the Prophet and the consideration of attribution to him until it became like a name for the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And the word 'iman' is in the action of the believer in terms of attribution to him, and it did not become like a name for the religion, and for this reason iman often requires mentioning the object, such as: 'Believe in Allah and His Messenger' (57:7). And other than that, unlike Islam.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines the position that iman and Islam are identical. It begins by stating the thesis and adds that if identical, there are only three categories in the Prophet's time: believer, disbeliever, hypocrite. The first proof uses Quran 3:85: if iman were other than Islam, seeking a religion other than Islam would not be accepted, but it is accepted, so they must be the same. The objection claims iman might be different but still not another religion because 'din' here means acts of obedience, which could include iman. The answer refutes this by clarifying that 'din' in the verse means the established way of the Prophet, which is exactly iman. The difference in usage is explained: 'Islam' has become a proper name for the religion, while 'iman' is used attributively for the believer's act, hence often requiring a direct object.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Quran 3:85 implies iman is a different religion that is not accepted because it is not called 'Islam'.
Correction: The verse actually proves they are identical: if iman were different, it would be a religion other than Islam and would not be accepted, but it is accepted by consensus.
