Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether istithna is permissible at all.
Correction: Actually, the Salaf who permit it consider it valid, but the objection is about whether it is preferable and why it is specific to faith.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the first proof fail to justify?
A) The permissibility of istithna
B) The preference of istithna over direct statement and its exclusive application to faith
C) The definition of faith
D) The necessity of istithna

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that the first proof for istithna only establishes its permissibility, not its preference over a direct statement, nor the exclusivity of this etiquette to faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection points out that making an exception is a form of politeness to Allah. Is your faith accompanied by genuine humility and dependence on Allah, or do you take your belief for granted? What one change will you make in your daily remembrance to cultivate this adab?
?
The passage argues that the etiquette of istithna is not exclusive to faith. Are there areas in your life where you claim a special spiritual status that you don't extend to other obligations? How will you practice consistency in your obedience starting today?

The Majority View Forbidding Istithna in Faith
Sanusi reports the majority opinion forbidding istithna in faith, with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of scholars forbade saying 'if Allah wills' when professing faith, and how this relates to the certainty of belief.

Core Idea
Faith is certain; saying 'if Allah wills' implies doubt, which contradicts true belief.

This lesson examines a classical theological debate: can one say 'I am a believer if Allah wills'? While some early scholars permitted it, the majority, including Abu Hanifa, argued that such phrasing introduces uncertainty into an otherwise definite inner conviction. The passage presents both views and explains the reasoning behind the majority's prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two positions presented.
The passage is concise; take your time to absorb the key terms.
2
Identify the two scholarly positions
Find where the permissive view (some Salaf, al-Shafi'i) and the prohibitive view (majority, Abu Hanifa) are stated.
3
Understand the reasoning for prohibition
The majority argue that faith (tasdiq) is a matter of certainty; hesitation in stating it implies a lack of true belief.
The phrase 'if Allah wills' suggests doubt about one's current faith status.
4
Learn the recommended alternative
Instead of 'if Allah wills,' one should say 'I am a believer truly' to avoid any hint of uncertainty.
This is a practical takeaway for belief and speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب كثير من السلف، وهو المحكي عن الشافعي (رضي الله تعالى عنه والمروي عن ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه أن الإيمان يدخله الاستثناء، فيقال : أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله تعالى. ومنعه الأكثرون وعليه أبو حنيفة (رضي الله عنه وأصحابه، لأن التصديق أمر معلوم لا تردد فيه عند تحققه، ومن تردد في تحققه له ، لم يكن مؤمناً ،قطعاً، وإذا لم يكن للشك والتردد، فالأولى أن يترك ، بل يقال : أنا مؤمن حقاً . دفعاً للإيهام .
A large number of the Salaf went—and it is narrated from al-Shafi'i and reported from Ibn Mas'ud—that faith admits of exception, so one says: 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' And the majority forbade it, and upon this are Abu Hanifa and his companions, because affirmation (tasdiq) is a known matter with no hesitation in it when it is realized, and whoever hesitates in its realization for himself is definitely not a believer. And if it is not for doubt and hesitation, then it is better to leave it, rather one should say: 'I am a believer truly,' to remove misunderstanding.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the majority opinion (Abu Hanifa and others) on the issue of whether one can say 'I am a believer if Allah wills' (istithna fi al-iman). It states that the Salaf and al-Shafi'i permitted it, but the majority (including Abu Hanifa) forbade it because faith is a definite inner conviction (tasdiq) without uncertainty. If one doubts his own faith, he is not a true believer. Therefore, to avoid implying doubt, one should assert his faith firmly.

Key Terms
التصديق
الاستثناء
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the majority forbid istithna because they deny any possibility of change in faith or because it is disrespectful to God.
Correction: The majority's prohibition is specifically about present certainty: faith is a definite inner state, so expressing hesitation (istithna) contradicts its nature.

Checkpoint
Why does the majority (Abu Hanifa) forbid saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'?
A) Because it implies doubt about one's present faith
B) Because it is disrespectful to God
C) Because faith cannot change
D) Because it contradicts the Quranic command

Key Takeaway
The majority forbade 'if Allah wills' in professing faith to avoid implying uncertainty about one's present belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' do you ever inwardly hesitate—perhaps out of fear of being presumptuous? Could that hesitation actually be a sign that your conviction is not as firm as you think? Today, resolve to examine your heart and ask Allah for unwavering certainty.
?
The passage warns that hesitation in affirming your own faith may indicate you are not truly a believer. Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are not fully convinced of Allah's promise or threat? What would it take for you to say 'I am a believer truly' with no reservation?
?
If you were asked right now, 'Are you a believer?' would your answer be immediate and certain, or would you feel the need to add 'if Allah wills'? What does that reveal about the strength of your iman? Commit to strengthening your certainty through study and reflection on Allah's signs.

Two Arguments for Permitting 'If Allah Wills'
Two arguments for permitting istithna: blessing/politeness and hidden nature of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments used to justify saying 'if Allah wills' regarding one's faith, and the scholarly critique of each.

Core Idea
Istithna in faith is argued by politeness and hidden impediments, but both have limitations.

This passage presents the first two arguments of those who permit saying 'if Allah wills' about one's faith. The first argument emphasizes politeness and blessing, while the second points to the hidden and precarious nature of saving faith. The author of the commentary highlights the strengths and weaknesses of each, preparing us for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument structure. Note the two numbered arguments.
The passage is dense; focus on the key terms.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument says istithna is for blessing and politeness, avoiding self-praise. Identify why the author finds it insufficient.
The author notes it does not explain why this is specific to faith.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument focuses on hidden impediments to faith like desire and Satan. Note that it seems reasonable but contradicts claimed consensus.
The author says 'this is close' but problematic.
4
Review the translation and commentary
Compare the translation with the commentary to see how the author expands on each argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللقائلين بصحته وجوه : الأول - أنه للتبرك في ذكر الله والتأدب بإحالة الأمور إلى مشيئة الله ، والتبرؤ عن تزكية النفس والإعجاب بحالها والتردد في العاقبة والمآل وهذا يفيد مجرد الصحة، لا إيثار قولهم : أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله على أنا مؤمن حقاً، ولا يدفع ما ذكر من دفع الإيهام، ولا يبين وجه اختصاص التأدب والتبرك بالإيمان دون غيره من الأعمال والطاعات . والثاني - أن التصديق الإيماني المنوط به النجاة أمر قلبي خفي له معارضات خفية كثيرة من الهوى والشيطان والخذلان بالمرء، وإن كان جازماً بحصوله لكن لا يأمن ان يشوبه شيء من منافيات النجاة سيما عند ملاحظة تفاصيل الأوامر والنواهي الصعبة المخالفة للهوى، والمستلذات من غير علم له بذلك. فذلك يفوض حصوله إلى مشيئة الله . وهذا قريب لولا مخالفته لما يدعيه القوم من الإجماع ، ولما ذكر في الفتاوى من الروايات .
For those who hold its validity, there are aspects: First, that it is for blessing in mentioning God and politeness by referring matters to God's will, and disavowing self-praise and self-admiration, and uncertainty about the outcome and end. This merely indicates validity, not a preference for their saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' over 'I am a believer truly,' and it does not remove the aforementioned removal of misunderstanding, nor does it clarify the reason for limiting politeness and blessing to faith rather than other acts and devotions. Second, that the faith-affirmation (tasdiq imani) upon which salvation depends is a hidden heart matter with many hidden opposing factors from desire, Satan, and divine abandonment, even if one is certain of its existence, but one is not safe from it being mixed with something from the impediments of salvation, especially when considering the difficult details of commands and prohibitions contrary to desire and pleasures without his knowledge. So he entrusts its attainment to God's will. This is close, were it not for its contradiction to the consensus they claim, and what is mentioned in fatwas from narrations.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage details the first two arguments of those who permit saying 'if Allah wills' regarding faith. The first argument is that it is a form of politeness and blessing, avoiding self-praise. However, the author points out this does not explain why it is specific to faith. The second argument is that genuine faith leading to salvation is hidden and can be affected by hidden factors, so one cannot be absolutely certain, thus it is appropriate to entrust it to God's will. The author notes this is reasonable but contradicts claimed consensus.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the second argument implies one should doubt one's faith entirely.
Correction: The argument does not call for doubt, but acknowledges that hidden factors may affect the salvific quality of faith, hence entrusting its true state to God.

C) Because faith can only be known at death

Key Takeaway
The second argument for istithna—rooted in the hidden and vulnerable nature of faith—is considered stronger than the politeness argument, but it remains contested due to consensus claims.

Faith at Death: The Ash'ari Criterion for Salvation
Imam al-Haramayn's argument that istithna refers to the final state of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari scholars distinguish between present faith and faith at death, and how this resolves the apparent contradiction in saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills.'

Core Idea
Faith's saving power depends on its final state, not its present certainty.

This lesson presents the third and most relied-upon argument from Imam al-Haramayn. He clarifies that the Salaf's use of 'if Allah wills' was not doubting their present faith, but acknowledging that final salvation depends on the faith at death. This distinction resolves many theological puzzles about predestination and waqf.

Guided Path
1
Read Imam al-Haramayn's statement
Start by reading the passage carefully, noting that faith is certain in the present but saving faith is the one at death.
2
Understand 'muwafat'
Learn the meaning of muwafat: 'arrival at the end of life and the first stage of the Hereafter.' This is the faith that matters for salvation.
3
See the Ash'ari position
Many Ash'aris hold that the criterion is the final state (iman al-muwafat). They do not deny present faith is real, but deny its final importance.
4
Consider the objection and answer
The objection: if present faith is true, one cannot say 'if Allah wills.' The answer: one can because the final state is unknown.
5
Reflect on the hadith reconciliation
The hadith about happiness/misery in the womb refers to the final seal, not the initial state. This shows the focus on the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ـ وعليه التعويل ، ما قال إمام الحرمين إن الإيمان ثابت في الحال قطعاً من غير شك فيه . لكن الإيمان الذي هو علم الفوز، وآية النجاة إيمان الموافاة، فاعتنى السلف به وقرنوه بالمشيئة ولم يقصدوا الشك في الإيمان الناجز، ومعنى الموافاة الإتيان والوصول إلى آخر الحياة وأول منازل الآخرة، ولا خفاء في أن الإيمان المنجي والكفر المهلك ما يكون في تلك الحال، وإن كان مسبوقاً بالضد، لا ما ثبت أولاً وتغير إلى الضد. فلهذا يرى الكثير من الأشاعرة يبتون القول بأن العبرة بإيمان الموافاة وسعادتها . بمعنى أن ذلك هو المنجي، لا بمعنى أن إيمان الحال ليس بإيمان وكفره ليس بكفر ، وكذا السعادة والشقاوة والولاية والعداوة، وعلى هذا يسقط عنهم ما يقال إنه إذا اتصف بالإيمان على الحقيقة، كان مؤمناً حقاً، ولا يصح أن يقول : أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله تعالى، كما لا يصح أن يقول : انا إن شاء الله تعالى. وإذا كان مؤمناً حقاً، كان مؤمناً عند الله تعالى، وفي علم الله ، وإن كان الله تعالى يعلم أنه يتغير عن تلك الحال. وإذا كان مؤمناً في الحال كان ولياً لله، سعيداً. وإن كان كافراً ، كان عدواً له شقياً، وكما يصير المؤمن كافراً، يصير الولي عدواً والسعيد شقياً، وبالعكس . وما يحكى عنهم من أن السعيد لا يشقى، والشقي لا يسعد، وأن السعيد من سعد في بطن أمه، والشقي من شقي في بطن أمه ، فمعناه من علم الله منه السعادة المعتبرة التي هي سعادة الموافاة، فهو لا يتغير إلى شقاوة الموافاة، وبالعكس . وكذا في الولاية والعداوة وأن السعيد الذي يعتد بسعادته من علم الله أنه يختم له بالسعادة . وكذا الشقاوة.
The third—and upon it is the reliance—is what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Faith is definitely established in the present without any doubt in it. However, the faith that is the sign of success and the token of salvation is the faith at the time of death (iman al-muwafat).' So the Salaf paid attention to this and linked it to the divine will, and they did not intend doubt in the present faith. The meaning of 'muwafat' is arrival and reaching the end of life and the first stage of the Hereafter. There is no hidden fact that the saving faith and destructive unbelief are what occur in that state, even if preceded by its opposite, not what was first established and then changed to its opposite. For this reason, many of the Ash'aris firmly hold that the criterion is faith at the time of death and its happiness. Meaning that it is what saves, not meaning that present faith is not faith and present unbelief is not unbelief. Likewise for happiness, misery, friendship, and enmity. On this basis, the objection falls away that if one is described with faith in truth, he is truly a believer and it is not valid for him to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills,' just as it is not valid for him to say 'I am if Allah wills.' And if he is truly a believer, he is a believer in the sight of God and in God's knowledge, even if God knows that he will change from that state. And if he is a believer in the present, he is a friend of God, happy; if he is a disbeliever, he is an enemy of God, miserable. And just as a believer can become a disbeliever, a friend can become an enemy and a happy person miserable, and vice versa. And what is narrated from them that the happy will not be miserable and the miserable will not be happy, and that the happy is the one who is happy in his mother's womb, and the miserable is the one who is miserable in his mother's womb—its meaning is that the one whom God knows to have the considered happiness, which is happiness at the time of death, he will not change to misery at the time of death, and vice versa. Likewise for friendship and enmity, and that the happy person who is counted as happy is the one whom God knows will be sealed with happiness. Likewise for misery.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the third and most relied-upon argument from Imam al-Haramayn. It distinguishes between present faith (iman al-najiz) and faith at the time of death (iman al-muwafat). The Salaf's use of 'if Allah wills' was not about doubting present faith but about the unknown outcome of one's faith at death. The author then explains how the Ash'aris interpret the hadith about happiness and misery in the womb as referring to the final state (muwafat). This reconciles the apparent contradiction between affirming present faith and saying 'if Allah wills.'

Key Terms
إيمان الموافاة
علم الفوز
السعادة
الشقاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position denies that present faith is real.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm present faith is real, but they hold that the decisive criterion for salvation is faith at death, not present faith.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, why did the Salaf say 'if Allah wills' about faith?
A) Because they doubted their present faith
B) Because of uncertainty about their final state at death
C) Because they were not certain of God's mercy
D) Because it is a form of worship

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position holds that the faith that ultimately saves is the faith at death, not the faith one has earlier in life.

The Second Opinion: Hidden Nature of Assent
The author presents the second opinion on the istithna' in faith, based on the hidden nature of assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that the faith-based assent upon which salvation depends is hidden and subject to opposing forces, leading the believer to entrust its final reality to Allah's will.

Core Idea
Even certain faith can be undermined by hidden forces, so salvation is entrusted to Allah.

The author has already mentioned one opinion and now turns to a second. This opinion emphasizes the internal and hidden nature of genuine faith, pointing to the many subtle factors that can compromise it. As a result, the believer does not claim certainty about their ultimate salvation, but defers to Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author describes the hidden nature of faith and the opposing forces.
2
Identify the key claim
The opinion claims that even a person certain of their current faith cannot be sure that something contrary to salvation has not mixed with it. Find the sentence that expresses this.
3
Note the author's critique
The author says this opinion is plausible but contradicts consensus and fatwas. Understand why this critique matters for evaluating the opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني - أن التصديق الإيماني المنوط به النجاة أمر قلبي خفي له معارضات خفية كثيرة من الهوى والشيطان والخذلان بالمرء، وإن كان جازماً بحصوله لكن لا يأمن ان يشوبه شيء من منافيات النجاة سيما عند ملاحظة تفاصيل الأوامر والنواهي الصعبة المخالفة للهوى، والمستلذات من غير علم له بذلك. فذلك يفوض حصوله إلى مشيئة الله . وهذا قريب لولا مخالفته لما يدعيه القوم من الإجماع ، ولما ذكر في الفتاوى من الروايات .
الثالث ـ وعليه التعويل ، ما قال إمام الحرمين إن الإيمان ثابت في الحال قطعاً من غير شك فيه . لكن الإيمان الذي هو علم الفوز، وآية النجاة إيمان الموافاة، فاعتنى السلف به وقرنوه بالمشيئة ولم يقصدوا الشك في الإيمان الناجز، ومعنى الموافاة الإتيان والوصول إلى آخر الحياة وأول منازل الآخرة، ولا خفاء في أن الإيمان المنجي والكفر المهلك ما يكون في تلك الحال، وإن كان مسبوقاً بالضد، لا ما ثبت أولاً وتغير إلى الضد. فلهذا يرى الكثير من الأشاعرة يبتون القول بأن العبرة بإيمان الموافاة وسعادتها . بمعنى أن ذلك هو المنجي، لا بمعنى أن إيمان الحال ليس بإيمان وكفره ليس بكفر ، وكذا السعادة والشقاوة والولاية والعداوة، وعلى هذا يسقط عنهم ما يقال إنه إذا اتصف بالإيمان على الحقيقة، كان مؤمناً حقاً، ولا يصح أن يقول : أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله تعالى، كما لا يصح أن يقول : انا إن شاء الله تعالى. وإذا كان مؤمناً حقاً، كان مؤمناً عند الله تعالى، وفي علم الله ، وإن كان الله تعالى يعلم أنه يتغير عن تلك الحال. وإذا كان مؤمناً في الحال كان وليا الله ، سعيداً. وإن كان كافراً ، كان عدواً له شقياً، وكما يصير المؤمن كافراً، يصير الولي عدواً والسعيد شقياً، وبالعكس . وما يحكى عنهم من أن السعيد لا يشقى، والشقي لا يسعد، وأن السعيد من سعد في بطن أمه، والشقي من شقي في بطن أمه ، فمعناه من علم الله منه السعادة المعتبرة التي هي سعادة الموافاة، فهو لا يتغير إلى شقاوة الموافاة، وبالعكس . وكذا في الولاية ،والعداوة وأن السعيد الذي يعتد بسعادته من علم الله أنه يختم له بالسعادة . وكذا الشقاوة.
وبالجملة لا يشك المؤمن في ثبوت الايمان وتحققه في الحال. ولا في الجزم بالثبات ، والبقاء عليه في المال، لكن بخاف سوء الخاتمة ويرجو حسن العاقبة ، فيربط إيمان الموافاة الذي هو آية الفوز والنجاة ، ووسيلة كل الدرجات بمشيئة الله جرياً على مقتضى قوله تعالى : { ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غداً إلا أن يشاء الله } جعل الله حياتنا إليه ، ومماتنا عليه. وختم لنا بالحسنى ويسرنا للفوز بالذخر الأسنى بالنبي وآله.
And the second: that the faith-based assent (al-tasdiq al-imani) upon which salvation depends is a hidden matter of the heart, having many hidden opposing forces from desire, Satan, and abandonment (al-khidhlan) of a person. Even if he is certain of its occurrence, he is not safe from something of the opposites of salvation mixing with it, especially when observing the difficult details of commands and prohibitions that oppose desire, and pleasures without him knowing about that. So he entrusts its occurrence to the will of Allah. And this is plausible were it not for its contradiction of what they claim of consensus (ijma'), and what is mentioned in the fatwas from the narrations.
The third – and upon it is reliance – is what Imam al-Haramayn said: that faith is established in the present state (al-hal) definitively without any doubt in it. But the faith that is the sign of success (alam al-fawz) and the token of salvation is the faith of the time of death (iman al-muwafat). So the Salaf were concerned with it and linked it to the will (mashia), and they did not intend doubt in the present faith (al-iman al-najiz). And the meaning of al-muwafat is arriving and reaching the end of life and the first of the abodes of the Hereafter. And there is no concealment that the saving faith and the destructive unbelief is what occurs in that state, even if it was preceded by its opposite, not what was established first and then changed to its opposite. For this reason, many of the Ash'aris decisively state that the criterion is the faith of the time of death and its felicity. Meaning that that is what saves, not meaning that present faith is not faith and its unbelief is not unbelief, and likewise felicity and wretchedness, and friendship and enmity. And on this, there falls away from them what is said: that if he is described with faith in reality, he is truly a believer, and it is not correct for him to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills', just as it is not correct for him to say 'I am if Allah wills'. And if he is truly a believer, he is a believer with Allah and in Allah's knowledge, even if Allah knows that he will change from that state. And if he is a believer in the present state, he is a friend of Allah, fortunate. And if he is an unbeliever, he is an enemy of Allah, wretched. And just as a believer becomes an unbeliever, the friend becomes an enemy and the fortunate becomes wretched, and vice versa. And what is reported from them – that the fortunate does not become wretched and the wretched does not become fortunate, and that the fortunate is the one who was fortunate in his mother's womb, and the wretched is the one who was wretched in his mother's womb – its meaning is: he from whom Allah knows the considered felicity, which is the felicity of the time of death, he does not change to the wretchedness of the time of death, and vice versa. And likewise in friendship and enmity, and that the fortunate whose felicity is counted is the one whom Allah knows that He will seal for him with felicity. And likewise wretchedness. 
In summary: the believer does not doubt the establishment and actuality of faith in the present state, nor in certainty of steadfastness and remaining upon it in [the present] state, but he fears an evil end and hopes for a good outcome, so he links the faith of the time of death, which is the token of success and salvation and the means of all degrees, to the will of Allah, in accordance with the requirement of His saying: {And do not say about anything, 'Indeed I will do that tomorrow,' except 'If Allah wills'}. May Allah make our life and our death for Him, and seal for us with goodness, and make easy for us success in the most excellent treasure, through the Prophet and his family.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari theological position on the issue of making an exception (istithna') when declaring one's faith, i.e., saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' The author has already mentioned a first opinion (not in this excerpt) and now discusses the second and third opinions.

The second opinion holds that the faith upon which salvation depends is a hidden matter of the heart. It is constantly under attack from desires, Satan, and divine abandonment (khidhlan). Even if a person is certain of their current faith, they cannot be sure that something contrary to salvation has not mixed with it—especially when considering the difficult commands and prohibitions that go against desire, as well as pleasures that may lead them astray without their knowledge. Therefore, such a person entrusts the reality of their faith to Allah's will. The author notes that this opinion is plausible, but it conflicts with the claimed consensus (ijma') and with rulings found in fatwas.

The third opinion—and it is the one relied upon—is that of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He states that faith in the present moment is certain and beyond doubt. However, the faith that truly matters for salvation is the faith at the time of death (iman al-muwafat). The Salaf linked their declaration of faith to Allah's will intending this latter faith, not doubting their current faith. 'Muwafat' means arriving at the end of life and the first stage of the afterlife. It is obvious that the faith that saves and the unbelief that destroys is what is present in that final state, even if preceded by its opposite. What was established earlier but later changed to its opposite is irrelevant.

Thus, many Ash'aris decisively state that the criterion is iman al-muwafat and its resulting felicity. They do not mean that current faith is not genuine faith or that current unbelief is not genuine unbelief; rather, they mean that the decisive factor for salvation is the final state. This resolves the objection: if someone is truly a believer now, it would seem incorrect for them to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills'—just as it is incorrect to say 'I am if Allah wills' without completing the sentence. However, because the believer fears an evil end (su' al-khatima) and hopes for a good outcome, they link their final faith to Allah's will in accordance with the Quranic command in Surah al-Kahf (18:23-24).

The author also explains the Ash'ari statements about felicity (sa'ada) and wretchedness (shaqawa): the fortunate one (sa'eed) does not become wretched, and the wretched one (shaqi) does not become fortunate. This means that those whom Allah knows to have the considered felicity—that is, the felicity of the time of death—will not change to the wretchedness of the time of death, and vice versa. The same applies to friendship (wilaya) and enmity ('adawa). So the one whose felicity is counted is the one whom Allah knows will be sealed with felicity.

In summary, the believer does not doubt their current faith or their steadfastness upon it in the present. However, they fear an evil end and hope for a good outcome. Therefore, they link their faith at death—the token of success and the means of all degrees—to Allah's will, obeying the Quranic verse. The passage ends with a supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, why does a believer say 'if Allah wills' regarding his faith?
A) Because he doubts the existence of his current faith
B) Because hidden opposing forces may compromise the faith that saves
C) Because he is unsure about the commands and prohibitions
D) Because he fears losing faith due to sins

Key Takeaway
The second opinion holds that even a certain believer cannot be sure of salvation due to hidden threats, so they say 'if Allah wills' out of caution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt certain of your faith, yet later realized that a hidden desire or distraction had weakened your devotion? What does this passage teach you about guarding your heart against unseen threats?
?
The second opinion humbly entrusts salvation to Allah’s will. Are you relying more on your own certainty or on Allah’s mercy? What would change in your daily worship if you truly lived with this reliance?
?
Consider the 'hidden opposing forces' mentioned: desire, Satan, and abandonment. Which of these do you sense most actively pulling you away from obedience without your notice? What concrete step can you take this week to expose that force and seek Allah’s protection?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Third Opinion: Iman al-Muwafat and the Ash'ari Position
Imam al-Haramayn's position establishes the certainty of present faith but the decisive role of faith at death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relied-upon Ash'ari position that present faith is certain but the decisive faith is at death (iman al-muwafat), and how this resolves the issue of istithna'.

Core Idea
Faith at death determines salvation; present faith is certain but the end is unknown.

The third opinion, attributed to Imam al-Haramayn, is the one upon which the Ash'aris rely. It affirms that a believer's current faith is certain, but the faith that truly counts for salvation is that which persists until death. The Salaf's use of istithna' refers to this final faith, not to doubt about the present. This lesson unpacks this nuanced position and its implications for terms like felicity and wretchedness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the distinction between present faith (al-iman al-hal) and faith at death (iman al-muwafat). Identify the key sentences that establish the Ash'ari position.
2
Understand the meaning of muwafat
The author explains that 'muwafat' means arriving at the end of life and the first abodes of the Hereafter. Understand why this is the decisive moment.
3
Examine the explanation of sa'ada and shaqawa
The passage clarifies that statements like 'the fortunate does not become wretched' refer to the final state (muwafat), not the present. See how this resolves apparent contradictions.
This shows the coherence of the Ash'ari system.
4
Reflect on the summary
The final summary states that the believer does not doubt present faith but fears an evil end, linking muwafat to Allah's will per Quran 18:23-24. Grasp the practical piety implied.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying 'if Allah wills' about one's faith implies doubt about the reality of one's current faith.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the istithna' refers to the faith at death (iman al-muwafat), not present faith. Present faith is certain.

According to Imam al-Haramayn and the Ash'ari position, what does 'iman al-muwafat' refer to?

?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

Balancing Certainty and Fear: The Believer's Hope for a Good End
Al-Taftazani concludes the discussion on the believer's hope and fear of bad end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a believer maintains certainty of faith while simultaneously fearing a bad end and hoping for a good outcome, and why final faith is tied to God's will.

Core Idea
Certainty in faith does not eliminate the need for fear, hope, and reliance on God's will.

Al-Taftazani concludes his discussion on the believer's psychological state regarding faith. He affirms that while the believer is certain of faith in the present and its continuation, he must also fear a bad end and hope for a good outcome. This leads to tying one's final faith to the will of God, as exemplified by the Quranic command to say 'if God wills.' This lesson unpacks that balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's tone: it is a concluding summary.
The word 'المال' is likely 'المآل' (final outcome). Keep this in mind.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three sequential points: (1) certainty in present faith, (2) certainty in steadfastness but with fear and hope, (3) tying final faith to God's will with a Quranic proof. Find each one.
3
Reflect on the balance
Consider why the believer does not doubt faith now but still fears the end. How does tying to God's will resolve this? The supplication shows the desired outcome.
This connects to the learning objective.

وبالجملة لا يشك المؤمن في ثبوت الايمان وتحققه في الحال. ولا في الجزم بالثبات ، والبقاء عليه في المال، لكن بخاف سوء الخاتمة ويرجو حسن العاقبة ، فيربط ريمان الموافاة الذي هو آية الفوز والنجاة ، ووسيلة كل الدرجات بمشيئة الله جرياً على مقتضى قوله تعالى : ( ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غداً إلا أن يشاء الله ) (۱) جعل الله حياتنا إليه ، ومماتنا عليه. وختم لنا بالحسنى ويسرنا للفوز بالذخر الأسنى بالنبي وآله.
And in summary, the believer does not doubt the existence of faith and its realization in the present. Nor does he doubt certainty of steadfastness and remaining upon it in wealth [or: the final outcome?]. But he fears a bad end and hopes for a good outcome, so he ties the faith of the final meeting (muwāfāt) which is the sign of success and deliverance, and the means to all degrees, to the will of God, following the import of His statement: 'And do not say about anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow," except [if] God wills.' (Quran 18:23-24) May God make our life unto Him and our death upon Him. And seal for us with the goodly [end] and ease for us the attainment of the highest treasure through the Prophet and his family.

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary of the believer's state regarding faith. It states that the believer is certain of his faith in the present moment and certain of its continuation, but he fears a bad end (sū' al-khātimah) and hopes for a good outcome (ḥusn al-'āqibah). Therefore, he connects his final faith (īmān al-muwāfāt) which is the sign of success, to God's will, citing the Quranic verse that one should not say 'I will do it tomorrow' without adding 'if God wills.' The passage ends with a supplication asking God to make life and death for Him, and to grant a good end. This shows the balance between certainty and fear, and reliance on God's will.

Are you more afraid of a bad worldly outcome or a bad spiritual end? If you are not actively afraid of a bad end (sū' al-khātimah), does that mean you have forgotten that your final faith is not guaranteed by your present certainty?

The Validity of the Faith of the Blind Follower (Muqallid)
Al-Taftazani presents the debate on the muqallid's faith, including the majority view, objection, and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the faith of a blind follower (muqallid) is valid, including the majority view, the objection, and the answer.

Core Idea
Faith requires certainty, not personal investigation.

Al-Taftazani presents a key debate in Islamic theology: does a person who accepts religious beliefs on authority (taqlid) truly have faith? The majority say yes, because faith is affirmation (tasdiq), which only requires decisive belief. But an objection is raised: affirmation requires knowledge. How do scholars respond? This passage lays out the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the positions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two main positions: the majority who accept the muqallid's faith, and those who reject it (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila).
Notice that the majority includes all jurists.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that tasdiq requires knowledge ('ilm), which the muqallid lacks. Underline the definition of knowledge given: a decisive and conforming belief based on necessity or inference.
This objection challenges the muqallid's claim to have tasdiq.
3
Study the answer
The answer clarifies that what matters for tasdiq is certainty (yaqin) – decisive and conforming belief. Even dominant opinion (zann ghalib) that excludes doubt suffices. Also, tasdiq can exist without detailed knowledge, as we believe in prophets without knowing them individually.
The answer shows that faith does not require personal inference.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the broader implication: faith is accessible to all believers, not only scholars. This reinforces the validity of simple faith.
This reflects the Ash'ari majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الخامس - (الجمهور على صحة إيمان المقلد، لأن التصديق لا يتوقف على ثبات الاعتقاد. بل جزمه، وعدم النفع . قياساً على إيمان اليأس بجامع عدم مشقة النظر والاستدلال التي بها الثواب فاسد أو على تقدير ثبوت مثله بالقياس . فالعلة في الأصل كونه إيمان دفع عذاب لا إيمان حقيقة، وأنه لم يبق حينئذ للعبد قدرة التصرف في نفسه والاستمتاع بها) ذهب كثير من العلماء وجميع الفقهاء إلى صحة إيمان المقلد وترتب الأحكام عليه في الدنيا والآخرة، ومنعه الشيخ أبو الحسن (۱) والمعتزلة، وكثير من المتكلمين . حجة القائلين بالصحة أن حقيقة الإيمان هو التصديق، وقد وجدت من غير اقتران بموجب من موجبات الكفر. فإن قيل : لا يتصور التصديق بدون العلم لأنه إما ذاتي للتصديق أو شرط له، على ما سبق . ولا علم للمقلد ، لأنه اعتقاد جازم مطابق يستند إلى سبب من ضرورة أو استدلال. قلنا : المعتبر في التصديق هو اليقين . أعني الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق، بل ربما يكتفي بالمطابقة ويجعل الظن الغالب الذي لا يخطر معه النقيض بالبال في حكم اليقين، وقد يقال : إن التصديق قد يكون بدون العلم والمعرفة ، وبالعكس . فإنا نؤمن بالأنبياء والملائكة ولا نعرفهم بأعيانهم ، ونؤمن بجميع أحوال القيامة من (۱) هو علي بن إسماعيل بن إسحاق، أبو الحسن من نسل الصحابي أبي موسى الأشعري مؤسس مذهب الأشاعرة كان من الأئمة المتكلمين المجتهدين ولد في البصرة عام ٢٦٠ هـ وتوفي ببغداد عام ٣٢٤ هـ راجع طبقات الشافعية ٢ : ٢٤٥ والمقريزي ۲ : ٣٥۹ وابن خلكان ١ : ٣٢٦ .
He said: The Fifth Section - (The majority [hold] the validity of the faith of the blind follower (muqallid), because tasdiq (affirmation) does not depend on the firmness of belief, but on its decisiveness and absence of contradiction. The analogy to the faith of desperation (īmān al-ya's) due to the absence of the toil of reflection and inference by which reward is earned is invalid, or if the establishment of its like by analogy is assumed, then the effective cause in the original is that it is a faith to repel punishment, not faith in reality, and that then the servant has no power to dispose of himself and enjoy it.) Many scholars and all jurists went [to the view] of the validity of the faith of the blind follower and the application of rulings upon it in this world and the Hereafter, and it was prohibited by Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (1) and the Mu'tazila, and many theologians. The proof of those who hold validity is that the reality of faith is tasdiq, and it has occurred without being accompanied by any of the causes of disbelief. For it is said: Tasdiq is inconceivable without knowledge (ilm), because it is either intrinsic to tasdiq or a condition for it, as previously mentioned. The blind follower has no knowledge, because [knowledge] is a decisive and conforming belief based on a cause of necessity or inference. We say: What is considered in tasdiq is certainty (yaqīn), i.e., decisive and conforming belief. Rather, sometimes conformity suffices, and predominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) with which the opposite does not occur to the mind is treated as certainty. And it may be said that tasdiq can exist without knowledge and knowledge without tasdiq, and vice versa. For we believe in the prophets and angels without knowing them individually, and we believe in all the states of the Hereafter from... (1) He is Ali ibn Ismail ibn Ishaq, Abu al-Hasan, from the descendants of the companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, founder of the Ash'ari school. He was one of the leading theologians and mujtahids. He was born in Basra in 260 AH and died in Baghdad in 324 AH. See Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya 2:245, al-Maqrizi 2:359, and Ibn Khallikan 1:326.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the validity of the faith of the blind follower (muqallid), i.e., one who accepts religious beliefs on authority without personal investigation. The author quotes the matn (text) which states that the majority hold it valid because faith is tasdiq, which requires only decisive belief without contradiction, not independent verification. An analogy to the faith of desperation (when one believes out of fear of punishment) is rejected. Then the commentator explains: many scholars and all jurists accept the muqallid's faith as valid in this world and the Hereafter, but it was rejected by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila. The proof of the majority: faith is tasdiq, and the muqallid's belief does not entail any cause of disbelief. An objection is raised: tasdiq requires knowledge (ilm), which the muqallid lacks because knowledge depends on necessity or inference. The answer: what matters is certainty (yaqīn), which is decisive and conforming belief. Sometimes mere conformity suffices, and dominant opinion that excludes doubt is treated as certainty. Moreover, tasdiq can exist without detailed knowledge; we believe in prophets and angels without knowing them individually. The answer continues beyond the page, but the core argument is presented: faith does not require personal investigation; intellectual certainty or even dominant opinion suffices. The footnote provides biographical information on Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Correction: Faith is valid if one has certainty (yaqin) or even predominant opinion that excludes doubt, and it does not require detailed knowledge of every object of belief.

The Validity of the Imitator's Faith
The author presents the majority view, gives proof, and answers an objection about knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for the validity of the imitator's faith (muqallid) and how the objection concerning the necessity of knowledge is answered.

Core Idea
Faith is assent with certainty, not necessarily from personal reasoning.

This lesson examines a central debate in Islamic theology: Is the faith of someone who follows religious authority without independent reasoning valid? The author presents the majority view that it is, and defends it against a key objection that faith requires knowledge derived from reasoning. Through careful analysis of the nature of assent and certainty, you will see how the muqallid's faith is affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the opening sentences to see who supports and who rejects the validity of the muqallid's faith. Notice the contrast between the majority/fuqaha and the Ash'ari/Mu'tazila.
Sheikh Abu al-Hasan refers to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.
2
Understand the core proof
The proof for validity is that faith is essentially assent (tasdiq), and the muqallid possesses this assent without any cause of disbelief. Grasp this simple but powerful point.
The proof assumes a definition of faith as assent, not as knowledge plus acceptance.
3
Consider the objection and response
The objection claims assent requires knowledge. The author responds by distinguishing between knowledge and certainty (yaqin). See how certainty is defined and why it suffices.
The response also notes that assent can exist without full knowledge, as in belief in prophets and the afterlife.
4
Reflect on the examples
The author provides two examples: (1) Believing in prophets/angels without knowing them individually, and (2) The People of the Book knowing the Prophet but not believing. These illustrate that knowledge and assent are separable.
The final remark clarifies that the impossibility is only that assent can exist without belief (knowledge), but the reverse is possible.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The passage concludes that the debate is about whether knowledge can exist without assent, not the other way around. The muqallid's faith is valid because he has assent with certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب كثير من العلماء وجميع الفقهاء إلى صحة إيمان المقلد وترتب الأحكام عليه في الدنيا والآخرة، ومنعه الشيخ أبو الحسن والمعتزلة، وكثير من المتكلمين. حجة القائلين بالصحة أن حقيقة الإيمان هو التصديق، وقد وجدت من غير اقتران بموجب من موجبات الكفر. فإن قيل: لا يتصور التصديق بدون العلم لأنه إما ذاتي للتصديق أو شرط له، على ما سبق. ولا علم للمقلد، لأنه اعتقاد جازم مطابق يستند إلى سبب من ضرورة أو استدلال. قلنا: المعتبر في التصديق هو اليقين. أعني الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق، بل ربما يكتفي بالمطابقة ويجعل الظن الغالب الذي لا يخطر معه النقيض بالبال في حكم اليقين، وقد يقال: إن التصديق قد يكون بدون العلم والمعرفة، وبالعكس. فإنا نؤمن بالأنبياء والملائكة ولا نعرفهم بأعيانهم، ونؤمن بجميع أحوال القيامة من الحساب والميزان والصراط وغير ذلك، ولا نعرف كيفياتها وأوصافها وأهل الكتاب كانوا يعرفون النبي عليه السلام كما يعرفون أبناءهم ولم يكونوا مؤمنين وفيه نظر لأن المراد العلم بما حصل التصديق به، ونحن نعلم من الأنبياء والملائكة ما نصدق به فامتناع التصديق بدون العلم، بمعنى الاعتقاد، قطعي. وإنما الكلام في العكس.
Many scholars and all jurists hold the validity of the faith of the imitator and that rulings are attached to it in this world and the hereafter, while Sheikh Abu al-Hasan, the Mu'tazila, and many theologians forbid it. The proof of those who hold validity is that the reality of faith is assent (tasdiq), and it is found without being accompanied by any of the causes of disbelief. If it is said: Assent cannot be conceived without knowledge (al-ilm), for it is either essential to assent or a condition for it, as previously stated. And the imitator has no knowledge, because he has a firm and corresponding belief that is not based on a cause of necessity or inference. We say: What is considered in assent is certainty (al-yaqin), meaning firm and corresponding belief. Rather, sometimes mere correspondence suffices, and the dominant opinion with which no contrary comes to mind is treated as certainty. And it has been said: Assent may occur without knowledge and acquaintance, and vice versa. For we believe in the prophets and angels without knowing them individually, and we believe in all the states of the afterlife—the reckoning, the scale, the path, etc.—without knowing their modalities and attributes. And the People of the Book knew the Prophet, peace be upon him, as they knew their own sons, but they were not believers. And there is consideration in this, for what is meant is knowledge of that to which one gives assent. And we know of the prophets and angels what we assent to. Thus, the impossibility of assent without knowledge, in the sense of belief, is certain. The discussion is only about the converse.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the validity of the muqallid's faith and responds to an objection. The author begins by stating the majority position: the faith of one who follows authority (muqallid) is valid, with rulings applying both in this world and the hereafter. The exception is Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the Mu'tazila, and many theologians, who reject it. The supporters argue that faith is essentially assent (tasdiq), and the muqallid has that assent without any concomitant cause of disbelief. An objection is raised: Assent requires knowledge ('ilm), either as essential or as a condition. The muqallid lacks knowledge because his belief is firm and corresponding but not based on a necessary reason (e.g., sensation) or on inference. The response: What truly matters in assent is certainty (yaqin), which is firm and corresponding belief. In fact, sometimes mere correspondence suffices, and a dominant opinion with no contrary thought is treated like certainty. Moreover, assent can exist without detailed knowledge: we believe in prophets and angels without knowing them individually, and we believe in the details of the afterlife without knowing their modalities. Conversely, the People of the Book knew the Prophet's identity but did not believe, showing that knowledge without assent is possible. The response concludes that the impossibility of assent without knowledge (in the sense of belief) is certain; the real debate is about the opposite: whether knowledge can exist without assent.

تصديق
يقين
اعتقاد جازم
علم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that valid faith requires personal reasoning and knowledge of proofs.
Correction: This passage shows that faith is essentially assent (tasdiq) with certainty (yaqin), which the muqallid possesses even without independent reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the essential element of faith (iman) that the muqallid possesses?
A) Knowledge ('ilm) acquired through reasoning
B) Certainty (yaqin) in the form of firm assent (tasdiq)
C) The ability to refute doubts
D) Performing acts of worship

Key Takeaway
The faith of an imitator is valid because it includes assent (tasdiq) with certainty, even if not based on personal inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold your beliefs because you have personally verified each one, or because you trust someone else? Can you point to a single belief for which you have no personal proof but you are absolutely certain? What does that say about the validity of your faith?
?
If the imitator's faith is valid, does that free you from seeking deeper knowledge? Or rather, does it ground you to build upon certainty? Where in your life have you stopped at 'I just believe it because my parents did' when you could have grown?
?
The passage shows that the People of the Book knew the Prophet but did not assent. Is there any truth you know in your mind but have not fully accepted in your heart? What would it take to move from knowledge to genuine assent?

Distinction Between Iman al-Muqallid and Iman al-Ya'is
The author refutes the claim that the muqallid's faith is like the faith of desperation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between the faith of an imitator (muqallid) and the faith of desperation (iman al-ya'is), and why the latter is invalid while the former can be accepted.

Core Idea
Coerced faith to escape punishment is invalid; sincere faith, even if based on authority, is accepted.

This passage addresses an important objection: if the muqallid does not reason independently, is his faith like the faith of desperation, which is rejected? The author responds by clarifying that the cause of rejection for desperation is compulsion and loss of free will, not lack of independent reasoning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of faith in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. This gives you the overall argument.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
Notice that the opponent compares muqallid's faith to iman al-ya'is, claiming it does not benefit. They cite al-Maturidi's explanation about lack of inferential ability.
The objection is that faith without reasoning is useless.
3
Locate the author's response
Find where the author counters: first by limiting texts and consensus to iman al-ya'is only, then by refuting the analogy and pointing out the true cause.
The response is in two parts: textual and rational.
4
Understand the difference in cause ('illa)
The author says the cause in iman al-ya'is is compulsion to repel punishment, not lack of inference. This is a crucial distinction.
The cause is what invalidates the faith, not the lack of reasoning.
5
Reflect on sincerity and free will
Consider how the muqallid's faith is voluntary and seeks God's pleasure, unlike the coerced faith of desperation.
This shows the importance of intention and free will in religious belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: نحن لا ننفي كونه إيماناً وتصديقاً لكنا ندعي أنه لا ينفع، بمنزلة إيمان اليائس، فإن عدم نفعه على ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي معلل بأن العبد لا يقدر حينئذ أن يستدل بالشاهد على الغائب ليكون مقاله عن معرفة وعلم استدلالي فإن الثواب على الإيمان إنما هو بمقابلة ما يتحمله من المشقة، وهي في آداب الفكرة، وإدمان النظر في معجزات الأنبياء، أو في محدثات العالم. والتمييز بين الحجة والشبهة لا في تحصيل أصل الإيمان. قلنا: النص إنما قام على عدم نفع إيمان اليأس ومعاينة العذاب، دون إيمان المقلد. والإجماع أيضاً إنما انعقد عليه، والتمسك بالقياس، لو سلم صحته في الأصول، فلا نسلم أن العلة ما ذكرتم، بل ذهب الماتريدي وكثير من المحققين إلى أن إيمان اليأس إنما لم ينفع لأنه إيمان دفع عذاب، لا إيمان حقيقة، ولأنه لا يبقى للعبد حينئذ قدرة على التصرف من نفسه والاستمتاع بها، لأن عذاب الدنيا مقدمة لعذاب الآخرة، إذ ربما يموت العبد فيه. فينتقل إلى عذاب الآخرة، بخلاف إيمان المقلد، فإنه تقرب إلى الله تعالى وابتغاء لمرضاته من غير إلجاء ولا قصد دفع العذاب ولا انتفاء قدرة على التصرف في النفس.
If it is said: We do not deny that it is faith and assent, but we claim that it does not benefit, like the faith of the desperate person (iman al-ya'is). For its lack of benefit, according to what Sheikh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned, is caused by the fact that the servant then cannot infer from the visible to the invisible so that his statement is based on knowledge and inferential science. For the reward for faith is in exchange for the hardship one bears, which is in the etiquettes of reflection, persistent examination of the miracles of the prophets or of the originated things of the world, and the distinction between proof and doubt—not in acquiring the foundation of faith itself. We say: The text only indicates the lack of benefit of the faith of desperation at the sight of punishment, not the faith of the imitator. And consensus is also only established on that. And if one adheres to analogy, even if we accept its validity in fundamental principles, we do not accept that the cause is what you mentioned. Rather, al-Maturidi and many investigators held that the faith of desperation does not benefit because it is a faith to repel punishment, not true faith, and because the servant then has no ability to dispose of himself and enjoy himself, since the punishment of this world is a prelude to the punishment of the afterlife, as perhaps the servant dies in it and then moves to the punishment of the afterlife. Unlike the faith of the imitator, for it is an approach to Allah and a seeking of His pleasure without compulsion, without intending to repel punishment, and without the inability to dispose of oneself.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The opponent argues that the muqallid's faith does not benefit, comparing it to the faith of the desperate person (iman al-ya'is) who believes only when seeing punishment. They cite al-Maturidi's explanation that such faith lacks benefit because the person cannot use the evidence of the visible to infer the invisible, so his statement is not based on knowledge and inferential science. Reward is given for the hardship of reflection and examining proofs, not for merely acquiring the foundation of faith. The author responds: The scriptural texts and consensus only deny benefit to the faith of desperation when the punishment is actually seen, not to the muqallid's faith. Even if analogy were valid, the cause ('illa) in the case of desperation is not the lack of inference but rather that it is a coerced faith to ward off punishment, not true faith. Moreover, the desperate person loses the capacity to dispose of himself freely because the worldly punishment is a prelude to eternal punishment, and he might die in that state. In contrast, the muqallid's faith is a voluntary approach to God and seeking His pleasure, without compulsion or intention to avoid punishment, and he retains full capacity over himself.

Key Terms
إيمان اليائس
دفع عذاب
قدرة التصرف
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith without independent reasoning is like the faith of desperation and thus invalid.
Correction: The faith of desperation is invalid due to coercion, not lack of reasoning. Sincere faith based on authority can be valid.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the faith of desperation (iman al-ya'is) not benefit?
A) Because it lacks knowledge of proofs
B) Because it is a faith to repel punishment, not true faith
C) Because it is not accompanied by good deeds
D) Because it occurs after death

Key Takeaway
The faith of desperation is rejected because it is coerced, not because it lacks reasoning; the muqallid's sincere faith is accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is your true motivation when you perform acts of worship—is it to seek Allah's pleasure or merely to avoid His displeasure? How would you know the difference?
?
If you were suddenly faced with evidence that your faith was based solely on what you were taught without personal conviction, would it crumble? What would you do to ensure your faith is sincere?
?
Reflect on a time you acted religiously out of fear of punishment. Did that action bring you closer to Allah or just relieve anxiety? How can you transform your worship from daf' al-'adhab to sincere devotion?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

The Validity of Blind Faith
The author refutes the objection that iman al-muqallid is useless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the faith of the imitator (iman al-muqallid) is considered beneficial according to the author, in contrast to the faith of despair (iman al-ya'is).

Core Idea
Iman al-muqallid is beneficial because it is a sincere act of worship, not just an escape from punishment.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that blind faith is useless, akin to the faith of someone who only believes when faced with certain punishment. The objector argues that reward requires intellectual effort, so mere imitation cannot suffice. The author refutes this by clarifying the nature of true faith and the specific reason why the faith of despair fails to benefit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. Notice the reasoning: reward requires hardship in thought and inference, which the imitator lacks. The objector compares iman al-muqallid to iman al-ya'is, citing al-Maturidi.
The objection is that iman al-muqallid is not beneficial because it lacks the hardship of inferential reasoning.
2
Identify the author's refutation
The author distinguishes the two cases based on motive and consequence. He argues that iman al-ya'is is only to escape punishment and removes one's ability to act freely, while iman al-muqallid is a voluntary seeking of God's pleasure.
Key distinction: intention and presence of free will.
3
Reflect on the implications
Understand why this distinction matters for the validity of iman al-muqallid. The author concludes that the imitator's faith is genuine and beneficial, as it involves drawing near to God without compulsion.
This affirms the value of faith based on trust in scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن لا ننفي كونه إيماناً وتصديقاً لكنا ندعي أنه لا ينفع ، بمنزلة إيمان اليائس، فإن عدم نفعه على ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي معلل بأن العبد لا يقدر حينئذ أن يستدل بالشاهد على الغائب ليكون مقاله عن معرفة وعلم استدلالي فإن الثواب على الإيمان إنما هو بمقابلة ما يتحمله من المشقة، وهي في آداب الفكرة، وإدمان النظر في معجزات الأنبياء، أو في محدثات العالم . والتمييز بين الحجة والشبهة لا في تحصيل أصل الإيمان . قلنا : النص إنما قام على عدم نفع إيمان اليأس ومعاينة العذاب، دون إيمان المقلد . والإجماع ايضاً إنما انعقد عليه، والتمسك بالقياس، لو سلم صحته في الأصول، فلا نسلم أن العلة ما ذكرتم ، بل ذهب الماتريدي وكثير من المحققين إلى أن إيمان اليأس إنما لم ينفع لأنه إيمان دفع عذاب، لا إيمان حقيقة، ولأنه لا يبقى للعبد حينئذ قدرة على التصرف من نفسه والاستمتاع بها، لأن عذاب الدنيا مقدمة لعذاب الآخرة، إذ ربما يموت العبد فيه. فينتقل إلى عذاب الآخرة، بخلاف إيمان المقلد ، فإنه تقرب إلى الله تعالى وابتغاء لمرضاته من غير إلجاء ولا قصد دفع العذاب ولا انتفاء قدرة على التصرف في النفس.
If it is said: We do not deny that it is faith and belief, but we claim that it is not beneficial, like the faith of the despairing. For its lack of benefit, according to what Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned, is explained by the fact that the servant then cannot infer from the witnessed to the unseen, so that his statement is based on knowledge and inferential cognition. Reward for faith is only in return for the hardship one bears in the disciplines of thought, persistent examination of the miracles of the prophets or the originated things of the world, and distinguishing between evidence and doubt, not in attaining the foundation of faith. We say: The text establishes only the non-benefit of the faith of despair and witnessing punishment, not the faith of the imitator. Consensus also was concluded on that, and clinging to analogy, if its validity in principles is granted, we do not concede that the cause is what you mentioned. Rather, al-Maturidi and many of the verifiers held that the faith of despair did not benefit because it is faith to ward off punishment, not faith in reality, and because the servant then has no power to act from himself or enjoy it, since worldly punishment is a prelude to punishment in the Hereafter, for perhaps the servant dies in it and transitions to punishment in the Hereafter. This is unlike the faith of the imitator, for it is drawing near to God Almighty and seeking His pleasure without compulsion, intention to ward off punishment, or absence of power to act on the soul.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer regarding the validity of blind faith (īmān al-muqallid). The objector claims that such faith is useless, comparable to the faith of one in despair (īmān al-yā'is) at the time of death, which according to al-Maturidi is not beneficial because the person cannot use inferential reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen. Reward requires hardship in intellectual effort, not mere acceptance. The author answers by distinguishing the two cases: the text and consensus establish the non-benefit of the faith of despair and of seeing punishment, but not of the imitator's faith. The cause of non-benefit in the despairing is that it is faith to escape punishment, not genuine faith, and the person loses the ability to act freely, making it a prelude to eternal punishment. In contrast, the imitator's faith is a voluntary act of seeking God's pleasure without compulsion or intention to avoid punishment, and therefore remains beneficial.

Key Terms
إيمان المقلد
إيمان اليأس
تقليد
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman al-muqallid is useless like iman al-ya'is because both lack inferential reasoning.
Correction: The author shows they differ in motive: iman al-ya'is is to avoid punishment and removes agency, while iman al-muqallid is a sincere act of worship.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is iman al-ya'is not beneficial while iman al-muqallid is?
A) Because iman al-ya'is is forced while iman al-muqallid is voluntary.
B) Because iman al-ya'is lacks inferential reasoning while iman al-muqallid has it.
C) Because iman al-ya'is is only to escape punishment and removes free agency, while iman al-muqallid is a sincere act of worship.
D) Because iman al-ya'is occurs after death while iman al-muqallid occurs during life.

Key Takeaway
Iman al-muqallid is beneficial because it is a voluntary act of seeking God's pleasure, unlike iman al-ya'is, which is merely a last resort to escape punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself going through the motions of faith—praying, fasting—out of habit or fear of consequence rather than a genuine desire to draw closer to Allah? How does this passage expose the difference between that and the faith of the imitator that the author defends?
?
Think of someone you may have dismissed as having 'blind faith' without evidence. Does this lesson change how you view their sincerity? What would it take for you to see their faith as potentially more valuable than your own reliance on reasoning?
?
If your faith were tested by the approach of certain punishment, would your belief remain the same, or would it shift to a desperate attempt to save yourself? How can you cultivate now a faith that is purely for Allah, not merely a shield against Hell?

The Main Positions on Rational Proof for Faith
Classification of early positions on rational proof for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early positions on whether rational proof is necessary for true faith, including the view of the shaykh, the Mu'tazila, and the later scholars' reframing of the debate.

Core Idea
The necessity of rational proof for faith was debated, not a settled point.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a classification of views on whether blind faith (iman al-muqallid) is valid or requires rational demonstration. It first discusses a position that does not require the ability to produce proof but still requires evidence-based belief, then contrasts the Mu'tazila's stricter requirement. We will then see how later scholars reframed the disagreement as one concerning punishment in the afterlife rather than worldly rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get an overview of the different views presented.
Pay attention to the transitional phrase 'والمتأخرون'.
2
Identify the three main positions
Identify the view attributed to 'al-shaykh', the Mu'tazila view, and the later scholars' (al-muta'akhkhirun) reframing.
Notice that the shaykh's view is nuanced: he does not require ability to produce proof but still requires belief based on evidence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the later scholars shifted the discussion from worldly rulings to punishment in the afterlife. What does that imply about the nature of the disagreement?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما المانعون فالشيخ لا يشترط التمكن من إقامة الحجة ودفع الشبهة في كل مسألة من الأصول ، بل انتفاء الاعتقاد فيها على دليل حتى لو انتفى لم يكن مؤمناً. وحمله على نفي كمال الإيمان لإخلاله بالواجب مما لا يتصور فيه نزاع والمعتزلة يشترطون حتى لو انتفى انتفى الإيمان، وهو ظاهر البطلان إلا إذا أريد الوجوب على الكفاية، فيصير مسألة صاحب الكبيرة، وعن بعضهم أن وجوب النظر إنما هو في حق البعض. وأما العاجز كالعوام، وبعض العبيد والنسوان فلا يكلف إلا بتقليد المحق والظن الصائب، وقيل : كلفوا سماع أوائل الدلائل التي تتسارع إلى الأفهام، فإن فهموا فهم أصحاب الجمل وإلا فليسوا مكلفين والمتأخرون على أن ليس الخلاف لأنه إجراء أحكام الإسلام بل في آية هل يعاقب عقوبة الكافر؟ فقيل: نعم، جاهل بالله ورسوله وقيل : لا ، بل ينتقض عقابه بما له من التصديق، ثم الخلاف فيمن نشأ في شاهق الجبل ولم يتفكر ، فأخبر بما يجب عليه اعتقاده فصدق. وأما نشأ في دار الإسلام ولو في الصحاري، وتواتر عنده حال من النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) فمن أهل النظر . يعني القائلين بأن إيمان المقلد ليس بصحيح ، أو ليس بنافع، فمنهم من قال : لا يشترط ابتناء الاعتقاد على استدلال عقلي في كل مسألة، بل يكفي ابتناؤه على قول من عرف رسالته بالمعجزة مشاهدة أو تواتراً، أو على الإجماع فيقبل قول النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم ) بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته . ومنهم من قال لا بد من ابتناء الاعتقاد في كل مسألة من الأصول على دليل عقلي، لكن لا يشترط الاقتدار على التعبير عنه، وعلى مجادلة الخصوم، ودفع الشبهة وهذا هو المشهور عن الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري حتى حكي عنه أنه من لم يكن كذلك، لم يكن مؤمناً ، لكن ذكر عبد القاهر البغدادي أن هذا، وإن لم
He said: As for the preventers, the shaykh does not require the ability to establish proof and repel doubt in every matter of principles, but rather the absence of belief based on evidence such that if it is absent he is not a believer. Interpreting this as negating perfection of faith due to neglecting an obligation is something about which there is no dispute. The Mu'tazila require it such that if it is absent, faith is absent, which is clearly false unless obligation is intended as communal obligation (fard kifaya), then it becomes the issue of the one who commits major sins. And from some [there is a view] that the obligation of reflection is only upon some people. As for the incapable, like the common people, some slaves and women, they are not obligated except to imitate the truthful and correct opinion. And it was said: They are obligated to hear the beginnings of proofs that quickly reach minds; if they understand, they are like those who grasp premises, otherwise they are not obligated. The later scholars hold that the disagreement is not about applying Islamic rulings but rather whether he is punished with the punishment of an unbeliever? It was said: Yes, ignorant of God and His Messenger. And it was said: No, rather his punishment is annulled by his belief. Then the disagreement concerns one who grew up on a mountain peak and did not reflect, then was informed of what he must believe and believed. As for one who grew up in the abode of Islam, even in deserts, and the situation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) was conveyed to him recurrently, he is among the people of reflection. That is, those who say that the faith of the imitator is not valid or not beneficial. Among them are those who say: The foundation of belief need not be based on rational inference in every matter, but rather it suffices to be based on the word of one whose prophethood is known by miracle through direct observation or recurrent transmission, or on consensus, so he accepts the Prophet's statement about the world's contingency, the existence of the Maker, and His oneness. And among them are those who say: Belief must be based on rational proof in every matter of principles, but ability to express it, argue with opponents, and repel doubts is not required. This is the well-known view from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, such that it was narrated from him that whoever is not .
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage classifies the views of those who reject or restrict blind faith (المانعون). It begins by noting a position that does not require the ability to produce proof in every issue, but requires that belief be based on evidence—otherwise one is not a believer. Interpreting this as negating perfection of faith is uncontroversial. The Mu'tazila require proof for the existence of faith itself, which the author considers clearly false unless interpreted as communal obligation. Some restrict the obligation of reflection to certain people; the incapable (commoners, slaves, women) need only imitate the truthful. Others say they must hear initial proofs that are easy to grasp. The later scholars (المتأخرون) hold that the disagreement is not about applying Islamic rulings but about whether the ignorant person is punished as an unbeliever. One view punishes, another cancels punishment due to belief. Then the text discusses cases: one who grew up isolated and then believed upon hearing, and one who grew up in the abode of Islam with recurrent knowledge of the Prophet—the latter is obligated to reflect. Finally, it mentions two positions among those who reject the validity/benefit of imān al-muqallid: (1) belief need not be based on rational inference in every matter; acceptance of miraculous proof or consensus suffices; (2) belief must be based on rational proof in all principles, but expressive ability is not required—this is attributed to al-Ash'ari. The passage ends abruptly with a reference to Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi.

Key Terms
مانعون
معتزلة
متأخرون
إيمان المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila require rational proof for every individual believer as a personal obligation.
Correction: The passage notes that if interpreted as communal obligation (fard kifaya), it is not clearly false and becomes an issue about major sins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) say the disagreement is about?
A) The application of Islamic rulings
B) Whether the person is punished as an unbeliever
C) The validity of taqlid
D) The definition of Iman

Key Takeaway
Early scholars disagreed on whether rational proof is required for faith, with views ranging from not requiring ability to produce proof to requiring proof as a communal obligation, later reframed as a question of afterlife punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that some scholars excused the incapable from rational proof, accepting imitation. Is your own faith built on second-hand imitation or on personal conviction? What would you say if you were asked to give a rational justification for your belief in God right now?
?
The later scholars framed the disagreement as about afterlife punishment rather than worldly rulings. Do you ever judge someone's faith based on their inability to articulate proofs? How would you feel if Allah judged you by that same standard?
?
The Mu'tazila considered absence of rational proof as absence of faith. Have you ever doubted someone's sincerity because they could not debate theology? How does this passage challenge you to rethink your assumptions about others' faith?

Faith and Disbelief

Special Cases: The Incapable and the Isolated
Application of the rational proof debate to incapable and isolated individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over rational proof applies to special cases such as the incapable (commoners, slaves, women) and those who grew up in isolation.

Core Idea
The obligation of rational proof may be lifted for the incapable and those without access.

This passage continues the classification by addressing specific cases. It discusses the view that the obligation of reflection (nazar) is only for some, while the incapable need only imitate. Then it considers the person who grew up isolated on a mountain and the one who grew up in the abode of Islam with recurrent knowledge of the Prophet. These cases help clarify when faith is acceptable without personal rational demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the incapable
Read the segment about العاجز (the incapable) and note the different opinions on what they are required to do.
The passage mentions taqlid, hearing proofs, etc.
2
Examine the case of the isolated person
Read about the person who grew up on a mountain peak and did not reflect. Note the two views on whether such a person is punished.
3
Contrast with the one in Dar al-Islam
The passage then says that the one who grew up in the abode of Islam with recurrent knowledge of the Prophet is among the people of reflection (نظر). Why?
This shows that the obligation of reflection depends on circumstances.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everyone is equally obligated to produce rational proof for their faith.
Correction: The passage shows that the incapable and isolated may have different obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a person who grew up in the abode of Islam with recurrent knowledge of the Prophet?
A) He is not obligated to reflect
B) He is among the people of reflection
C) He is considered an unbeliever
D) He must imitate others

Key Takeaway
The obligation to reflect on rational proofs depends on one's circumstances; those in the abode of Islam with knowledge of the Prophet are required to reflect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have grown up in the abode of Islam, with access to the Prophet's teachings repeatedly. Are you truly among the people of reflection, or have you been coasting on inherited belief? When was the last time you sincerely examined the rational proofs for your faith as a matter of personal accountability?
?
If a stranger asked you tomorrow why you believe in God and His Messenger, would you be able to present a coherent rational argument, or would you rely on 'I was raised this way'? What does your answer reveal about whether you have fulfilled the obligation of nazar?
?
The person on the mountain who never reflected is a matter of scholarly disagreement. But you are not that person. You have knowledge and capacity. Knowing that, what specific step will you take this week to move from blind acceptance to genuine reflection on the proofs of your faith?

Faith and Disbelief

The Two Views on the Foundation of Belief
Two views on the foundation of belief among those who reject blind faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among those who reject the validity of blind faith: one that allows reliance on miraculous proof or consensus, and another that requires rational proof in every principle.

Core Idea
Even among those who reject blind faith, there was disagreement on what counts as sufficient proof.

This final part of the passage focuses on those who hold that the faith of an imitator (al-muqallid) is not valid or not beneficial. Two views emerge: one that does not require rational inference in every issue, but deems sufficient the acceptance of the Prophet's statements based on miraculous proof or consensus; and another that requires rational proof in every principle, though not the ability to express it. The latter is famously attributed to al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage begins with 'يعني القائلين بأن إيمان المقلد ليس بصحيح، أو ليس بنافع' – these are the two groups. Note that they both agree on the invalidity of iman al-muqallid, but differ on the remedy.
2
Examine the first view
The first group says that belief does not need to be based on rational inference in every matter; it is sufficient to base it on the word of a prophet known through miracle or consensus.
They accept the Prophet's statements about the contingency of the world, existence of the Maker, etc.
3
Examine the second view
The second group insists on rational proof in every principle, but does not require the ability to express or defend it. This is the well-known Ash'ari position.
Note the passage ends mid-sentence referencing al-Baghdadi.
